The Red Page
Hell
It is well to remember that though war is
hell it is only one kind of hell, and that
there are other hells even more hellish. Thus,
the peace that would follow our backing-
down in the matter of a White Australia
would be too shameful to contemplate. And
the hell of the non-combatant, in an invaded
country, is much hotter than the hell of the
soldier. The trouble with us is that we have
not experienced even the lesser hell of war,
else we would want to prevent that hell.
And there is only one way to prevent war,
and that is to stop it beginning, by force.
Few historians have described war. What
an historian is concerned with is the moves
in a game in which armies are the pawns
and the general staffs the opposing chess-
players. But war isn’t a game: it is a
particular hell that will afflict a particular
soldier or a particular non-combatant. In
brief, it is the effect upon you that is the
important thing about war. If you knew
what happened to some individual during a
recent war, or even were told what might
happen to you in the event of a war in l the
immediate future, you might come to dislike
war and decide that your country was worth
saving from that horror.
Of these four books upon war, two term
war a game, and two show that it is hell.
The Campaign of lAao-Yang, being a treatise
upon the strategy of a recent war, rightly
leaves out the human element. The author
is dealing with a subject that should inter-
est all Australians. For this campaign has
striking analogies to the campaign that will
have <to be fo.uglit by Australia before she
makes good her claim to this continent. This
campaign illustrates warfare “under modern
conditions when it is being conducted in a
theatre of operations lacking in communica-
tions and offering considerable difficulties to
the movement and the feeding of great
masses of men.” And this brilliant analysis
of a war known chiefly to us through the
sensational accounts of special war-corre-
spondents, with its cold correction of mis-
statements and its mathematical demonstra-
tion of the real factors of success and fail-
ure, is as interesting as a fine novel. It is
the story of an antagonist who had made
adeqdate preparations against an unready
foe; af government interference with the
commanding officer; of reckless bravery in
offence and of stubborn bravery in defence;
of the lack of morale that destroys an army
which, for valid strategical reasons, is com-
pelled doggedly to retreat, and of the para-
mount importance of big battalions. The
author exposes the hesitations and the mis-
takes of the Japanese leadership, whose
success was largely due to the worse organi-
sation of the Russians. Kouropatkin’s fatal
decision to retreat from Liao-Yang was due
to three messages that he received after so
long holding his own against the Japanese;
one, of these messages, to the effect that
ammunition was running short, was not
correct.
With the knowledge at our disposal and
sitting far from, the toil and turmoil of battle,
it is clear that the Russian commander
should have made another bid for victory,
and that the result would then have depended
cn endurance and morale. The Russian
morale had no doubt suffered severely, but
the gallant fighting in this prolonged battle
by the Ist and 3rd Siberians, which, since
the commencement of the campaign, had en-
dured defeat after defeat, without once feel-
ing thd glamor of victory, showed how quickly
daring leadership or slight success might re-
store it. Moreover,-impartial observers have
stated that, amongst gallant troops,
the breaking: strain had been very nearly
reached, and, if the steel were snapped, it
was a commonly expressed belief that the
high temper of the metal would militate
against any repair or welding process.
One lesson drawn by the author from this
titanic struggle is that-
Whatever may be the brain-power, the in-
tellect, the skill in the leadership of an army,
and whatever may be the expenditure of
money, the subtletv of strategy, the result
of a campaign will depend largely on the
courage of the man. For there will always
be times and places where heroism on the
one side and lack of it on the other may
break the back of every military calcula-
tion.
But the chief cause of the temporary de-
feat of the Russians —for, had the war gone
on, Japan would have been beaten back by
sheer weight of numbers —was the unfortu-
nate influence exercised on the Russian
armies by the system of dual control.
Had Kouropatkin been given a free hand,
he would have retreated right back to Har-
bin, and, taking the offensive later with
sunerior numbers, might have achieved the
reputation, not indeed of a Napoleon, but
possibly of an Archduke Charles.
In the Cockpit of Europe attempts to
treat, in the guise of fiction, a war of the
immediate future. But the author cannot
write fiction ; and his account of the expedi-
tionary British army that was so brilliantly
victorious with the French against the Ger-
mans is merely a dull narrative of alleged
strategy and the bulldog breed. And he
delightfully places his war in the other fel-
low’s backyard. .Thus the non-combatant’s
woes do not trouble him. As our war will
certainly be fought in our own back-yard,
Alsager Pollock does not help us to envisage
this particular sort of hell. And he calmly
states in his preface that his assumption of
a British victory against German troops is
quite unlikely. “However, I required Brit-
ish victories, and I have therefore caused
the enemy to pave the way towards them...
Similarly, I fear I have unduly flattered the
British Nation, Government and War office.”
lie has. He admits that “war is a very
terrible thing,” but adds that this war did
England much good.
It brought together classes that, for base
political reasons, much had been dyne to
divide into opposite camps. ... When great
ladies sat working and talking for hours
with factory girls and the like, speaking of
matters equally near to the hearts of them
all, the good done was immense. Not only
did rich and poor meet on common ground,
but; the wives and daughters of plutocrats,
very worthy good women in all else but
their snobbery, found to their bewilderment
that gentlewomen seemed quite at home in
the company of tradespeople and the working
classes.
Even hell has its good side!
So by gallant officers, leading companies
of soldiers speaking one of the many un-
intelligible dialects of that polyglot island
in the North Sea, Germany is overwhelmed.
It is true that Australia and Maoriland and
France and Russia helped. Then the
.hero returns to England, covered with
medals and glory, and we hear all the
speeches at his home-coming, and even
accompany him to Windsor to chat with
the King,
Piffle.
Jt
The Iron Year has been immensely popu-
lar in Germany. It is a brilliant account of
the Franco-Prussian trouble, written from
the view-point of the soldier. Here we do see
that war is hell. The detail is grimly real-
istic, though through the narrative is
threaded a strand of romance to give a
little color to the drab material. One
picture must suifice: the story of the first
man killed.
“Fifteen pounds for the man who shoots
the first Frenchman, and I’m going to try
for the chink.”. .. .“Damn it! Somebody
else has shot the brute!”
He was a sturdy young fellow with a
chubby good-humored face beneath the tan,
he lay on his back, his left hand pressed to
his chest. Rosenberg raised it: a grey hole
was torn into the bright blue of his tunic
and a few thick, heavy drops oozed slowly
fi*om it.
“By Jove, Kraus!” Rosenberg raised his
hand in salute. “Bull’s-eye !”
There was an odd, stupefied expression in
Kraus’ eyes. His ling trembled.
“Fifteen quid and a medal,” said Rosen-
berg. “Why don’t you laugh, rnateV”
Kraus burst into sudden sobs. “O
mother!” he gasped, “O God, mother!”
_ Behind the thin network of patrols and
pickets facing each other there were millions
preparing for the fray. It seemed to him
as though one long, endless shriek of terror
rent the air, a fearful, magnified echo of the
uncontrollable sobs with which the first lucky
marksman had celebrated his victory. Ay,
they would all weep; victors and vanquished,
dead and living. Mourning and lamenting
without end would arise on earth, and njl
the enthusiastic raptures of victories gained,
triumphs earned and armies shattered would
not be able to deaden that fearful chorus of
weeping and wailing
And between them a pale blue and scarlet
spot lav stretched in the sand, a solitary
corpse surrounded by flies, which settled down
on the warm dark drons oozing out of the
tiny hole in the tunic, and drank their fill.
That is the soldier’s hell. Now we come
to the hell of the non-combatant. In War,
W. Douglas Newton shows us, in frenzied
journalese-and-Kipling, what happens to
non-combatants, Quakers, Peace Societies,
and such, when a country, island or conti-
nent, is invaded. “This book,” the writer
of the preface explains, “will be called sen-
sational and disgusting. That is precisely
what it is, because it is an account of the
sensational and disgusting thing called
War.”
It does not pretend to give statistics of the
general ruin and devastation which must
fall upon a country invaded by another of
the same degree of civilisation as his own ;
or of the death roll; or of the years of misery
and poverty that must follow: it does not de-
scribe the purely internal horrors that would
certainly take place in our larger towns \ i t
merely pictures the kind of thing which we
should all witness, so long as we survived our-
selves, in the familiar lanes and fields and
villages of our countryside.
War is a trade, something like the
slaughtering of cattle at an abattoirs. It
is unfortunate for the cattle, because they
are non-combatants. Newton pictures the
invasion of England by the Germans, of a
standard of civilisation equal to that of
their foes. From this lurid picture we can
glean some of the horrors of an invasion of
Australia by the Oriental. But not all.
There is outstanding that question of a
standard of civilisation.
Brun was an English non-combatant; and
his is a story of the non-combatants who,
unfortunately, got in the way. There was
one parcel of women and children who got
mixed up in the fighting... .They happened
to inhabit a village that stood between the
two combatants. There were the doctors
who did their best with the wounded. And
in the battles men died —variously.
Men came to fall continuously with the
strange sudden manner of the abruptly dead.
They fell down like empty sacks. They fell
down still full of jerky life. They lay in
odd and impossible attitudes as though care-
fully posed there by an eccentric artist.
Some fell clutching with clawliko, bloody
fingers at mythical aerial supports.
Others went up and came down —crash.
Sometimes men lay as though sleep had
come to them very softly. Sometimes they
writhed with all the kicking fervor of smit-
ten horses. Some fell flopping, and after
a second began to crawl. The fields were
full of crawling live tilings and silent dead
things, sprawled and impossibly angular
under the sun. There is no, mistaking the
utter negation of death.
And Brun’s girl—a non-combatant, too —
moans, “It ought to be stopped.”
“You can’t stop it now,” Brun points out.
“The way to stop it is to stop it before it
happens. But nobody thought about this
side before it happened. It was all armies
marching to victory. All soldiers doing
brave deeds. Nobody thought of the people
who weren’t soldiers getting mixed up with
the fighting. Nobody thought of what would
happen to non-combatants who got in the
way of armies. And nobody told u's.”
“Why?” demanded Agnes. “If we’d only
known we’d have moved heaven and earth
to prevent horror, happening to
anybody. Why?”
Brun turned to her with a bitter smile.
“They were afraid you’d be shocked, my
dear.”
Through all the horrors of the casual
slaying of people who would get in the way
of bullets, through the terrors of a siege of
a country town, Brun goes till he seeks out
a forgotten village as a refuge for his girl
and himself. Then, without warning, he
finds himself mixed up in guerilla warfare.
Here and there the beaten English, many
of them civilians, were keeping up a hopeless:
sort of fight. Guerilla warfare was inevit-
able* “It was in history from Greece to
the Transvaal. France had done this;
Spain, America, Cuba. It was a universal
habit.” And Australia will do it, too.
Then, attempting to escape from this dan-
gerous zone, Brun and Agnes fall into the
hands of drunken German soldiers. Brun’s
fate is to be knocked senseless. Agnes’s
fate is worse. When Brun recovers he goes
mad. He kills two German soldiers—and
he isn’t a soldier; he has no lic'ense to
kill. The General points this out to him
when he is captured.
Brun looked at the General. lie was, for
the first time in his life, facing things
squarely. He was facing war. This re-
lentless thing he hadn’t troubled about, this
thing he had permitted to descend upon him
without a thought was going to kill him. It
had killed, or worse than killed, all that he
loved most in the world. Now it was going
to kill him. In a clear light he saw that
it was his own fault. He had ignored it all
along, he had refused to recognise the fact
that it could touch him. He might have
faced it in its beginnings and prevented
it. ...He saw all the men in this stricken
countryside who might have banded together,
to prevent what had happened, and so lived
.... He would die, not because he had killed
two troopers, but because he had permitted
a state of things in which he had been doomed
to kill two troopers....
As the firing party stepped briskly away
the General Officer lay back in his chair.
His eyes were tired and sad. He was won-
dering why people refused to know these
things, refused to see.
Copies of this book should be sent to all
Geese Societies, all Quakers, and the Minis-
ter for Defence who is diffident about equip-
ping our army with up-to-date air-craft.
They would all like to be non-combatants.
Kathleen Watson’s pale prose for pale
people, Litanies of Life and Later Litanies,
now appear in half-crown editions, pub-
lished by Heinemann.
Every picture tells a stc-ry in Arthur
Wright’s latest “Bookstall” shilling’s worth,
In the Last Stride. The cover shows a
knock-out at the Stadium, the frontispiece
shows a fight on the football field, another
illustration shows an opium-smuggling inci-
dent, and in the rest there are fights, the
heroine struggling with the villain, horses
and revolvers, more Chinamen, and the de-
tective reading “the papers.” With these
ingredients is it any wonder that, according
to the publishers, the sales of Arthur
Wright’s four earlier “Bookstall” thrillers
have reached nearly 60,000 volumes? Who.
says that the higher, or jockey-and-biff, lit-
erature doesn’t pay in Australia?
A rattling good yarn, in which the author
has made shrewd use of our early history,
is Don Delaney’s A Rebel of the Bush
(N.S.W. Bookstall Cos., Sydney, Is.). In
the colony ruled by Governor Macquarie
Don Delaney has set an exciting story of
love and adventure, culminating in the
Bathurst insurrection.
E. Bruce Mitford, in Japan's Inheritance
(Uowin, per Sydney Book Club), points out
that the late Emperor’s Rescript—a sort of
modern decalogue for Young Japan—has two
notable omissions: two commandments are
missing: “Thou slialt not steal” and “Thou
shalt not commit adultery.” He also men-
tions that when a train in which he was
travelling in Japan was stopped by an “acci-
dent,” he, in common with all the passengers
got down in time to see a suicide being
scraped off the wheels; but the attitude of
the Japs was, to him, more horrible than the
other mess. “Neither horror, nor disgust,
nor pity could be traced upon a single face.
If the general expression might be summed
up in a phrase, it was one of amused in-
terest. ‘Only another case of shinju [sui-
cide] !’ laughed the beholders.” We learn,
too, that the Japs make a point of winning
over to their side the most important for-
eign correspondents and exponents of
opinion. “One of their greatest hauls was
the late correspondent of the London Times ;
another was the representative of the Ameri-
can Associated Press. .. .Newspapers pledged
"to uphold Jap interests have been established
in Seoul, Peking. New York and San Fran-
cisco. .. .The Jap has the advantage of all
other Orientals in courage and versatility,
but there his superiority ends. He lacks the
more solid qualities of the Chinaman.” The
book is just readable.
It appears, from Famous Fights at the
Stadium, by C. A. Jeffries (The Platypus
Press, Sydney), that there is a place of
that name situated at Rushcutter’s Bay, and
that there various persons have acquired the
habit of hitting each other violently on the
jaw or thereabouts, and that a number of
citizens have been, accustomed to gather
together to view the proceedings. 0.
A. Jeffries, who is apparently a bloke
who has attended both afternoon and
evening services since the consecration
of the building, narrates, in picturesque
Jeffrese, the strange and abrupt hap
penings in that locality; and has fin-
nished photographs of the chief participants
in the succession of frays, posed in attitudes
of prayer. If there is anybody on this con-
tinent who is interested in these doings, he
will find the whole drama of what is called
Stoush herein graphically and authorita-
tively unrolled. It is an Epic of Biff. It
may be mentioned that 0. A. Jeffries is
rumored to be the individual who narrates
these prsh-happenings weekly in another
page of The Bulletin ; and that the book
is sold for one shilling. It is regrettable, to
have to add that the publication is now in
its second edition.

The time of all others when reli-
able cartridges are invaluable is
in big-game hunting. A miss-fire,
an inaccurate cartridge, or one
having poor penetration may
mean the loss of a coveted trophy
or even injury to the hunter.
Winchester the W brand—of
big-game cartridges, either smoke-
less or black powder, can always
be relied on to be sure fire, accur-
ate and to have the proper speed
and penetration. You can help
MAKE YOUR HUNT A
, SUCCESS BY USING %
THEM.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Local Spirit.
The Local Spirit never dies,
Though it is mostly rotten,
For Local Spite and local lies
Can never be forgotten ;
The local truth dies very young
When wed to local merit:
’Tis murdered by the envious tongue.
And —that’s the Local Spirit.
The General Good is sacrificed. .
In spite of all petitions
To paltry private interests
And local mean ambitions.
And when we lose ’neath fortune’s frown
The manhood we inherit,
The Local Spirit treads us down—
For that’s the Local Spirit.
But, though they may be very few,
And poor as autumn stubble,
The local friends are leal and true
Whenever one’s in trouble.
They make a man hold up his head
And face the world and dare it!
And that’s —when all is done and said —
The other Local Spirit.
rj.s.w.
HENRY LAWSON.
Seawater.
“Tell me, truly, seawater on your, lips, as the
fishermen say.”— Robert Hicliens.
A flower before your car and the red sun in
your eyes
By ihe grass-green tinkling wavelets and the
blue Dalian skies,
While over there amid the haze, the pearl of
Naples lies....
Tell me that you love me, caro mio, tell me
true,
With the water on your warm lips, ladled
from the Blue,
While my hot Southern glances drown there
in love of you !
With the great grave Saint behind you,' San
Francisco of the rocks,
With the sunlight on the villa and the liz-
ards on the blocks,
By a linger of the ocean that upon the ruin
knocks....
Pale as grape bloom on the hillside lies the
spinning of the mist,
As the wraith of last night’s moon hours
when two lovers met and kist.
While the little winds chid “Hasten!” and
the little waves cried “Hist!”
And the oars above the silent pool were
plashing in and out,
And far off along the mainland we could
hear the singers shout,
Till a chain of silver music linked the silver
isles about....
Now he leaves me to the sunshine. . . .and
the water is so clear
I can see the nodding shadow of the flower
at his ear
As lie sings “Addore” for the wide, blue
world to hear.
Before the feathers of the storm the world
is still and grey.
Ischia has an ebon wing spread quivering to
the bay,
And.... was the truth upon his tongue'be-
fore he rowed away?
The shrinking sun, a copper ball, is smoul-
dering through the cloud.
I think, although he spoke the word, yet
low he never bowed
To fill his palm and touch his lips with
water, as he vowed....
There’s a twisted street in Naples over there
across the bay.
And lie turned his face unto it and his boat
was set that way.
. . . .<S7ie is shaped like a Madonna. . . .but a
devil in her play !
* * *
The storm is on the waters and high the
grey witch rides,
She has called her demon lovers, and the
In.l low of the tides
Makes rings of foam for those who woo the
Witches for their brides. . . .
All the yellow light of morning has been
quenched in wrack and rain,
But a fisher boat is drifting to the- still
Saint's pool again,
And the billows that have brought it at the
gates of Death complain.
Does she laugh among her lovers with the
narrow panes shut tight.
Does she set a red lamp in the niche to
guide him through the night,
While / crouch down to read the truth on
dead lips....wet and white!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
My Request.
When I am living you may let me be
Or in your kind consideration urn me :
But when I’m dead make no delay, I beg
But promptly burn me.
Old Mother Earth wlio fed and nourished me
Through all the hours of life has done her
share;
Good taste forbids that I should cumber her
With the soiled garments I no longer wear.
Of course you cannot quite obliterate
My shell, and to its elements return it.
But you .can to a white and harmless ash
Considerately burn it.
The dust-to-dust command we think we keep
When six feet deep we tuck away our
dead.
But centuries may pass before the wol'd
“Disintegration” can be truly said.
Poor human frame! Unto what dread re-
morse
Does foul decay in horrid cycles turn it.
He is my best friend who will take my corse
And kindly burn it.
X.R.W.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.

Christmas Number, post paid.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings and Sixpence Sterling you van have

Cheques, drafts, etc., which should he crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (.and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking some
cure as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
11 is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy/’ Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, APRIL 30, 1014.
THREE MEN WHO MAKE BRICKS
WITHOUT STRAW.
In the wicked, muddled old days that decent
citizens hoped were dead, but which aie,
perhaps, less dead than they should be, every
branch of the public service in every State
was a hunting-ground for politicians. No
man got a public job in that stirring period
unless some politician recommended him.
Very few men retained their jobs if they
neglected to keep an eye on the local mem-
ber or some other member, for if the billet
was wanted to placate a more valuable sup-
porter the less valuable one who held it had
to sro. These were strenuous times and occa-
sions, when every man’s hand was against
every other man, and each person was a law
unto himself, and did what was bad in his
own sight. Sometimes there were as many
as three men holding one billet. The poli-
tician who secured most civil service situa-
tions for his crowd was re-elected, and the
one who secured fewest situations wasn’t. As
a further development of the position, the
Minister who found most jobs for the con-
stituents of the members who supported him
most strenuously hung on longest to his port-
folio. There were a few glorious years of
this sort of adventure, and then a deadly
weariness came over the politicians. When
at last a member had to shove over 1000 of
his supporters into the railway service to
make his seat reasonably secure, he longed
to be able to tell all applicants that he had
no patronage or influence whatsoever, and
couldn’t find employment for even a dog.
That was how the habit arose of putting
appointments aud promotions in the hands
of Commissioners, and regulating the busi-
ness by a process of examination. It may be
a dull, wooden, rule-of-thumb arrangement,
but then nothing is perfect, and even the
most ignorant folk aren’t infallible. Now
that some of the old methods are returning
in the N.S.W. railway service, which is the
biggest public service of the most populous
State, it is worth while to remember that
they are veiy old methods, and that, to
people who are old enough to remember them,
the odor of them is something terrible.
The devices that are returning are not as
yec exactly the old ones, but they are already
a very colorable imitation. Minister Hoyle,
tire busy meddler and muddler who had his
biggest financial Bill twice ruled out by
reason of incompetent draftsmanship, has
made it clear that -there is to be a great in-
crease of political interference with the rail-
ways. Some of his colleagues have spoken
to the same effect. Certainly there has never
been a complete lack of interference. The
State Legislature has always retained full
control of the railway policy. It has re-
tained the right to decide where new lines
are to be built, and how they are to be built;
to regulate fares, wages, working hours, and
many other details. But it has stopped
short of late at the actual sale of jobs
in return for aid. The appointment of
Bill to be junior assistant porter at Dan-
daloo, and the reprimanding or fining of Bill
when he sent some choleric bagman’s lug-
gage to Melbourne instead of Milparinka, and
the final removal or abolition of Bill when
his habit of shoving belated passengers into
the wrong train became chronic—these things
have been left to the Commissioners. The
old habit by which the politician tried to
catch a vote by taking up the grievance of
Bill on Bill’s ex parte statement that he
was a wronged and persecuted bloke has been
suspended for a number of years. As the
present N.S.W. Government has threatened
to abate the pomp and tyranny of the Kail-
way Commissioners, it is presumed that poli-
ticians .are to resume their active personal
interest in Bill. That is about the only way
in which the pomp and tyranny of the Com-
missioners can be abated.
As a first instalment of the new disarrange-
ment of things, Minister lloyle recently de-
livered an addiess to a number of railway
men at Bathuist, and made them a number
of promises which may help to secure poli-
tical support, but which don’t promise to
make the railways any safer to travel on.
In the first place, he said that the cruelties
perpetrated on the sluggish stock train —-the
vehicle in which sheep and cattle die of thirst
or hunger or both, while the delayed contrap-
tion is hung up at sidings or dawdling along
at an average rate of one mile per hour —
must cease. The chronic lateness of pas-
senger trains must cease. The chronic stop-
pages in the bottle-neck entrance to Sydney’s
one big station must cease. Almost everything
must cease, and if the present Commissioners
don't know how to ai range all this cessation
it will be a question of finding new Commis-
sioners. At the same time, lloyle promised
to abolish the “please explain” order. At
present, when something goes wrong on the
impossibly congested and tangled railways of
N.S.W., the wearied Commissioners send a
form to be filled up by the officer who was
nearest to the trouble —the man who was
driving the train when it fell one hour behind
its schedule, or the person who was in charge
of the signal when it didn’t work, or the in-
dividual who bossed the station where the
collision happened or the dog committed sui-
cide. The document is a nuisance, and is
widely disliked, but it represents the constant
effort of the men in charge to find out how
to do the impossible, and how to prevent
Hoyle’s relations and other travellers, like-
wise railway servants, cows and keas of beer,
being killed too often on one of the worst-
planned railway systems in the world. And
it is at least fairly polite, for most railway
systems don’t say “please.” What a
Private Enterprise system says is much
more like “damn.” Hoyle’s assurance
that railway employees won’t, in future,
be even asked for an explanation or a theory
or a fragment of information when things go
wrong, suggests a very anxious scuffle after
political support. And his broad hint that
unless everything goes right in future the
present Commissioners may lose their jobs im-
plies that no railway employees except the
Commissioners ever make mistakes, and that
the absolutely perfect Government of which
Minister lloyle is a member, never makes
mistakes either, and that it has supplied the
Commissioners with a railway system which
only requires decently sane management to be
a complete success. The attitude of Hoyle
doesn’t make for the discipline which pre-
vents one train climbing on top of another.
But as a compliment to a very big voting-
interest, and as a self-presented testimonial
or bouquet thrown at himself, there is a
great deal to be said for it. Also, it is a
strong appeal to the public. “These things.”
says Hoyle, “must cease,” and there is
nothing the Cornstalk public wants more
earnestly than that a long procession of
things should cease.
* * * *
Meanwhile, the case for the Commission-
ers—the men who have only three votes, and
perhaps den’t count—has some points of
interest. They have to manage the railways,
being appointed to that job by the abso-
lutely perfect Government of which lloyle
is a member, because the unblemished Gov-
ernment of which lloyle is a member didn't
know any better. N.S.W. possesses about
39?0 miles of railway open for business,
and. except for a pnrelv local coal
traffic which centres at Newcastle, almost
everything that these Hues carry parses
through or into or out of Sydney. The 3930
miles of railway form a big proposition.
Great Britain has a little over 20.000 miles
of- railway, but then its lines converge, for
purposes of foreign trade, on Loudon, Car-
diff, Liverpool, Birkenhead, the Tyne ports
(several of them), Southampton, Hull, the
Clyde ports (Glasgow, Port Glasgow and
Greenock), Newport, Aberdeen, Blyth,
Dover, Plymouth, Swansea, Sunderland,
Middlesbro’, Grimsby, Leith, Manchester,
Methil, Grangemouth, Goole, Hartlepool,
Burntisland, Harwich, Bristol, and a num-
ber besides. Twenty thousand miles of rail-
way carrying foreign trade to and from 40
or more ports are a very different proposi-
tion from nearly 4000 miles of railway shov-
ing trade on to one port. Again, less than
half the mileage in Great Britain is single
iiue, and the single lines are located in lone-
some regions far away from the harbors
where the foreign trade congregates. The
hauls are short. The country is so small,
and the ports where foreign commerce hangs
its banner on the outer wall are so many,
that it is only in rare cases that the railway
management at a shipping centre has to
steer any great quantity of goods over more
than 100 or 150 miles of country. And the
idea of crowding all the trade of a big city
tlnough one bottle-neck into one station
has never dawned on these unenterprising
British folk. Glasgow, which has very little
more population tnan Sydney, and rather
less shipping than Sydney, considers itself
badly served with four great railway sta-
tions similar to the Sydney Cential (save
that one of them is a sort of double decker,
having one class of traffic in the basement
and another upstairs) : with main lines run-
nine- right through the city; and with over-
head and underground lines for local t. attic.
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners are
called upon to make bricks without straw.
In no other country on earth is a railway
management expected to steer the commerce
of so large and prosperous an area of coun-
try, and to hump the burden carried by so
many miles of railway—mostly single-line
railway—through one bottle-neck, into the
one dead-end station of one congested city.
Every time a new loan is floated (new loans
are being floated at an unprecedented rate),
arid a new branch line is opened (new branch
lines are being opened very often), and the
traffic is hurled at Sydney (the traffic is
always hurled at Sydney by reason of the
great Congestion policy), and the bill for
the loan money with which the new branch
is built is left to Posterity (the bill was
never left more fervently and unanimously
to Posterity), things grow a little worse.
The traffic becomes greater. The mileage of
line that converges on the bottle-neck
becomes longer. The multitude of petty
branch lines which have to connect accur-
ately with the main lines, unless somebody
is to be killed, grows more numerous. The
bulk of the traffic may be nothing remark-
able, but the length and complication of if,
and the lade of facilities for dealing with it
at the solitary dead-end or bottle-neck sta-
tion,_ are serious matters. Now the Com-
missioners are up against a Government
which apparently regards the railway ser-
vice, not so much as a device for carrying
goods and passengers as a scheme for acquir-
ing votes. The attitude of Minister Hoyle
—-who was once a railway employee himself,
though not a very notable one—is that the
general staff is so perfect (it has a heap of
votes), and the Congestion is so completely
beyond reproach (the Congestion party in
Sydney commands very many votes), and
the method on which new railways are being-
laid out by the Holman-Hoyle Government
is so wise (the Holman-Hoyle Government
naturally believes in itself), that nothing
can be wrong save by the default and in-
capacity of the Commissioners (the Commis-
sioners have only three votes). So Hoyle
says firmly that “These things must cease.”
SANE PAROCHIALISM.
Tiie Sydney Evening News is pre-eminently
the organ of Sydney. It is less capable than
any other paper of realising that there is
any place in N.S.W. except Sydney, and that
is saying a good deal. So it was character-
istic of the News that it recently allowed
one Hakkness to outpour himself eloquently
in its columns about the proposed “mad, reck-
less linldng-up” of certain portions of liiv-
erina with Melbourne. The excited contri-
butor pointed out that the distance from
Finley to Sydney is 454 miles, while that
from Finley to Melbourne is only IGG miles.
Therefore if the very few miles of line (about
13) are built which will finish the connec-
tion with Melbourne, the Finley farmers and
the farmers of a great area thereabouts will
save nearly 300 miles of railway carriage
and GOO miles of sea carriage on their wheat
exports; and to these good, bucolic N.S.W.
citizens the saving may make all the differ-
ence between comfort and beggary. It may
make them much more numerous and pros-
perous taxpayers and much more plentiful
defenders. And all this the E. News con-
tributor regarded as three different kinds of
horrible. At present, mainly because
of the impossible distance from the
only port with which they are permitted
to trade, the people of the region that all the
row is about are something of a failure.
They are of little value to man or dog. and
are of little use to themselves or anybody
else. And they are no asset worth mention-
ing to Sydney. The N.S.W. county of Tara,
handy to Portland. Adelaide and Port Pirie,
and located beside the Murray, where
the Murray is generally wet, ought
to do some good for itself; but then it is so
far from Sydney that railway communica-
tion couldn’t possibly pay, and it isn’t allowed
communication with its natural outlets. So
it has a steadily declining population which
is now less than one person to 12 square
miles, and it supplies the State canital with
little save old-age pension claims. Wentworth,
the next county, situated where the Murrav
and Darling join, had, despite the splendid
chances for irrigation, fewer people in 1911
than in 1891. Its record in 1911 was one
person to three square miles. Practically
all the counties of Ttiverina. except those
that connect easily with the adjacent port of
Melbourne by the main line, are in
the same category. They have very
little population. They are losing such
population as they have. They don’t
yield enough public income to pay for the
mounted cop who wanders over tlie face of
the country looking for the person who stole
the cow. And the most solid contribution*
they make to the wealth and glory and re-
venue and prosperity of the State is their'
old-age pension claims.
These counties of the South aud South-
West might supply a heap of revenue if they
had people to pay taxes and money with
which to pay taxes. But, as already men-
tioned, some of them have no railway to
carry their wheat and wool and hides and
meat to any place where the goods are
marketable, because the whole value of the
goods wouldn’t nay for freight to Sydney,
and no access is allowed' by any other ports.
And some of them have a railway to Sydney,
but the distance is so absurd and so need-
less, and it eats up so much of the profit, that
the game isn’t worth the candle. So N.S.W.
citizens depart or diminish or impoverish,
and cease to supply the big city with profit-
able business.
* * * *
The most remarkable chapter in the whole
gospel of Congestion is the N.S.W. port of
Twofold Bay. If railways were constructed
to Twofold Bay, and a city was laid ont
there, and every effort was made to give the
place a chance as a great sea-doorway for
foreign commerce, about a dozen N.S.W.
counties which arc now almost vacant, and
steadily becoming more vacant, would begin
to look up. They would boom just as much
as do the regions immediately adjacent to
Sydney—possibly more so, because they arc
much more fertile. And, incidentally, that
N.S.W. port would annex the trade ©*#
Eastern Victoria. Eastern Victoria has iro»
deep-water harbor of its own, but if N.S.W.
created a State-owned sub-metropolis about
Eden, the adjacent country over the border
would have a chance, and N.S.W. would reap
much profit. Any hardened and incurable
State Frighter should see that there
is a harvest to be gathered at Twofold
Bay. Its development would bring to life
and solvency, and even wealth, a great area
of N.S.W. which is now little more than
a hollow mendicant and an untenanted bank-
rupt. and this would be a material gain to
the State Tree •• It would put an end
to the clamor of u large section of N.S.W.
for means of communication with Melbourne,
b.v nroviding something much better in Corn-
stalk territory. And it would annex, for
all commercial purposes, a very consider-
able part of fire adjacent State to the State
about which the Evening News shows such
ill-regulated enthusiasm. The possibilities
of the case are obvious. There is a chance,
not only to greatly reduce the risk of the
hated hereditary enemy (Victoria) “looting”
l\Tq Sfnie. hut to vet in early and loot
the hated hereditary enemy. Yet because of
the noliev which regards Sydney as all
N.S.W., in a commercial sense, and every
nart of N.S.W. which isn’t Sydney as a
foreign country and a hostile proposition,
the opportunity is neglected. A sane paro-
chialism might do wonders in a matter like
this. But perhaps there is no such thing
as sane parochialism.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Religious Discourse.
Dear Bulletin, —A N.S.W. parson of the
Congregational persuasion—J. J. Willings
by name —driven well-nigh dippy, seemingly,
by the exiguousness of the Sabbath thrip-
pence, let fly last week in no uncertain
fashion on the wickedness of Australians
inasmuch as they uo longer crowded to wor-
ship the Gentle Nazarene “in the good old-
fashioned way”—meaning under the direc-
tion of clerics of his stamp. Willings
could not have appeared more scandalised
had he been a pro. whose turn had been
given the utter bird by a callous multitude.
Speaking of the type of citizen who reckons
that he gets no ethical uplift worth writing
home about, from his (Willings’) minis-
trations, the divine remarked :—-
We have observed him on the Sabbath Day-
arrayed in more or less picturesque undress uniform,
digging, weeding, planting or hosing his garden. We
have observed him taking relaxation from his hard
labors of the week in exciting games of tennis and
other open-air sports. We have seen him on the
Manly tram, with golf-sticks or other sporting equip-
ment, going to or returning from his open-air de-
votional exercises. We have had many a passing
glance at this “worshipper,” splashing in the surf
or playing bowls on the green. He certainly is
under the blue dome of Heaven in all these situa-
tions, but worshipping God he certainly is not.
Heaven is affronted by his brazen declaration, and
for our part we unhesitatingly denounce him for
what he is—a hypocrite, a Sabbath-breaker and a
cheat.
The enraged Willings attributed the entire
calamity to what he called “selfishness” :
This selfishness hails with joy the Sunday tram,
dictates the attitude of the tea-room proprietors,
keeps open the shops on Sunday, demands the Sun-
day paper, crowds the Sunday trams — but not to go
churchward —spreads itself on the beaches, patron-
ises the Sunday sports and concerts, and in no sepse
remembers the Sabbath day to keep it holy. It
ministers to the avarice of the few, and looks after
Number One.
There is nothing more pathetic than the
spectacle of a failure railing at an inappre-
ciative world. The poor little hedge-parson,
with his empty chapel, his seedy soup-plate
hat and his vast family—all of which have
to be maintained on a stipend of about 30s.
per week —is in the same class as the soured
old maid, the actor who has been egged out
of his job, the burst shopkeeper and the in-
solvent restaurateur. All these different types
are ready to supply every imaginable _ ex-
planation of their failure barring the right
one. namely incompetence. There are thou-
sands of distracted little clerics of the Wip-
ings stamp, all over Australia, who spend
miserable hours every week peering into the
bag or plate or hat or whatever it is, and
asking wildly why it is so barren of cash.
They never realise that they are themselves
to blame. The essence of sky-pilotage, _as
the normal person understands it, is charity.
If there was any one virtue which the
Saviour demanded of His followers more
than another it was that they should behave
with courtesy and self-restraint and regard
for other people’s feelings—in brief, as one
might say, like gentlemen. He deprecated
for the most part the creation of strife and
illwill: the use of insulting and abusive
language; and all other things tending to-
wards the stirring up of hatred and manor.
Yet alleged believers in His kindly teachings
weigh in with stuff like the following about


their business rivals, and expect to be taken
seriously as earnest gospel-mongers:
The Roman Catholic Church is an agent of evil,
subtle, crafty, scheming'. I refer to the influence,
sinister, menacing, selfish, of the Roman Catholic
Church. The devout R.C„ as we all know, if lie
groes early to Mass on Sunday, may go to the Devii
for the rest of the day, etc., etc.
The R.C. Church comports itself far more
subtly than this. Whatever it thinks about
the opposition it rarely says anything in pub-
lic. One might read through a dozen sec-
tarian papers—British, Continental or Aus-
tralian—and find not a word in disparage-
ment of Protestants. That is one of the
reasons why its educated and intelligent
members are able to regard it seriously. It
resembles a well-behaved Christian Church
more than it does a mediaeval Baslii-
Bazouk. The liome-loathing parsons will
do better business in the wav of at-
tracting congregations from the moment
they cease to cover themselves with
the mud which they sling at their trade
opponents, and get down to their legitimate
job of soul-salving. Their present tactics
are absolutely suicidal. The Willings of
the religious world do more for Sabbath
surf-bathing than all Rome’s minions. They
have but one hope of luring the thrippence
back to the plate; they must cease to bay
the moon, abandon profitless back-chat in
general, and nose as people with a genuine
belief in the Snirit of the Word. —I remain,
etc., A Humble Preacher.
Oil in Mexico.
Thus Max Adams in frenzy:—
I do hope that every greaser, mestizo, Mexican and
Indian in Mexico gets out his gun and rallies behind
the assassin lluekta against Woodrow Wilson's big
Oil Trust, until the President who “makes peace”
with a 12-inch gun finds that he has bitten off more
than he can chew, that his international cake is
dough, and that he is generally as mixed as these
metaphors. Over the murdering in Mexico there
hangs a horrible smell of Oil. Oil kept old-man Diaz
president till he was in his dotage, and then it turned
him out. Oil fooled round with revolutions till they
grew too hot for Oil to feel comfortable in their
vicinity ; and then Oil began to squeal to the navy
and army of the United States to “protect its in-
terests.”
Now President Wilson has butted into Mexico on
a pretext that is discreditably flimsy. The man
actually on top in that country of excitable half-
breeds is the amiable Huerta, who is establishing
himself according to the precedents set by Diaz in
liis early days; that is to say, he is getting rid of
troublesome people by shoving them underground.
The Oil that backed Diaz called him the strong auto-
crat necessary to keep order in a violent country.
The Oil that is worried by Huerta calls him a
usurper guilty of manslaughter. 'What Oil says goes.
The other big nations of the world, which are not
controlled by Oil, frankly admit that Huerta is top
dog in Mexico, and recognise him as the head of a
nation. But Oil doesn’t want him, and therefore the
leg of President Wilson, and the leg of the whole
United States, is pulled to make them go out and
slaughter Mexicans.
The slaughter has begun in a sufficiently bloody
fashion. On April 21 the U.S. marines landed at
Vera Cruz and killed 200 Mexicans. Four Ameri-
cans were killed; and the difference between four
and 200 shows what a cold massacre it must have
been, with the pick of a modern navy on one side
and the scrag garrison of a wayside port on the
other. President Wilson says that he is invading
not Mexico, hut Huerta only. Huerta is trying to
gather insurgents as well as Government armies
against the big bully who smells of Oil, and the
chances seem to be in favor of his success. When
that happens where will Wilson be? Either he, will
have to burn, massacre and. pillage a whole nation
— as Kitchener did to the Boers when other Eng-
lish generals proved not savage enough for the job—
or else he will have to run away from his _ present
pose, and own up that Huerta is boss in Mexico.
When Wilson was seeking the presidency, of the
United States he made some very fine speeches
against the grip of money interests in which America
is choking. “The people of the United States,” he
used to say, “do not govern themselves. They are
ruled by a few individuals.” His mission in politics,
as declared by himself, was to restore real self-
government as against domination by big financial
groups. And now Oil has proved too cute and too
powerful for him. It has pushed and twiddled and
faked him into ■ massacring Mexicans, with a bag of
200 as a first instalment.
Every nation of comparative weakness should
devoutly hope that the Mexican people will , stand
united against the big, greasy Oil-hullv who has come
into their country with a gun. Some day Australia
may quite possibly be a kind of Mexico. The Aus-
tralian republic is always a possibility of the poli-
tical future —there are quite a lot of ways in which
it may conceivably he brought into being, even
within the lifetime of David Storey —and in that day
money interests will be just as powerful and just
as cold-bloodedly selfish as they are in Mexico this
month. The lesson for Mexico to teach to Oil, and
to Beef, and to every huge financial interest is that
a whole nation is going to get up and fight against
the strong Power that comes in to pacify by mas-
sacre. The Mexican mestizo, dying 200 at a time
with his rusty gun, is fighting the battles of a
future Australia as well as his own.
So as an Australian 1 pray for a consolidation of
the rebels and the government in Mexico, till the
misguided Wilson has been taught that a nation is
not going to sit down and let itself he run by a
greasy, greedy barrel of Oil.

How Ulster Voted for Home Rule.
Dear Bulletin, —This is an example of
the logic of Rev. Fitchett, of “Deeds that
Won the Empire,” writing in defence of the
anti-Home Rule attitude of Edward Carson
and certain other Ulsterites -
If we were about to federate, and Western Aus-
tralia stubbornly stood out, and some lunatic pro-
posed to send a British squadron to bombard Fre-
mantle to compel Western Australia to come in by
the logic of guns, what would be said?
It is another curious development of the
Ulster superstitiou. When the Home Rule
Bill was before the House of Commons
ULSTER VOTED FOR HOME RULE.
In view of all the fuss and howl and clamor
and drink and religious protestation it seems
almost impossible, i L>t ULSTER VOTED
FOR IIOME RULE. Out of the 33 mem-
bers who represent that province there were
17 Irish Nationalist votes and 16 Unionist
votes. It might not be much of a majority,
but it was as good a majority as the
McGowen Government used to have in the
N.S.W. State Parliament, and as good a
majority as the Cook Government has now
in the House of Representatives. A recal-
citrant section of Ulster, occupied mostly
by non-Irish people—-the smaller section in
point of area, population and voting power—
is making a powerful fuss, and by sheer
clamor and effrontery it passes itself off as
being Ulster. Seeing that every daily paper
calls this impudent minority of a province
“Ulster,” it has certainly clamored with
some success. The case is just the same as
if all the States of Australia had voted for
Federation, but Geelong and the adjoining
districts had risen in revolt, and had called
themselves “Victoria,” and had declared they
would die in the last anti-Federal ditch, and
hnd called on the British ..Government, to
send a fleet and an army to prevent Federa-
tion, failing which aid Geelong and sur-
rounding districts would curse Britain with
their last breath, and haunt it with a troubled
ghost all through the Hereafter. The com-
position of the United Kingdom is somewhat
curious. Wales was conquered, but then
Wales wasn’t big enough to put up a serious
resistance, and it had never been a real
kingdom. At the best it was a collection
of miscroscopic principalities. And its tail
feathers were somewhat smoothed down by
the fact that the King’s eldest son was usu-
ally entitled “Prince of Wales.” The place
received a certain measure of polite recogni-
tion. Yet some parts of Wales are not much
more reconciled now than they were cen-
turies ago, and not very long since three
Welshmen in finding that the
writer couldn’t speak Welsh, spat bitterly
in all directions, and uttered the last pos-
sible word of Welsh reproach, which is “Sas-
senach.” The Scotch were more or less
reconciled to the union with England, be-
cause a Scotch king went to London to
rule over the hated English. They felt that
they had really annexed John Bull. Even
to this day the only claim that George V.
has to reign in London is that he is de-
scended from a Scotch king. Ireland, in the
early days, was far ahead of England, Scot-
land and Wales in art, literature and cul-
ture. It was so big that it couldn’t make, a
tame surrender without disgrace, yet it
wasn’t big enough to make a successful
resistance against the great brutal Angevin
Empire. No one soothed its vanity by
sending King Roderick O’Connor to
reign after a nominal sort of fashion
in London as did King James Stuart.
Nobody had sense enough to make the wife
of the heir-apparent Princess of Tara, or
anything of the sort. Ireland has always
been the insoluble problem and the lost dog
of the British Isles. Now an attempt is
being made to patch up the misery of cen-
turies by giving the island something ap-
proaching—only approaching—the same mea-
sure of self-government that Australia enjoys.
The necessary Bill was carried in the House
of Commons by a big majority. Wales,
where some of the people still spit with
great derision when they meet a person who
can’t; speak the old language of Gwent, gave
a 30 to one majority in favor of Irish Home
Rule. Scotland, which has got over the fever
of enthusiasm and swelled head which pos-
sessed it when a Scotch king went to reign in
London, gave a 3| to one majority. The
members representing three of the Irish pro-
vinces voted for Home Rule to a man. In
the fourth province—Ulster—the Home Rule
idea was. carried by a majority of one, the
hostile minority representing a purely foreign
element. England, with an eye to the fact
that it is the dominant and con-
quering and unpopular element of the
partnership, voted against the idea of
an Irish sub-Parliament such as every
Australian State possesses, but even Eng-
land did so only by a small margin. The sen-
timent of the British Isles as a whole, as ex-
pressed in the House of Commons, was
strongly in favor of some measure of Irish
autonomy. And all the blither that has
filled the papers for months past is the
blither of the tail-end, which is also the alien
end, of Ulstel - .. Having been defeated at the
noils even on its own stamping ground, and
defeated in the House of Commons, it can’t
take its defeat like a gentleman.—Yours, etc.,
Henry llorsecollar.

The Immigrant Attitude Towards Local
Defence.
A well-meaning specimen of the genus ass
or goat, who subscribed himself “Highland
Laddie,” in a letter to the S. M. Herald
(23/4/T4), gave away the whole case for
what are called “National Regiments.” He
began by deploring the drab ingloriousness of
the uniforms supplied to the Highlanders of
the Commonwealth forces. He complained
bitterly of the dearth of “embellishments”
on the Scottish warrior’s doublet, the un-
alluring architecture of his kilt; the cheap-
ness of his spats ; and the inadequacy of his
sporran. He deduced from all these things
that the Australian Government has no real
belief in the value of the “National regi-
ment” business, and he wound up this way:
If the authorities are sincere, by all means let
them have issued uniforms identical with those worn,
say, by the Black Watch (Royal Highlanders), for
the Scottish; the 3rd Buffs, or the 7th Royal Fusil-
iers, for the English; and the green of the Royal
Irish Rifles, for our national regiments. These new
regiments will then be rushed by men of picked
physique, and they will at once become the crack
corps of the Commoniuealth.
Here we have a priceless example of the
C. E. Jacomb, or immigrant, point of view
as regards local defence. The writer
of the letter fails, in the first place,
to appreciate that the record and tra-
ditions of the Black Watch, the Buffs,
etc., afford no inspiration to Aus-
tralians. There is a limit to the importing
craze; and a point exists at which a self-
respecting white nation feels impelled to put
a period to the habit of monkey-like imita-
tiveness. It is hard to imagine a more'
hilarious piece of imbecility than the spec-
tacle of a bogus Black Watch regiment com-
posed chiefly of young men who have never
even seen Scotland, and who speak the lan-
guage of Surry Hills and the ’Loo; while
there is equal humor in the idea of a regiment
of Royal Irish Rifles, none of whose members
has lived in Ireland, or is more like an
Irishman than any other born and bred Aus-
tralian. These pathetic frauds would be the
laughing-stock not only of Australia, but of
every Britisher who had the merest glimmer
of a sense of proportion. The defence autho-
rities, by clothing the “national regiments”
in garments of so involved and nondescript
a character that they mean nothing in par-
ticular, have saved the misguided warriors
who enter them from the greater ridicule, if
they have done nothing more. If they have
also thereby prevented these regiments from
being “rushed by men of picked physique”
and turned into “the crack corps of the
Commonwealth.” then have the authorities
done the whole defence movement a good
turn. There are two sorts of crack corps in
existence in most European armies. The
cracknels of the one variety is denendent
chiefly on “swank” ; of the other on efficiency.
The 10th Hussars is a crack British corps
inasmuch as no officer can enter it who is
not rich, of distinguished birth, and person-
ally,, known (o , the King. The Marines is
not a crack corps. The very humblest and
most indigent person can be an officer of
Marihes, provided his character is good and
he has sufficient intelligence to pass the
requisite examinations. Yet the fighting
record of the Marines, as a corps, is more
brilliant than that of the 10th Hussars, and
incomparably more so than the records of
such glittering corps as the First and Second
Life Guards. If the Australian citizen army
is to be a success the very first element that
requires to be eliminated is the “swank”
element. Questions of gorgeous imported
haberdashery, and of the country of origin
of the trainees’ parents or grand-parents
should be disregarded utterly. Efficiency
should be the one and only mark of a crack
corps. Almost any ostentatious idiot can
heap sporrans and dirks and shamrocks on
himself for review purposes or street wear;
but the useful warrior is one who. regardless
of “embellishments” and the like, lays him-
self out to seriously learn his job.
Asa sample of the Law’s immemorial
asininity, consider the following narrative. A
while ago Sophie George, of Fitzroy (Vic.),
told the Law that her snouse, Henry George,
had deserted her. The Law said it would
salvage him. was unearthed ulti-
mately at Goulburn (N.S.W.), and led back
to Fitzroy, at a total cost to the State of
£S 15s. The lady, having recovered her
snouse, intimated that she didn’t want him
gaoled. All she required was his company
and part of his earnings. The Law said
that it would consider this question favor-
ably. on condition that Henry paid the
£8 15s. that his dash for freedom had cost
it. At about this stage the Law unex-
pectedly fell upon George and carted him
back to Goulburn to answer a charge of mis-
prision or barratry or something. He was
acquitted, so the Law' humped him over the
border once more, and as he. somewhat ex-
ctisably, hadn’t paid any of the original
£S 155., it hauled him before the ’itov beaks
again. He pleaded that he had been so
busy travelling at the Law’s behest that he
hadn’t had time to earn the cash —or main-
tain his wife either. He wound up a pas-
sionate exposition of his wrongs by offer-
ing the Law ss. a week, the which was
scornfully rejected. Thereupon the Law
on the bench informed the Law in the body
of the court that nobody had any power to
make an order for Costs —a singular over-
sight on the part of Costs —and that as
there was nothing to be got by gaoling the
man, it would adjourn the case some more,
in the hope that his better feelings, or his
love for his widowed mother, or something
would impel him to pay up. The Law off
the bench concealed its feelings admirably,
and courteously requested Henry Geobgk
to produce the coin—but this writer reckons
it was a fool not to have accepted the ss.
offer. So far it is £26 ss. to the bad, and
£l7 10s. of that is gone for good, also its
chances of getting the rest are not too rosy.
Still, it has had some relaxation out of
Mr. George; he and his vagaries and his
journeyings must have helped pleasurably
to pass the time.

The present Vic. Ministry has at least one
distinction : it has let more urgent matters rip
Ihan any other in the history of the State.
The expensive Goulburn levies scheme was
abandoned by it; it has dropped the more
costly Maffra Beet Sugar enterprise; it de-
clines to put an end to the infamous dredg-
ing business; it has gone back on its policy
of reserving the coal measures of the State
for State development; it is carefully jetti-
soning every progressive plank in its original
programme, and even in such a comparatively
small matter as running the State timber-
seasoning yards at Newport, it can’t follow
the straight and honest track. Private
Enterprise has all along supplied these sea-
soning kilns so intermittently and inade-
quately, that they cannot be worked as a
cheap, steady job, so the departmental forest
and timber experts recommended the estab-
lishment of State mills, in order that good
cutting, even quality and a regular supply
might ensure the success of the seasoning
undertaking. Minister of Forests, J. Dry
Brown, however, has superior ideas. He
proposes to let the drying kilns go to perdi-
tion, and will have timber cut in the forests
and allowed to lie there till it is seasoned.
Brown’s patent idea is to save expense and
let Nature do the work of the kilns. Un-
fortunately, Nature also has other ideas, and
when the usual bushfire happens in the sum-
mer, and Brown’s seasoned timber-stacks
disappear in smoke, it may dawn on him
that he didn’t know quite as much as he
thought. Meanwhile he is busily denying
that he has been “approached by any secret
deputations representing private interests
and concerned at the development of the
Newport kilns.”

(Q.) suggested a trial of the Elective Min-
istry system in Australian politics was a
nasty innuendo against the character of his
own side of the House. “Does the hon. mem-
ber not think that the Ministry would be
wholly chosen from the side which had the
majority?” asked the lofty representative of
Capital. Bruce Smith, seemingly, cannot
conceive it possible that a party of 38, in-
cluding Mr. Speaker, would give the other
party of 37 a chance in the allotment of even
one portfolio. As for the fact that the party
line would be obliterated in subsequent Par-
liaments, elected on the Elective Ministry
understanding, Bruce Smith is stone blind
to it. The inevitable result of a radical
change doesn’t come within the vision of a
Tory who wears an eyeglass and who has
dissociated himself from Senator Miixen in
Speaking in disparaging terms of Japan.
«. ——
Dear Bulletin, —Your remarks re the Vic-
torian decentralisation scheme and Portland
hit the nail full in the centre of the occiput.
The said scheme is an elaborate design to
decentralise nothing. The crux of the ques-
tion lies in the railways, and the direction in
which they run ; a point anyone can see by
examining the black spokes radiating from
Melbourne. Portland’s connection with the
North is being pushed on with the rapidity
of a steam roller. But the joke or tragedy
of the whole business is that the line under
construction south from Toolondo is being
laid with light rails discarded elsewhere as
unfit to carry heavy burdens. So when the
Portland-Wimmera connection is completed,
the wheat will be as far as ever from its
natural outlet until the line is relaid with
heavier metals. Decentralisation by that
time will be advertised as a failure, because
Portland will not have attracted any more
trade, than the scraps that the pro-
posed line will be capable of carry-
ing. The Maroona-Gheringhap line, just
recently opened, is laid with large metals,
and helps to relieve the congestion of goods
traffic on the main Melbourne-Adelaide line.
(Though why, by the way, the overflow
shouldn’t go from Maroona to Portland, in-
stead of to 'Melbourne, a greater distance, is
an insoluble problem.) The case of Vic-
toria’s other natural harbor, Westernport, is
still more desperate. This fine bay is booted
out of the hunt , altogether, and now that it
carries a naval station Melbourne’s chance
of receiving merchandise per its deep portals
may be closed for ever. So Victoria’s one
chance of seeing the 50,000-ton liner of to-
morrow is Portland. For the heavy expen-
diture of blasting the Heads and dredging
Port Phillip will, after all, only allow small
ocean mammoths within sight of the Yarra.
Many weird schemes are being concocted to
make Port Phillip navigable to all craft.
Among the weirdest is one to cut anew
opening at Sorrento, and block the present
heads with the material therefrom. Locks in
the new cut would raise Port Phillip waters
five or six feet. Where this depth-creating
liquid was to come from, and where the
ships inside would be when the Japs blew
up the locks, were questions left out of the
estimates. Meanwhile the cocky whose wheat
is held up. and the owners of the live stock
which perish in great agony on the over-
crowded lines, are supposed to be content
with the news that a few thousands, are
being spent in Portland, that nothing is to
be outlaid on Westernport. and that there is
a scheme for blowing millions on the impos-
sible rask of transforming Port Phillip into
a modern harbor.—Yours, etc., Leo Lear.
Concerning the Reports.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My advisers submitted to the Premiers' Confer-
ence a proposal for the establishment of a uniform
railway gauge throughout the Commonwealth, and
suggested that the question be referred to the Inter-
state Commission for examination and report.—
Governor-General's speech at opening of Federal
Parliament.
Let us have a report!
Our railway system is confusion.
The gauges are broken at every State boun-
dary.
We have considered the matter;
The Premiers’ Conference has considered it;
It is meet that the Inter-State Commission
consider it some more.
Let us have a report!
Who is he that sayeth “Yah”?
Who is he that snorteth scornfully?
Is not a report good?
Is it not comely that the thing should be
considered?
Why do the. people say “Yah!” and also
“Tush!” and “Bah!”?
Why do they grin with derision?
What aileth them that they repeat again our
words with mockery?
Yah! Get a move on. We have had plenty
of reports.
Tush! Get work. Ye know what is to be
done.
Bali! Stand not with the look of shorn
mutton, but shift the rails.
Nay, but let us consider,
Let us have a report:
Let us do all things in order;
Let us discover if the time be ripe!
Perchance the engineers may yet tell us that
there is no need to worry.
Perchance the gauges are the same if they be
closely examined.
Perchance 3ft. Gin. in the North may be
4ft. BJin. in the South.
Perchance our engines may be taught to
expand and contract with the climate.
Perchance the whole affair may be adjusted
by hypnotic influence.
Perchance Perchance
But, anyhow, let us have a report.
What report would one of independent mind
give?
What word would the faithful of heart write?
Behold, these words would the man of truth
say:
“Ye have known what is at fault these
forty years.
Ye know what is to be done, and do it not.
Your conferences are but a shuffle to avoid
working.
Your reports are but an expedient to waste
time.
There is no uniform gauge in Australia,
because ye desire none.
There is no remedy for the evil save ye be
kicked into action.
This is the true and faithful report of the
matter as it stands.”
Nay. but a report is a pleasant thing.
Someone gettetli paid for drawing it up.
It rejoiceth the people to gaze upon it.
Let us have a report.
Yea, even a little report,
That we may bring it before a conference,
Or two or three conferences.
Let us have reports on the conferences.
And thereafter a report by some engineers
on the report of the conferences,
And a report by some conferences on the
report of the engineers;
And thereafter let us wait until the time is
ripe.
Yea, let us have a report on the ripening of
the time.
What report is this?
Say ye that the foeman hath set his boots
in the North?
Blow ye the bugle!
Call the troops together that we may smite
him!
Lo, the railway is broken in pieces !
The thunders of our battle sit kicking their
heels on the loose ends!
What report is this?
It is .the explosion that burstetli a continent.
Yea, it is the blowing up of a careless nation.
For their leaders waited a report,
And a report upon a report upon a report,
Till the enemy reported at the gate,
And his report was the report of cannon.
And there was none to answer them until
the 247th conference had brought in
its recommendation on the 498th
report!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMATWEALTH.

A ETESIAN
S'- experience
If Sydney Morning Herald (27/4/T4) is
right, Guillaux, the French aviator who is
floating round, has strong claims for official
interference. On Saturday he flew at New-
castle, and amongst those who were on the
take-off ground were 200 military cadets.
Describing the flight, S.M.H.’s local corres-
pondent, under the heading “Testing the
Cadets,” lias this remarkable piece of folly
to relate: —
Mr. Guillaux soared to a great height, and, evi-
dently with the idea of testing the stability of the
cadets in a crisis, the Frenchman directed his machine
towards them. He tilted the monoplane, and took
a straight dive directly for the small company of
cadets. Down the maehire. came like an arrow.
People held their breath for a moment, and gave
vent to a scream as they realised that the airman
would be dashed to pieces and the ground strewn
with dead cadets, .lust when the audience expected
to hear the awful crash as the machine struck the
cadets, the aviator pulled a lever and gracefully
skimmed over the heads of the cadets. Death and
the cadets were separated only by about four feet,
but they stood their ground.
For sheer asininity that would be hard to
match. If professional airmen, most of
whose stock in trade is risk, are to be
allowed to put thrills into their shows at
the chance of murdering a company of
cadets, the small unpopularity against com-
pulsory training will be given an unholy
boost. Will this bounding Gaul please
understand that the testing of cadets is no
i art of his job !

, , Tuesday was the cablegramimer’s busy day.
'I he items included a temporary upheaval
in the eastern corner of Ulster, with the
Orange rebels in possession of roads and
railway stations and landing guns openly;
a battle on the Greek border; news that the
miners' strike in Colorado (U.S.) had prac-
tically developed into a civil war. and that
the staggered President had so much Mexi-
can fuss on his hands that he had no soldiers
to spare; report that 800.000 more miners
proposed to strike and paralyse the IJ.S.
Government.: alleged great exaltation of the
tail of President Huerta and projects for a
Mexican invasion of the States; rumors of
possible, interference by Brazil, Argentina
and Chile; illness of the Emperor of Aus-
tria. with the usual rumors of complica-
tions ; also sundries.
The very sloppiest, most inartistic and
casual female liar on record transpired at
Goulburn (N.S.W.) last week. She burst
into the Ivenmore Lunatic Asylum, where
she is employed, with a fevered yarn of
robbery and rapine. She told the scandal-
ised Asylum authorities that shortly after
leaving Goulburn she had been leapt upon
by strange men who, despite her furious
struggles, prayers, protests and howls, out-
raged her and robbed her of her new hat.
The police failed to find the malefactors.
A doctor who examined their alleged victim
found her to be virgo Intacta. Also there
was not a solitary bruise on her of the sort
that a violent struggle would occasion. And,
finally, the hat of which she claimed to have
been bereft • turned out to be still at its
hat-shop of origin. What niche in life is
there for a wild, careless fabricator like that?
As editor-in-chief of one of those organs
which specialise in hectic tales about
the murderous ways of Romish minions
in Australia, she might do a bit of good
for herself. Sydney Watchman told
some years ago of the midnight assassina-
tion and burial in St. Mary’s Cathedral of
“a white-robed girl”—which happening was
witnessed from behind a pillar by two
Orangemen named respectively “Y” and
“X.” The Goulburn maiden should be able,
with encouragement, to evolve quite a lot
of romance on the above lines. But the
odds are she would put the acid of
disbelief on any given narrative by being
too explicit. The prevaricator who supplies
details is a danger to herself, to the news-
paper which publishes her fabrications, to
the gossips who retail them to the world —
to everyone, in brief, except the people they
are designed to injure.
Another Tale of Mean Streets. The
Broken Hill attempted murder and suicide
of last week happened in Slag-street. Can
anything good come out of Slag-street?
The councillors of Northcote (Vic.) de-
cided recently to “celebrate” the proclama-
tion of the dusty place as a “city” by
inviting the State Excellency and them-
selves to a banquet—-otherwise a free feed—-
which costly function they intend shall be
paid for by the ratepayers. If Northcote
were in a financial position to carry on in
this exuberant fashion it would be all right.
But it isn’t. The suburb’s 20,000 inhabi-
tants boast, in addition to many miles of
unmade streets, a debt of £BO,OOO and an
overdraft of £BOOO. A municipal debt of
£BB,OOO, among about 5000 adult males, is
the sort of liability that ought to cut
revelry off at the meter, so to speak, till
such time as the wolf of possible insolvency
is banished from the premises.
There is money in the tango. A song-
dance-and-drama school told a London REfebee in-
terviewer last month that it had in the nast nine
weeks earned £ 1 000 for teaching the foolishness, and
had any number of aonlications from hotels, etc.
for tango couples at £20 a week.

Athenveum Hall Alexander Watson Recitals.
While the Adelphi (the Sydney home of
bellowdrama) is experimenting with musical
comedy, the Royal, which has housed a long
string of m.c.’s, is now offering its customers
‘‘Sealed Orders,” a Raleigh and Hamilton
Drury Lane bellow of the largest size. It
remains to be seen whether the steady corned
beef patrons really want chicken, and the
chicken patrons red corned beef —not a little
corned beef, but heaps of corned beef —
corned beef piled in a smoking pyramid that
stretches from 7.45 to somewhere near 11,
and is moistened with the soup-water known
as “hurry music.” The whole of the first
act, consisting of a drop and a good “set,”
is devoted to telling how the diamond mer-
chant’s clerk, a widower with a girl-child
and the dreariest sister that ever moaned
at mortal man, frequents racecourses, is dis-
missed for that same on a Saturday after-
noon, and is about to poison himself with a
very special brand of traceless poison, in
his employer’s deserted office, when some
fragments of plaster fall from the ceiling.
As he watches, a hole appears therein and
a capsized umbrella is shoved through and
opened to catch the plaster the burglars
above are removing. The clerk puts his
poisoned wine aside, slips behind the window
curtain, and stays there, while the leading
burglar comes down a knotted rope and
swiftly opens the old safe in modern fashion
with an oxo-hydrogen jet or something of the
kind. Accidentally knocking some old papers
over and getting his nose and throat full of
dust, he seizes and drinks the poisoned wine
abandoned by the clerk. Result —one dead
burglar iu front of an open safe. The clerk
sneaks out fro,m his hiding-place, steals the
diamonds for himself and hurries out for the
police. The burglar on the next floor, be-
coming anxious, calls to his dead mate, and,
getting no answer, climbs down to investi-
gate. Just when he has realised that mate
Bill is dead a torrent of police
rushes in, headed by the thievish clerk, and
the live burglar is wrongfully dragged off to
do 20 years hard for stealing the diamonds
and slaying his pal. That is a good scene—
highly dramatic, original and well played.
HANS RENHAUSER CONDUCTING AT
SYDNEY ADELPHI.
But when the action begins again after 20
years, the bellow-in-chief starts in earnest,
and the playhaunter realises that what he
has just seen was merely a good prologue,
and that the central figure thereof is merely
a secondary character in the main shriek.
He lias degenerated into a sort of; agent for
the usual oily foreign ambassador wijh per-
fect manners and the worst intentions to-
wards dear old England. The usual grand
old Admiral’s beautiful young wife (on the
stage the exalted person always has a beau-
tiful young wife, who feels lonely while he
is unavoidably absent saving the Empire
with one hand) has bought too many ex-
pensive hats, and, after the oily baron has
oiled her into a stylish gambling hell and
got her a few thousands further into debt,
in the hope of getting out, it is comparatively
easy to get her to steal the dear old Sealed
Orders from her husband’s safe on the flag-
ship. (Note that this—the pivot of the play
—is reached about 9 o’clock, which is pretty
slow going.) The “Orders” (in most other
bellows they are “the papers”) are recovered
through the exertions of a Boy Scout, some
screaming naval officers, a man fortune-
teller, and a beauty specialist (Beatrice
Day). The last-named clever actress is
afflicted with a bustling part, which is as
nearly as possible the Female Comic Sailor.
This is calculated to sadden anyone who re-
members her in such comedies as “Dr.
Wake’s Patient.” By dint of having a cast
of 40 or so, and some 16 scenes to hurry
them about in, the wretched Orders still
honorably Sealed, are recovered from the air-
ship wreck about 11 p.m., and handed with
a salute to the grand old Admiral, whose
young wife apnears to be quite cheertui about
it. Presumably she is still on the verge of
bankruptcy and social ruin.
Ethel Warwick, as the indiscreet wife of
the Admiral, is e’esrant and coldly uncon-
vincing. .Lincoln Plainer bustles fairly well
as Hannibal Calhoun, the Yankee fortune-
teller. Edward O’Neill ranges from medium
to very good as Le Page, the thieving clerk.
And H. W. Varna earns thanks for an even
study of a Jewish gent. Frank Harvey is
sadly realistic as .Toe Allan, the gaol-crushed
burglar who comes out after 20 years, only
to be suspected and threatened by the son of
his dead mate. George Bryant was a suit-
ably heavy Admiral on the first night; at
the time of writing Reynolds Denniston has
an artistic grip on the cart. Fred Cam-
bourne is delightful as Old Alf. the kindly,
watchful, white-whiskered criminal who be-
friends Joe. The show is well mounted, but
the soup music should be poured down the
sink.
