Sydney Society is gladdening the heart
of the management of the Imperial Salon
de Luxe (which is the Imperial dancing
saloon) by rolling up in large parties to
hop, hug and tango. On Friday night there
is to be a “grand Thespian supper,” but who
the grand Thespian is doesn’t transpire on
the advertisement. Michael Joseph was in-
terrogated on the subject, but he merely re-
marked that he thought it was going to
rain.
Hilary Long, the up-side-down man, has
had his star place on the National Amphi-
theatre (Sydney) programme annexed by
the Banzai troupe of Japanese athletes. Not
that brother Long has departed. He still
bumps down the gangway on his head. The
Japs give the programme a lift, and when
the tiresome tango pair depart all should
be well.
* * * *
Another of Allen Doone’s chicken plays,
“Mollie Bawn.” was revived at the Palace
(Sydney) on Saturday night. The Bulle-
tin has. spoken of the piece more than once,
and always with a spirit of tolerance. The
Infuriated Editor being on holiday at pre-
sent, no alteration will be made in that
policy. Anyhow, Mollie is to be fired on
Friday night, and “The Colleen Bawn” given
another trial. How many “Bawn” plays
are there, by the way?
Carnivals are all the go at the White
City (Sydney) these days, and another is
to rage on Saturday, when, according to
more or less reliable information, an ox will
be roasted whole, and other old English fri-
volities practised. The last occasion in
Sydney that an ox was roasted in its en-
tirety was, as far as this paper knows,. on
the occasion of the departure of Governor
Darling. To show his appreciation of the
event W. C. Wentworth cooked the complete
animal at his Vaucluse residence, and the
guests returning home hurled the shin bones
at Darling’s ship. It was a great day.
The demise of Fred Niblo has been asain
postponed. “Never Say Die,” at Sydney
Criterion, will expire on Friday, but on
Saturday the immortal comedian will ap-
pear for a farewell five nights as “The For-
tune Hunter.”
Tlvse who have not seen Hugh Buckler
a« “Ti'e Gav Lord Quex,” at the Little
Theatre, Sydney, had better hasten, sinc-p
on Friday afternoon he is advertised to enter
the lions’ den at Wirths* Circus. But six
untamed lions, after all, are less dangerous
to a man in his position than a hungry
countess who asks him to her den at mid-
night.

The Sydney Tivoli, having a few bob to
spare, has decided to inflict a tango competi-
tion (amateurs only) on the undeserving
public. The competitions are to take place
at the tango teas and ou Friday nights,
when they will cause a minimum of incon-
venience to regular Tiv.-goers.

The Bulletin is still keeping an alert
optic on the .T. C. Williamson panto, at Her
Majesty’s. .Sydney, and the moment there is
any indication of a slackening pulse, specks
before the eyes, fever, beri-beri, dread of
something awful about to happen, or other
symptoms of decay, the public shall be noti-
fied. At present the writer has no hesita-
tion in stating that the show is as healthy
as it ever was in its life.
From an Adelaide critic:—
Mrs. Mumme, better known as Miss Florence
Britten, is e;ivinar a single-handed picture-show that
is a refreshing change from the usual. She has some
good, original work, mostly landscape,, architecture,
or decorative heads; and in black-and-white, for
fashion-plate or similar purposes, she is supreme.
But the novelty comes in with really good copies of
famous works that Australia knows only by repute:
These have been made at London National Gallery,
or wherever the paintings may now be kept, and have
been approved by the various keepers as worthy.
Miss Britten has caught the widely-differing tricks
with astonishing cleverness: Reynolds’s children,
Murillo’s Drinking Dov (the glass finelv handled!.
Romney’s Lady Hamilton, a knight b.v Velasquez, and
so on. Among the more moderns are Turner’s
unlikely Brook. Hacker’s delicate Annunciation,
Mason’s breezv Wind on-the-Wold, and a lifelike Alma
Tadema. The little exhibition is a success, both
popular and artistic.

Adelaide Royal, open once more and
hardly recognising itself in its changed and
beautified appearance, is giving the German
musical farce. “Autumn Manoeuvres,” most
skilfully Anglicised, and with dialogue of
unusual brilliance for this class of work.
The idea, openly pursuing girl and boast-
fully elusive man, is “Merry Widow” over
again, but the setting is novel. Florence
Young, brilliant as ever, does the girl full
justice in everything but appearance.
Dorothy Brunton comes to the front rank
with a bound, and Connie Milne makes a
small part stand out by polished acting.
Fr.ank Greene can both act and sing, and
Phil Smith is a worthy successor to the
long line of hard-working comedians in
comic opera. Quick changes are to mark
the rest of the season. At the Tivoli are
some restless, delightful leopards, with a
Vallecita ladv nutting them through their
tricks. Ed Ford, who claims to be “the
original Australian sundowner,” is back
from foreign parts; and the tango teas
continue popular as before. The King’s is
strong musically, to the Lassies now being
added Eagle Horse, a tamed Sioux. Fanny
Rice, the musical Dupres, and Hyman and
M ton’s acrobatics Imlp in a good bill.
Brnnscombe’s Dandips have packed up. and
w ade a swallow flight—Poland Henrv. Pace.

The latest outbreak of Repertory play in
Melbourne confirms one's suspicion that the
ideal feminine of our early Australian
dramatists is not an advanced woman.
She's the girl we’ve left behind us. She is
a dream of the golden age, when Love was
the only law, and proud man had the mak-
ing of it. This is the lesson of a double
bill produced at St. Peter’s Hall last Satur-
day, before a crowded audience of intellectual
nuts and nutesses, the latter of whom mostly
went home scoffing. First came “Adrift, - ’
a dualogue by Kathleen Watson Deardon, in
place of a previously-rehearsed curtain-raiser.
“Adrift” presents a gentle divorcee, living
a lonely life, whilst awaiting a visit from her
co-respondent. The decree nisi has not: yet-
been made absolute, and she is stitching a
frock for the babe that will be born later
on. When the co-re. arrives he is palpably
bored. He talks of marriage in a dejected
manner, giving the lady to understand it is
one thing for a man to love another man’s
wife, and quite another thing for him to
preserve the infatuation through a life-long
period of his own lawful matrimony with
her. Incidentally the co-re. is shown to be
ignorant of the baby on the ofiing, and when
be chances to discover the tell-tale frocklet,
during the lady’s absence from the room, he
is further convinced that marriage would
be a failure. So without mentioning his
sudden enlightenment he gets back to bache-
lor life, with the respondent’s warm kisses
on his lips. She is left to find happiness
in her unselfish solicitude for his future,
vvhich is just how the average co-re. would
like to leave the fair divorcee- —if he could.
Kathleen Watson’s dualogue deserved more
I'inotional treatment that it got from Miss
Isabel Handley and Gerald Carr.
Alfred Buchanan’s “The Image Breaker”
was a three-act dream of fond women. The
images were understood to be all the social
and political gods of such as read the Argus,
and Harry Manders was the self-satisfied
“Socialist” who broke ’em. He started
promisingly by cursing a coal-mine proprie-
tor for his indifference to the safety of his
employees. The second act dealt with
Manders as a firebrand and a Lothario.
Hetty, the daughter of another Socialist (his
whiskers proclaimed his ignobility of charac-
ter), had formerly given her affections 1.0
Manders, it seemed with the consent of her
hairy parent, and was now an anxious
mother. Her unlawful lord had cooled off
considerably since she became a mother, but
they were mutually interested in a
Housing Reform Association founded by
Madeline, the philanthropic daughter of
an angry coal owner, whose partner
wanted to marry her. Madeleine would
have preferred to marry Harry Man-
ders, _ but he shocked her at this time
by sticking to his free-love principles. Also
when Hetty mildly besought him to make
her Mrs. M., for their eheild’s sake, he told
her that his heart had turned towards the
other girl, whereat she signed heavily, but
was not at all jealous. In fact, when
Madeleine revenged herself on the intractable
Manders by turning frivolous and marrying
the bloated capitalist, gentle Hetty went to
live with her.
Three years later Madeleine felt so sick
of Sassiety that she sent for Harry and told
him to take her away on his own terms.
Unfortunately her husband dropped in at
this critical moment, and an animated con-
versation, in which Hetty joined, ended in the
Image Breaker retiring girlless from the
scene. Hetty had promised to go on loving
him hopelessly as before, whereas Madeleine
had fallen on her husband’s chest and grown
conventional again. Some neatly-sketched
minor characters, together with the melo-
dramatic situations, bits of bright dialogue
and surprising developments of the dislo-
cated plot kept the audience more interested
than edified. The author had not written
any “business” into his play, but he had at
least put some good theatrical effects into
it. Of the principal members of the co.
Miss Robson was merely a graceful and
pleasing heroine, whilst Cunninghame. as the
hero, was ungraceful and repellent. The
performance of the others, with the excep-
tion of the husband and the chief Sassiety
lady (Miss Leon), sank considerably below
tbe Repertory’s ordinary level. On a deeper
stage, with a professional co., the play would
make a favorable impression on the men.

At three of the Melbourne theatres the
shadow of impending dissolution has fallen
on the bill. “Madame X” will drag her
weary, disreputable feet from the Royal
stage on Thursday of next week, and “What
Happened to Mary” will happen no more
at the King’s after Friday (May 8). The
Bert Bailey Co. is due in Queensland shortly,
therefore the nights of Mary are numbered,
and when that refreshingly artless damsel
goes away the present season ends.
Also next week, is the last of. Lauder at
the Princess’s, for he will put in appear-
ances at Ballarat and elsewhere before pro-
ceeding to Adelaide. Harry Lauder is
changing ins songs somewhat these times,
but “The Saftest o’ the Family” remains his
centrepiece, and the chorus of the congrega-
tion is still earnestly requested for “A Wee
Deoeh and Doris.” No vaudeville artist has
rivalled the ingratiating, kilted songster as
an advocate of the wee drappie that makes
you happie.
* * * *
Alexander Watson will monologue for
three or four more nights at Melbourne
Athenaeum Hall, ere starting his long tour.
On his first reappearance in Melbourne last
week his recitation of Masefield’s “The Ever-
lasting Mercy” proved to be a wonderful
feat of memory and magnetism. For about
Xi hours without a break he held the audi-
ence with a story in verse, of the awful bad
bloke who found grace in a pub, and went on
the land to work out his salvation. Perhaps
the Athenaeum Hall was hardly the right,
place for his fine word-picture of the Fight.
It should have been given in the Stadium,
with a Salv’army captain to introduce the
orator, and Boko Bludger, champion of the
middle-weights, to wait upon him with a
water-bottle and tumbler. To-night (Thurs-
day) the Watson bill is “Paolo and Fran-
cesea.” To-morrow it will be “King Rich-
ard II.” by a famous old dramatist of the
Elizabethan age.

At Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, there is
no mention thusfar of the last nights of
“Come Over Here.” The debatable revue
happens as before,' and the flaming hair,
earnest gaze and conversational ditties of
Daisy Jerome are still the most boosted
features of the betangoed entertainment.
* * *
New songs by Ada Reeve are the only
noticeable difference in the Melbourne Tivoli
bill. The outside of the house is outlined
with colored lights, and the name of the star
lady is set forth in large, glaring capitals
to prevent any public misunderstanding as to
Ada being the highest-salaried lady per-
former in the universe. The appearance of
the building at night convinces every ob-
server that something terribly expensive
must be happening inside.
Down at St. Kilda Esplanade the Dandies
will go on appearing in their canvas-girt
theatre until May 11. This week they com-
mence a “topsy-turvy” season, during which
the girls will wear the garments and repro-
duce the character sketches of their male
brethren, and their male brethren will re-
taliate in short skirts and winning smiles.
The last Friday of the season will be a
“request” night, and a revue of all the sea-
son’s goods on the Saturday and Monday
will close the Branscombean show for the
present.
Ellen Terry comes to hand at Port Mel-
bourne on Monday next, when a deputation
from all the local societies that spell cul-
chaw with a capital C will greet the dis-
tinguished personage. Next afternoon the
Gaud Mayoress gives a reception in her
honor, and the Shakspeare Soc. will drag
her to the Austral Salon in the evening.
On May 7 and two nights thereafter Ellen
Terry discourses on the Bard’s heroines, with
illustrative acting, at the Town Hall. Jos.
Blascheck and two or three other artists are
to supply the balance of the entertainment.

The Bulletin’s music-hall expert con-
siders the Tasmanian problem :
The incursion of that eminent legal and consti-
tutional authority, Sir Frederick Pollock, into the
Tasmanian Parliamentary row was all that was
needed to complete a splendidly-farcical situation.
It was like an archbishop dashing his mitre to the
earth, and wading into a street row between a
couple of small boys. Tasmania is no place for seri-
ous thinkers. It is more a sort of island vaude-
ville entertainment. Consider some of its leading
low-comedy turns. When Ellison Macartney, the
most satisfactory Governor the eccentric place has
ever had (inasmuch as he runs truest to type) imposed
on Labor-leader Earle a number of preposterous con-
ditions as the price of office, he (Macartney) thought
he had scored. He knew that without his assent
Earle could not be Premier. And he also knew
that Earle badly wanted the job. Earle promised
all Macartney wanted, grabbed office, and immedi-
ately, to all intents and purposes, told Macartney
to go and boil himself, and refused to carry out
any of the latter’s behests. Macartney can’t take
the Premiership away from Earle. He can, of
course, dissolve Parliament, and, provided Earle is
returned with the same following as before, get
back to the original position. Hut in view of the
infernal uproar that his efforts have already caused,
he doesn’t dare to take so drastic a step as that.
This turn is called Pride before a Fall, or The Hood-
winked Governor.
And then there are the Comedy Judges—perfect
screams, every one of them. The plot of their act
is as follows: —Earle’s predecessor intended to ap-
point Elliot Lewis to the Chief Justiceship, in
succession to Dodds. Hut Dodds heartily dislikes
Lewis. Earle has no time for Lewis, and Dodds
knows it. So Dodds refused to resign, though he
is long past the age at which deeds of that sort are
usually done. With much tenacity the good old
man clung to his job, till the Earle Administration
transpired. Now he may be expected to resign at
at any moment. And whoever else is appointed in
his place it won’t be Lewis. (Quick Curtain, amid
shrieks of merriment.)
Whitsjtt, the legislator who had the row with
his original leader over the diseased potato issue, is
Earle’s majority. He is a host in himself, one
who may be relied on to keep any House at
which he shows in a roar so long as he is in evi-
dence. Cameron, the Tas. Federal majority of one,
in days gone by, is just such another as Whitsitt.
■Joe Cook has several times referred to him as “the
limit”; and John Forrest said of him, on
a memorable occasion: “The fellow has such
a fund of subtle humor that he makes me
larf lit to bust.” Cameron has a brother, also
a Liberal and an Independent to the backbone, whose
methods, while broader than those of his illustrious
relative, are no less artistic. This Cameron has not
yet essayed the wider fields, but as a State fun-maker
he has earned his niche beyond all possibility of
cavil, challenge, protest, egg or other missile. Need
I mention, in conclusion. King O’Malley, the Island’s
Lion Comique: the greatest Burnt Cork performer of
them all; the rival, as regards hairiness of the whisk-
ered woman of Barnum’s classic show; more ludi-
crous as regards his general appearance than many
a clown drawmg twice his salary ; a tireless coiner
of convulsing gags; a humorist who has but to open
his mouth to evoke howls of applause or derision or
something? There are others, of course, but I have
said enough I think to indicate that in the matter of
Parliamentary vaudeville Tasmania, for all its paltry
size, can put forward what is, in every sense of the
phrase, A Strong Team.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Carlyle, Jun.”: “Sealed Orders,” the big naval
bellowdrama now running at Sydney. Theatre Royal,
can do with a lot of reform on the sartorial side.
To call a super Lord Algernon de Ivnut, or what not,
and at the same time clothe him like a dustman, is
to cast a mantle of disillusion and ridicule
over the entire show. The frocks of the
walking ladies aren’t so bad, but the gar-
ments of the male mummers (barring, of
course, the principals) are horrible. An approxi-
mate expenditure of £lOO would procure for the
Society butterflies in the Flower Show scene a
collection of reasonably modern top-hats; trousers
and morning coats which fitted in parts, at least,
if not all over; and fairly credible, or believable,
or at least plausible hoots: If the hats were ironed,
the boots polished, and the Clothes carefully brushed
before each performance it would be all to the good
as regards verisimilitude. The dress-suits of the
reckless young Corinthianst*or Mohawks in the gam-
ing scene supply an element of hilarity where nothing
of the sort is required. The London young man
of fashion, who punts in thousands, may be weak,
dissolute, immoral, foolish and irreligious, but in
the matter of wearing ready-made white ties,
trousers that fail to reach the tops of his shoes, and

“Moana”: Maud Allan danced for the first time
in the Southern Hemisphere at Dunedin (M.L.) on
April 11. 1 saw her do it. No dead wowsers were
picked up after the show, though there were several
bitterly disappointed members of the cult to be dis-
cerned. Maud, who must be 45 if she’s a day, lias
one of those forms that make clothing a superfluity.
She shows her appreciation of the gifts of Providence
by leaving off as much as the Law allows. Never-
theless the result is unsensational. Maud, unlike
Gen&e —a mere toe-dancer —dances with her soul (vide
the advts.). However, the management omitted to ex-
hibit the soul, and so the audience wasn’t sure that
it got it’s money’s worth on this point. “Dancing"’
is not the correct term to apply to these exhibitions
of what the soul is alleged to think and feel when
it is forced to listen to certain pieces of music. “Pos-
turing” would be nearer the mark, Barefooted, on a
heavy, green carpet and dressed in a smile and a frag-
ment of soft-goods, Maud endeavors to present, against
appropriate backgrounds of scenery and with suitable
lighting, animated pictures interpreting the moods
inspired by various pieces of well-known classical
music, such as Mendelssohn’s “Spring Song” ; Schu-
berth “Moment Musical” ; and Grieg’s “Peer Gynt.”
These gentle caperings, consisting of the imaginary
plucking of flowers and the artistically restrained
pursuit of alleged butterflies, are all very classic,
delicate and artistic in a dim, subdued sort of fashion,
and would make anyone with a Walter Pater-Oscar
Wilde kind of soul go mad with languid joy; but
they are not exactly the type of thing that causes a
mere bloke like Bill to arise wildly in the fourth
row of the gods and let loose a frantic “Angcore!”
It is really all too chaste and beautiful for anything.
The notorious and allegedly naughty “Salome Dance”
isn’t in the present repertoire. Maud explains to
anxious pressmen who inquire after that famous sen-
sation maker that it requires special effects and a
full orchestra, while on the present tour the delicate
prancer has to rely for her music on Leo, Jan and
Mischel Oherniavsky.
Harry Lauder got a free and unpremeditated advt.
from the recent appearence of one Charles Coope, at
the South Melbourne bobby-court. Coope had come
to town from Kooweerup (Vic.) with five other spud-
diggers “to see Harry Lauder,” he pleaded. After the
show they went around sampling drinks to the tune
of a “Wee deoch and doris,” testing how far they
could go before they got stuck at the articulation of
“It’s a braw, bricht moonlicht nicht.” Their tongues
held out till the police stopped the concert, and ran
Coope into the lockup. He was fined £i, with a
seven-days’ alternative. The case was reported, where-
upon Lauder, who has not earned a reputation for
throwing away his bawbees, ’paid the spud-digger’s
penalty, gave him another ticket for the shew, and
made him otherwise happy.
The essential foolishness of the “revue” class of
performance is an unimportant objection to it from
the managerial point of view, but the bitter cry of
the galleryite who sits in ignorance of the enter-
tainment he has been paid to look upon is ,quite
another matter. Somebody voiced the recent griev-
ance in a letter to Melb. Age, and the wonder was
that the same protest hadn’t been lodged earlier.
“Performers who are stationed among the audience
to join in the ‘business’ of those upon the stage,
and performers who leave the stage to perambulate
in the front portion of the auditorium, disappear
from the view of practically every person seated in
the gallery. The natural consequence is that people
in the front seats stand up, with the result that
those behind them can neither see nor hear any-
thing of the performance, owing to the rustling of
the rising galleryites and the angry calls to ‘Sit
dow'n in front!’ of those whose view of the stage
is wholly obscured. I visited Her Majesty’s Theatre
last night to see ‘Come Over Here,’ and obtained a
2s. seat in the gallery five rows from the front. I
estimate that I saw and heard less than one-half of
the show, for the principal performers were doing
a sort of Tango Tea dress parade down the centre of
the stalls half the time.” As much might be said
by the people who pay more than 2s. for seats at
the back of the family circle. Also the d.c. people
miss some of the cheap altercation and expensive
costume parading which is arranged for the enter-
tainment of the front stalls. In short, the “speci-
ally attractive” features of a Tango Tea or revue
performance are missed by a large ’number of people
who have paid to see the whole of it. The manage-
ment may plead that the non-seers don’t miss much,
but the same excuse would applv to the outside
public that pays nothing for not being present. All
things considered, it is safe to say that shows which
have a rectangular plank in their platform, and
require bogus members of the audience to help them
along, are not likely to become acclimatised here-
abouts. Thev will pass away and be remembered
onlv as nightmares.
The portrait of Ladv Macbeth which Ellen Terry
(at managerial suggestion) brought with her to Aus-
tralia is Sargent’s original sketch in black and
white of the work he had in view. Subsequentlv
he changed his idea and painted the picture which
an ardent Terrvite bought for presentation to the
Tate Gallerv, in London. Ellen Terry’s art trea-
sures include a valuable lot of pictures, it goes
without saving. The finest of them is G. F. Watts’s
portrait of her, painted when she was a young girl,
and he the staid man who meant to marry her. Long
years after she had ceased to be Mrs. Watts the
old man gave her that love-inspired work of art.
She has willed it to the British public, and pre-
sumably it will be hung in the National Gallery when
she goes where Watts has gone.
“Screen”: At the recent general meeting of the
Amalgamated Pictures Co., in Melbourne, the process
of fusion with other concerns was explained. In
January, 1912, the first negotiations were started. It
must be mentioned that the Amalgamated Pictures
Co. is not the name of that fusion, but of one of the
concerns which entered into it. The first combine
was of West’s, Spencer’s and the Amalgamated, under
the title of General Film Co. The Williams Com-
pany, named the Union Theatres, then joined the
General Film. The Williams Company was to re-
ceive 321 per cent, of takings. The others divided
the rest, 67-J per cent. The Amalgamated Pictures
Co. is the smallest in the concern, getting 15 per
cent. Yet it is a large business, owning the Mel-
bourne Majestic Theatre and St. Kilda Theatre, and
sharing in subsidiary companies. Its whole dividend,
in the past two years, has only been 5 per cent.
“Norbell”: Rockhampton (Queensland) possesses a
dramatic show which has been running successfully for
upwards of six months. It is owned, run, financed,
managed and sub-edited by a syndicate of three local
publicans. Two changes of bill are presented every
week, which is pretty strenuous going, and keeps the
actors and actorines, to say nothing of the prompter,
moderately busy. Also, the management perspires
blood at the job o f finding a new, fresh-off-the-ice-
bellowdrama every three nights. A recent attraction
at the Palais de Shriek was the plentiful temper-
ance outpouring, “Ten Nights in a Bar Room.” It
was a strange item for three publicans to put on, but
thev did it. The monoline man on the morning news-
paper made it read “Ten Knights in a Bar Room.”
But he probably wouldn’t have minded if it had been
10 dukes in a soda-water factor}'.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
A vicar who has been travelling through
the Holy Land (every religious tradesman
has to have a look at the Holy Land once in
a while, just as the owner of a house has to
crawl underneath with a candle to make
sure the white ants haven’t eaten the foun-
dations) has brought back the depressing
news that Jerusalem isn’t likely to get the
electric trams with which it was threatened.
According to the traveller, David-street is
the only thoroughfare that could swallow a
tram ; but as there are only 300yds. of it,
the vehicle would just be able to run up and
down like a rat in a trap, or a mad cow in
a small yard. Even the Holman Government
might have doubts about the wisdom of con-
structing a line in such a locality with loan
money. In the event of a shortage of cash,
the conductor would hesitate to as"k (he free-
born citizen of Jerusalem for the ex Ira penny
for fear the free-born should thr ttle him
with his turban and advise all his friends to
walk. As a matter of fact, there are no
very reliable street names in Jerusalem.
The Cook’s Tourist Bureau people call their
street —which is nearest to the gate that is
nearest to the railway station at which the
train lands you when you come from Joppa
and Jonah’s whale, aud is also handiest to
the European hotels—David-street on spec.
It looks something like a street, and it looks
as if it might be David. By way of a jest
they call the highway behind Solomon-street.
You can only get through it sideways, and
there is no name on the corner, and no
native thereabouts ever heard of Solomon-
street. But Cook’s people have to amuse
the traveller somehow, and this is one of the
devices they employ.
The abolition of the half-crown in Aus-
tralia has some justification behind it. The
coin is an awkward one, being one-eighth of
a pound, whereas any sensible bit of money
ought to be one-tenth of something else, or
10 times something else. And the half-crown
appears, for some reason or another, to have
a demoralising influence. The dubious trader
has a curious habit of urging you to send
along just that amount. He charges
it for his big bottle of patent medicine. He
prays for 2s. 6d. at all times and seasons.
The Scripture says that a virtuous woman
is a crown —that is to say, five bob—to her
husband, but half that quantity of money is
moral wreckage. A recent Sydney sensation
was concerned with a firm that advertised an
offer of 10yds. of silk for 2s. 6d. A still more
recent annoyance is about a Sydney firm
whose advertisement was taken to mean that
it supplied boots for 2s. 6d. It didn’t supply
boots for 2s. 6d., and that was the reason
why there arose anger, ill-feeling, hostility,
unpleasantness, battle, skirmishes, lying-in-
wait, bitterness, recrimination, objurgation
and similar breaches of the Sabbath calm.
If the very mention of 2s. Gd. was made a
felony the standard of commercial morality
might rise considerably.
The tale of “Singemoine”:—
The Caillaux tragedy is one more tribute to the
rottenness of France’s libel law. There is even more
newspaper license in France than there is in the
U.S. It is almost impossible under the Code Napo-
leon for counsel to secure a conviction against the
most envenomed assailant of a citizen’s personal or
political honor. As to public characters outside
France, they have to submit to the most appalling
attacks on their morality, and so forth. The
boulvardier hears far more about' the secret habits
of prominent Britishers than the Londoner does ; and
criminal charges of an unspeakable nature are con-
stantly being levelled by the satirical press at famous
Germans. It was a Baris weekly that began the
campaign against “the Kaiser’s set” half a dozen
years ago—a campaign in which Harden, the famous
Berlin writer and editor joined, and which ended
in a number of lady-like princes, generals, etc., re-
tiring permanently from Berlin Society.
The unwritten law is the product always either of
bad law or of bad administration. In the United
States a rich man may commit almost any crime and
yet .evade the lethal chair. One effect of this is that
when a more or less affluent criminal does something
more than usually exasperating, he stands to have a
hole drilled in him. And so, in another sense, it is
in France. The Caillauxs could never have hoped to
get a verdict against Calmette, though he was saying
much and hinting more against both of them. They
had both been loose livers, and though a French
crowd is diverted at immorality in the papers, a
French jury takes precisely the same view in such
matters as the stodgiest of British juries. Further-
more, Caillaux would have had ranged against him,
in any such event, the whole Royalist and Catholic
clement —which latter is still enormously strong, and
more so since the convents and monasteries were
wiped out. Caillaux conducted the unpleasant busi-
ness of sequestrating the property of the religious
orders, and the clericals regard him as anti-Christ or
worse. His downfall will give the Church more un-
adulterated joy than it has known in quarter of a
century.
Calmette is the second Parisian newspaper man to
fall before an assassin’s bullet in recent times. The
other case was when a Woman drifted in to destroy
Millerand, of La Lanterne, and in mistake for him
shot, in all good faith, an inoffensive sub-editor.

the result is that, outside the walls of an

Lauder’s salary and prestige, be my brother’s

Suggestive advt. from Adelaide Adver-
tiseh (21/4/T4) :
VOUNG Girl, clean small office, twice week; no
toff or wowser.—“Sweetheart,” this office.
To the plain, matter-fact intellect of the
ordinary cynical person of the world it looks
as though the heavily respectable ’Tiser were
aiding somebody to acquire a White Slave.
A Wowser can clean a small office just as
well as a young woman who has the joy of
life fully developed; and the aloofest variety
of “tuff” is as satisfactory at this job as a
flagrant donah or female knut. Office-clean-
ing is, in brief, one of those activities into
which the personal element ought not to
enter. There is no more demand, legiti-
mately speaking, for a gay young office-
cleaner than there is for a blithe, care-free
young dustman. Also, there is an atmo-
sphere of profound suspicion hovering round
the would-be employer of young girls who
yearns to be known as “Sweetheart.”
Some people have less luck than others, and
some people have more, and some people get
the tired feeling. Also many people are dead.
A young couple in Melbourne, acting through
their solicitor or available next of kin, they
being laid up and unable to go out and do
business in blankets and an ambulance, ap-
plied last week for extension on two first-class
return tickets to Adelaide. The reason was
simply but eloquently set forth. They had
come from S. Australia on their honeymoon.
On the second day they had been taken ill
in their hotel simultaneously, and betrayed
similar symptoms. They made concentrated
progress from bad to worse, and the race
was a dead heat. On the fifth day both were
operated on for appendicitis. Their severed
appendices were buried together. Now the
loving couple are mooning ( sans honey) in
the same hospital, waiting for their patches
to heal, and slowly recovering from stitch
in the side. And they want an extension of
their return tickets so that they may go back
to Adelaide together, leaving certain lost
innards behind them.

“Moana’s” tale :
In Maoriland the Government gives a generous sub-
sidy of 245. in the £ (out of Uncle Cohen’s loan
cash, presumably) on any gift made by any individual
for charitable purposes. Freewill gifts of size are
few and far between, and it is not often that one
runs over four figures, so the strain on the public
cashbox isn’t usually, serious. But when an extra
geneious citizen turns up, like ore Cawthorn, of
Nelson, who recently threw his cheque for £15,000 at
the Nelson Hospital Board, and the Government is
asked to hand out the usual subsidy to the extent of
£lB,OOO, and a harassed Minister gazes in the official
till and notes an absence of loan money, the situa-
tion is embarrassing. The first thing the Govern-
ment did when the sudden request hit it was to faint.
When it recovered it protested that it really in-
tended to nay some day, and said it didn’t anticipate
that the Nelson Hospital would want the cash all at
once. The Board said it wanted the money all right,
whereupon the Government fainted some more, end
on recovering consciousness offered to pay the amount
.in instalments ! When the Board innocently inquired
if the Government would pay interest on the unpaid
amount the Government fell down dead. It was still
dead when the mail closed.
HER SCREAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The girls covered their faces and screamed, and
the burglar at once made off. —Daily paper.
It isn’t safe to trust her with a gun,
And she couldn’t throw a stone to save her life ;
But, though helpless, she can vanquish anyone
With the scream that rings above the loudest strife.
From the backyard, from the mansion, from the park.
Lo! it brings men filled with fury to her aid,
When it trails a flame of terror ’cross the dark.
Till she’s lost her voice, she need not be afraid.
Oh, pity him she screams at 1 Like a hare,
See! He doubles and he dodges in a dream 1
Then upon his back he feels the baleful stare
Of wild legions. How that shrill and fearful scream
Keeps ringing in his hot and buzzing head,
While his sobbing breath comes scalding up . his
throat,
And he toils to get away on feet of lead
To some sanctuary—quiet, cool, remote.
Man, the deep-voiced fighter, trembles at the shriek
As he flies to dodge the wrath that answers squeals.
With the Sweat of terror trickling down his cheek,
See, the dust-puffs rise beneath his guilty heels 1
Neither gun, revolver, baton, fist or boot
Will deter him ! But, dear lady, how he streaks
Like a cur, and leaves behind his pile of loot
When he’s welcomed with a few full-volumed
shrieks !

“0.1.”: Some old stone structures tire
being demolished in Elizabetli-street, near
King-street, Sydney. They in. old times
housed members of the Josephson family,
and in the big room on the top of one an
ancient Turveydrop of dancing, John Clark,
had his assembly rooms. On the Corner
adjoining, Richard Driver had the Three
Tuns Tavern, which was originally the home
of the legal Nortons. There was a time of
rum and madness and onions, when every
second house in Sydney had a liquor license,
and the one between sold beer surrepti-
tiously, and there was a partially concealed
wine emporium in the cellar, and a brandy
“joint” in the backyard. That is why it is
so difficult to write even the scrappiest kind
of old reminiscence without a tavern crawl-
ing into it.

The Old Soldier: —
A new type of conscientious objector to military
drill turned up at a Sydney police-court the other
day. It wasn’t the unwilling- warrior himself, but
his father. The latter, it was explained to the Bench,
was so rabid that he had told his son that “if he
went to drill he would be driven out of the house.”
The Bench, instead of insisting on the presence of
the eccentric parent and extracting explanations and
promises of reform from him, agreed to a compro-
mise suggested .by the prosecuting area-officer. The
latter said that “He could find work of a non-
combative character for the boy—he could be set to
clerical work.” As a prophylactic against malinger-
ing, under the guise of “conscientious” detestation of
drill, it would have been better to set the lad to
sweep and scrub out the orderly-room, or office, or
whatever they call it, while the others were at drill.
Also, his sort might be given other jobs. For
example, Sir lan Hamilton, in his “Staff-officer's
Scrap Book,” tells what one of his staff saw in Corea
at the beginning of the war with Russia. “On the
road he passed thousands of the two-wheeled hand-
carts he had just seen larded from the ships. They
were each pulled by three straining little men in
the uniform of the Guards’ infantry. These were the
military coolies, the rejections for height, and for
height only, from the year’s conscription.” Why should
not the Commonwealth provide each of its cadet
battalions with a certain number of handcarts to carry
anything, in the way of baggage that might be re-
quired on a whole-day parade, or a week-end camp,
and to be dragged by the conscientious objectors?
It would diminisn the army of objectors in an aston-
ishing fashion.
* *- * *
The judgment in (lie Locker v. Locker
suit, as reported in the M.L. papers, pro-
vided the world with a speaking likeness of
a certain sort of dairy farmer. The Bench
said :
The following are some of the facts proved, which,
in my opinion, amount to persistent cruelty—namely,
requiring his wife to milk 20 or 30 cows morning
and evening up to the date of the wife being con-
fined ; requiring her to carry buckets of milk when
pregnant, and to commence milking very soon after
the birth of her children ; in addition to housework,
feeding calves, cutting firewood at times; in not
supplying her with pocket money, or in lieu thereof
sufficient clothes, and defendant admits he never
bought her a dress during the five years they lived
together, nor gave her more than £l. It is proved
that ho is, and was for a considerable time, a man
of considerable means ; his smallest income from his
cows must have been well over £5OO a year net, in
addition to having some thousands of pounds of cash
invested, and also in addition to owning land. It is
proved that the wife worked from the early hours of
the morning until well into the evening at milking
and household duties, while she had also to look
after two small children, taking them in a basket
to the cowshed whilst milking.
Despite the mitigating circumstance that this
sturdy busliland toiler never, so far as the
evidence showed, attempted either by force,
threat or persuasion to get anything in the
shape of dirt cheap hard labor out of either
of his basketed offspring, the magistrate
turned upon him the eye of disfavor, and
made an order against him, and spoke harshly
about him, and in other ways heaped con-
tumely on Private Enterprise.

Adelaide is having some of the most ex-
pensive law on record. The long-pending
commercial dispute of Teesdale Smith (con-
tractor) with the Tramways Trust has come
on for hearing at last, and sees the unusual

John Hopdom, in Sydney, got properly
scared out of its No. 16’s last week by a
bright youth named Moloney, who had been
arrested on two charges, one for bashing a
fellow citizen, the other for stealing a box of
butter in company. The first case was de-
cided by the Bench awarding Moloney six
months hard labor. Later on, he and his
alleged accomplice stood before the Court
charged with larceny. They were found not
guilty of butter. The accomnlice proudly
bowed to the beak, the police and reporters,
and marched into the street a free though
maligned man. After him pranced his fellow
acquitee, Moloney, through the serried ranks
of the cops. Half an hour later someone
remembered Moloney’s six months stretch
for assault, and a hurried search of the
adjacent scenery failed to find him.
The Oldest Inhabitant sends along a few
more pubs:—
The site of the new theatre, in very close
proximity to St. Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney,
belonged to the estate of the ancient head of the
Insurance Fire Brigade, I’. ,1. Bown, who built upon
it the Engine House. On the corner half a century
ago was the Kangaroo Inn, run by one of the Yeend
family. Diagonally on the Bathurst-street corner
was the old Emu Inn, a relic of the ’3o’s, and first
opened by one Aarons, whose son became a wealthy
squatter at Wellington (N,S.W.). They sought not
after English names for their pubs in those days.
The Australian brewery occupied one corner, the
Emu another, the Kangaroo the third, and St.
Andrew’s Cathedral the fourth, the name of which
religious distillery was in accord with the wish of
Governor Macquarie, who laid the first stone.
The wife-deserter who bolts long before
he has had a chance to even begin ro
plumb the real horrors of matrimony ap-
pears to be on the increase. Thus a lady
who lately asked Vic. Judge Hood for a
decree nisi explained that her affliction had
left for Sydney, one week after her mar-
riage in ’OB, and had never returned. In the
next case the gentleman had fled in ’OO,
after three weeks’ bliss, to “do some fencing
at Taggerty,” wherever that absurd place
is, and from then onwards the Unknown
swallowed him up, and Mvstery claimed him
for her own. In a suit heard in Sydney
last week the respondent, an actor, went
one—if not tw r o or more—better than either
of the above. He abandoned his beloved
on the steps of a coffee-palace half an hour
after the nuptial ceremony, and proceeded
to Europe or Bourke or somewhere, “to have
his voice trained.” Xhe way of the man
who goes forth to do a job and disappears
from the yearning gaze of his wife for ever
is full of possibilities, and the end of it no
thinker ca'ri v see—or the middle of it either,
for that matter. All that the closest ob-
server can say definitely is that this variety
of casual citizen is on the increase; and he
will add a rider probably to the effect that
he is a blighter and ought to be discour-
aged.
“Croson” complains—
Sundry Melbourne girls have of late developed an
untidy habit of getting- accidentally locked in the
business places at which they toil. They omit to
leave at closing time, and the caretaker disremem-
bers them,, and the night watchman sinks into the
dreamless stupor of his kind, and they are left alone in
a large, silent building, with the darkness, the rats
and stray ghosts for company. Then they rush to
the telephone, if there is one. and it isn't in a
locked office, and urgently beseech the police and
the hre-brigade to haste to the rescue, and bring
ladders and ropes and fire-escapes and so forth. If
they can’t get to the ’phone they climb to a second
or third-storey window and make distressful noises
until the attention of the multitude is attracted
One such, a typiste, transpired the other evening.
She fled distractedly to the ’phone, rang up her
parents and told them of her danger, and then spoke
agitatedly with the Central. Police station. The
Law sent down a vast, bewildered John, who viewed
the beleaguered damsel ’where she moaned and
gesticulated at a high window, and suggested she
should leap into his arms. She' declined, and so he
borrowed a ladder, and asked her to descend by that
Again she declined. Finally, after a couple of
strangers had concentrated their intellects on the
problem of releasing the girl, the Law climbed up
once more, went inside and unscrewed the lock-
socket off the back door, and passed the damsel out, and
screwed the socket on again and returned the way it
had come. It was a tremendous bother over one
small, absent-minded tvpiste, but the causing of
bother, blasphemy and the like is the province of a
certain sort of mild, shrinking female.
I 1 rom the Household Hints’’ department
rvr T th ? IIUTT AND PETONE ClIKONICLE
(M.E.) :—
In eating eggs, if a whisk is not handy, use three
forks instead of one. They are just as easy to hold
as one, and the eggs ean be eaten in about a third
of the time.
A useful tip in these days of wild rush and
scurry and stand-up meals.
THE GHOSTLY CO-RESPONDENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
]t was deposed in the course of a recent Mel-
bourne divorce case that respondent, a spiritualist,
had refused to cohabit with her husband because
she had contracted an affection for an affinity in the
spirit world.
The case was John Dance versus Dance,
With mention of another.
It showed with plenteous circumstance
Dow Jane, a wife and mother,
Ilad thrown conjugal duties o'er,
And said, with marked decision,
She’d live with Mister Dance no more;
As she’d conceived affection for
A most attractive vision.
This fact set up a legal din—
The co-respondent was a Jinn!
The Judge across his volumes bent
(No lawyer was correeter).
The case was minus precedent.
How could he cite a spectre?
The stricken husband, sad and white,
Told how from out his portal
His guilty wife had stolen at night
To smoodge with one who wasn't quite
An ordinary mortal.
How she, despising his rebukes,
Was going gay with gnomes and spooks.
“I’ll have no earthly child,” said she
To Dance, her lord and master.
Her conduct with the dim co-re.
Meanwhile was growing faster.
The lady offered no defence
For co-respondent Pixy,
There had been no appearance, hence
His Honor's final eloquence
I.eft hubby in a fix. He
Gave damages that would atone—
Five thousand ’gainst a ghost unknown!

NEVILL,

Aide.
Died last week in Melbourne parson A. It.
Edgar, a shining light of local Methodism
who radiated from the Wesley Church and
its “Central Mission.” Rev. Edgar had a
good deal of the true fanatic’s energy and
power of self-deception. On his deathbed he
was capable of writing, in a message to his
flock:—
I love the people as God loves me. My heart is
full, thank God ! of Christ, and longs its message to
declare. .. .1 mount, I fly! What a joyful uplift!
Which might almost persuade a hardened
sinner to be a Methodist had not the vain-
glorious shepherd shown himself as an un-
conscious humbug in two or three notable
instances. Spiritually, (and physically, no
doubt) Edgar lost force a few years ago
through the exposure of the fact that he had
unduly influenced a dying sheep in the dis-'
posal of his property. Somebody’s last will
and testament, prepared under Edgar’s pious
direction, was challenged in the Court of
Probate, and .the Court set the document:
aside. Edgar, or his family, would have
secured a nice fat legacy by this unduly-
influenced will, but it appeared in evidence
that Edgar had professed to ,be quite dis-
THIS WORLD IS FULL OF STEPS. .
What they proposed to do to deal with the Beef
Trust was not to amend the Constitution. They pro-
posed to act as a sensible Government. They would,
take other steps.— W. H. Irvine.
IRVINE: “Oh, dear, no! That's far too-

interested in the matter. In short, the Wes-
leyan parson, when taken by surprise and
cornered, was exactly the usual sort of pre-
varicator, not to say liar. He had all the
natural untruthfulness of a rabid apostle of
Terewth, and he came out of the law case
vyith his halo hanging over one ear. Of
course he did a lot of “good” of the sort:
whicli a hard-working Salv’army major does.
It was his business to be an evangelist, and
for years his natural vitality and ambition,
kept him well supplied with business energy
and push. Finally—previous to the disputed
will case—parson Edgar got so struck on
himself that he decided to make a series of
appearances in the character of Christ up-to-
date. hie notified the newspapers that he
would stroll along Melbourne’s busiest,
thoroughfares every Saturday night preach-
ing the Word as he proceeded, in order to
give careless pleasure-seekers a chance to get
saved at the roadside. Rev. Edgar didn’t-
candidly state that he expected the Saturday
nigliters to drop their beers and follow him
right a,way, but his ambitious suggestion
could be read between the lines of his an-
nouncement. Edgar and his escort of
apostles peregrinated on one Saturday night
only, as far as the writer remembers. The
performance was a farce. A tall,,
stout man, wearing his collar and vest but-
toned at the back, and crying the blessed
Word from the Bourke-street tram-track, in
emulation of the Lord in old Jerusalem, was
nracticall.v inviting ridiculous comparison.
The crowd for the most part neither sneered
nor jeered. People merely stared at the
Wesleyan parson who could march round
town, unaccompanied by a drummer, expect-
ing to make converts by the way. The
failure of Rev. Edgar’s attempt to adapt
ancient methods of' soul-saving to modern
conditions was utter and complete. Mel-
bourne has forgotten that it ever happened.
The veteran lawyer, George Downer, who
has long been too ill to take his place as
chairman of directors of the Bank of Ade-
laide, has at last resigned. He had had 14
years of it, and had been a director for 25
years.
“Moana”: T. H. Whitson, the secretary
of the Union Steamship Co. at Dunedin
(M.L.), retires this month after spending 35
years of his life with the concern. lie joined
the service at Dunedin away back in" 1879
as chief correspondent, and a few years
later was pushed into the secretary’s chair,
where he has remained ever since. His energy
and ability have had a lot to do with the
success that has attended the big M.L. con-
cern. If he wanted to he could get drunk
on the nod in Christchurch,Wellington,
Auckland. Sydney and Fiji, afld occasionally
in Dunedin, where the\ inhabitants work
between whuskies.
John Proctor Gray has gone to join the
loam, at the age of 73. He made soap, was a
Freetrader with a leaning to Protection for
soap; and was, for a short time, a Senator
for N.S.W. in the soap interest.
Henry Holland, the widely-advertised
N.S.W. citizen who ordered his son to take
off his ill-fitting cadet uniform and to abstain
from defending his country, has had experi-
ences in Maoriland. He was in the running
for the billet of Mayor at Wellington when
the law rushed at him with a charge of “sedi-
tion” in the matter of the recent waterside
strike, and said that the bill would be 12
months in gaol.
Passed over suddenly at Christchurch
(M.L.), March 31, D. D. Macfarlane, one of
the Shepherd Kings of Canterbury. He
settled in the pledged isles in the early days,
and took up a great tract of country in
North Canterbury. Some years ago he cut
up a big portion of his well-known Lyndon
estate, and settled 26 families there on easy
terms. That was a practical way of advanc-
ing his closer settlement theories. At the
present time over 130 persons are being
supported by that part of the Lyndon estate,
and they are making it blossom like the beer-
nose.
That much-dangled fruit, the South Aus-
tralian Agent-Generalship, has dropped at
last into a mouth which the many would-be
prophets had overlooked. Fred Young is a
lawyer, and country bred, and in the Peake
Government he has made an unexpected
success as Commissioner- of Crown Lands.
It was he who announced in the Assembly
that someone had written to his firm asking
for a certain Crown lease, and offering a
big sum as “costs” : to which he had caused
a. reply to be sent that things were not done
that way in S.A., and that neither his firm
nor any other could do more than put in a
routine application, at a trifling fee. Young
is only 38, and, as his wife is a daughter
of the immensely wealthy John Darling,
lately deceased, they should be able to keep
Australia’s end up socially in London.
“Jeames” writes from the servants’ hall:—
A cable message (25/4/T4) mentions that Earl de
la Warr has been divorced for the second time. His
stirring career has a certain interest to Australians,
inasmuch as he is a first cousin of Lamington, for-
merly of Queensland ; a son-in-law (or ex-son-in-law)
•of that Brassey, of Victoria, who fell off things with
such persistency ; and a brother of Lady Edeline Strick-
land, of N. S. Wales. In addition, he has a claim
to notice which is cosmopolitan, by reason of the fine,
fruity vigor of his methods. He shares with Earl
Cowley the distinction of being the onlv member of
the King’s Club —the Marlborough—who has been
divorced twice. And he surpasses Cowley’s record in
at least one respect, inasmuch as lie has been made
bankrupt; while his fellow peer still remains sol-
vent. His insolvency arose, in part, from an heroic
but misguided effort to boom Bexhill-on-Sea—an Eng-
lish watering-place that has never been more than
fairly popular. He meant to make a Manly Beach of
it, and it refused to become anything more than a
Cronulla. His mother married en secondes noces, a
country parson of retiring habits, and the twain are
understood to watch the spectacular efforts of their
relative with the stunned interest that a Methody
elder evinces in the early stages of his first visit to
a London night-club.

The Restricted Alien.
Miss Sophia Arndell, a connecting link
with N.S.W.’s First Fleet, passed out on
April 19, at Dulwich Hill, Sydney. She
was second daughter of the late Thomas Arn-
dell, of Caddie. Pitt Town, Hawkesbury. The
original Arndell was assistant-surgeon to the
“settlement,” and camped on the llawkes-
bury when he retired from his various* official
troubles. His just-deceased descendant was
aged 80 when she handed back her skift to
the Creator.

another problem Wotild have been solved.

“Public Servant’s” confidential teina'rks :
The selection of F. A. COghlan is Auditor-Genfetal
for N.S.W. surprised the Service, despite its famili-
arity with such shocks. V'errfon, the Cetili rig Auditor-
General, was generally regarded is Att ©fftcial of the
rubber-stamp gender. His subordinates have usually
annexed the credit or something of the scathing
criticisms ©f Labor ifinAhV© contained in the Auditor-
General’s annual reports. The candid remarks therein
contained put out of court any chances that the
higher 'officers in the Audit Department had of pro-
motion. Why F. A. Coghlan went from the Chief Secre-
tary’s office to the Auditor-Generalship isn’t exactly
obvious, though there are a dozen ingenious theories.
Ho doesn’t get promotion, or more power, or patron-
age, but he gets away from Ministerial interference.
The new Auditor-General has not had any technical
training for the billet. He is not a figure man nor
accountant, let alone auditor, but is, all the same,
probably quite equal to the somewhat vague require-
ments of an Auditor-General.
Dr. Golding Bird, some time Dean of
Newcastle, has got what comes some time
to all astute and hefty self-advertisers in the
soul-salving profession, to wit, a bishopric.
You could no more overlook the existence
of Rev. Bird,' when he was in Newcastle,
than you could miss the activities of a
steam-siren. If he wasn’t dealing out re-
buke to the local unions he was springing
vigorously on the masters ; and if he didn’t
draw a full church one Sunday with a dis-
course on some Biblical topic he roped in the
plate-fillers seven days later with the inti-
mation that he was minded to speak on The
Relation of the Picture Show to the Cost
of Living, or The Empty Marriage-Bed or
some such sizzling problem. And if the
newspapers didn’t give him a good advt. —
well, the Dean wasn’t to blame. He is a
living example of the fact that though the
meek inherit the Earth it is the pushful
that inherit the Sees.
“Chasuble” writes:—
Exit Pastor Edgar, the resurrector of Melbourne’s
Wesley Church, and the most distinguished Victorian
Methodist. In 1857 the denomination had a church
in the Collins and Queen streets corner. It sold out
to the Bank of Australasia for £40,000. This
money bulked .most into the entirely new Wesley
Church (Lonsdale-street) and the remainder into
the new churches of Fitzroy, North Melbourne and
Prahran, three familiar edifices of the same design,
by Crouch and Wilson. Excellent churches in work-
manship, but none can be termed successful other-
wise. Wesley Church, opened 1859, was a frightful
white elephant, or rather blue one. It kept steadily
going down in the theological mire till Edgar came
along in 1893. The attendances were then something
like what poor capillary Bevan, admirable preacher
as he was, came to in the magnificent Collins-street
Independent Church. Edgar proposed new stock,
lock and barrel. The Melbourne Tory Wesleyans
were fiercely in opposition, and made a desperate
attempt to shunt Edgar to Maoriland. However, he
triumphed. His Pleasant Sundays Afternoons formed
the backbone, with a grand orchestra, and his
speakers have included almost every man of civil
distinction visiting Melbourne.
BENJAMIN BRIMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Benjamin Brims, a commercial traveller, has been
remanded on a charge of attempting to blackmail the
lady described as Princess Josephine of Thurn and
Taxis. —Uninteresting cable in the daily press.
If I had a name such as Benjamin owns
I’d never add figures, I’d never break stones
For a living—no fear!
A moniker round and sonorous as that
Is as good as a peerage to keep a man fat
And replenished with beer.
I reckon I’d gratify all of my whims
If my parents had christened me Benjamin Brims.
I once knew a ehap called Cadwallader Cobb ;
He squinted ; he wasn’t much good at his job ;
And his wife used to drink.
Each one of these woes was a handicap black,
But nothing could hold old Cadwallader back;
I honestly think
That a man’s like a story; he’ll win in his game
Provided he has a remarkable name.
Suppose you’re in business. A customer comes;
He hems and he haws, and he twiddles his thumbs,
And the deal doesn’t go;
You write to your client; “Just run into town,
And talk the thing over with our Mr. Brown?”
Do you get him? Oh, no!
But you’d snare him a treat if you said, “I would
like
To refer you to our Mr. Paradine Pyke.”
As Amaranth Ambler, or Gallipot Glubb,
A chap should contrive to get tick at a pub
On the strength of his name ;
To be blest with a name such as Emerson Eames
Should bring a man near to accomplishing dreams
Of unspeakable fame.
I envy the titles some fellows have got,
But Benjamin Brims is the best of the lot.
Imagine the stir through a party that swims,
When a waiter sings out, “Mr. Benjamin Brims!”
The people will turn
To whisper and whistle and chatter and look—
And how would it shine on the back of a book !
I’m sure I would spurn
The profits of blackmail as wages of shame
If Benjamin Brims were my baptismal name.
N.S.W.

If ever the A.M.P. is looking' round for someone to
whom it can make» a substantial gift of any of its
surplus cash, it might consider the Mutual Life and
Citizens. A few .fears baok the A.M.P. had the
whole field to itself: it got'fat and lazy and scarcely
moved. Business came to it automatically, and the
society merely took things as they came; in fact, it
didn’t care whether they came or not. Then the
M.L.C. be<ran to look up, and it started out in pur-
suit of the A.M.P. and incidentally in pursuit of
business. So the A.M.P. either had to, bustle or
take a back seat. The table shows that it preferred
to bustle. Since 1906 the amounts added to its
funds have been growing every year. For the five
„ to 1905 the average amount added was
£891,390. From 1906 to 1913 the average was
£1,21 8 ,708. Total income has also kent moving
ahead, and naturally expenses haven’t been stationary.
However, in comparison to the total income they are
being gradually compressed. This will show (o
what extent:—

No doubt the new Stamp Duty in N.S.W. will cause
the expenses to expand considerably next year. The
expenses at present are composed of management
exes., £212,059 ; commission, £94,816; taxes,
£36,305; and Officers’ Provident Fund contribution.
£2500. Of the taxes N.S.W. called for only £25
'(stamp duty), and supplied 80,735 policies. ' Maori-
land grabbed no less than £16,795 as taxes, almost
half the total—although the taxes are divided among
the six States, the Commonwealth, Maoriland and
London. Yet the Hungry Islands only provide about
one-sixth of the business. Unfortunately there’s no hint
as to what proportion of the exes. Maoriland calls for.
However, taken all round, the past year’s expense
rate is, with the exception of 1909 and 1910, when
the rates were abnormally low, the best for 10 years.
No exact estimate can be made of what the year’s
new business cost the co. The usual method is to
allow 7 p.c. on the renewal premiums and 3 p.c. on
the consideration for annuities as a fair cost for run-
ning the old business, and reckon everything in
excess of that as being spent in the hunt for new
business. This table shows how last year’s rate com-
pared with those that have gone before. —

Though the rate is slightly above that for the pre-
vious year it is a good way below the average, and
it leaves a fair margin either to be dropped back
into funds or to help to swell the bonus to policy-
hplders.
For this year the bonus is at the rate of 39.1 p.c ,
which is the highest since the boom years of 1888-
1893. But big bonuses may mean various other
things, and chief among them that the policyholders
are paying the co. too much in the shape of pre-
miums. Any insurance co. can, by the simple process
of charging the policy-holders twice as much as they
should, hand out very gaudy bonuses. But there is
no suggestion that the A.M.P. is guilty of these prac-
tices. There are, however, sure signs that its profit
comes from other sources. The actuary assumes
that the interest earned by the co.’s funds will be
from 3 p.c. to 3J p.c., and he bases his premiums on
that estimation. This table shows what the earn-
ings really were:—•

Another very simple method of increasing the
bonus is to invest the company’s funds so that they
will bring rn a high return, although the security
is risky. The A.M.P. chose the lower rate and the
strong security. Here is a comparison of how the

So each of the items is moving in a way which tends
to strengthen the security of the A.M.P. policy-
holders’ funds. Public securities—which, having the
whole of the taxable wealth of the country behind
them, are nearest to perfect safety—show a sub-
stantial rise. Loans on policies have, no doubt on
account of the country’s general prosperity, shown a
welcome decrease, and The Bulletin would like to
see it go still further.

The capital is now £2,000,000 —an extra £400,000
having been got in during the year at a premium
of no less than £600,000. Even then, although the
new capital can’t have been in use for the whole of
the six months, the profit is sufficient, after allowing
the usual £20,000 to be written off, to pay a 17
p.c. dividend and still have £30,000 odd to go to
reserves. It is simply a case of “unto those that
have shall be given.’’ The A’sia’s head office is in
England, and the accounts have to go to London and
come back again before Australia, which supplies
practically all the profits, gets a glance at them. > So
they are ancient history when they arrive here. The
antiquated directors still refer to Australia as one of
“the colonies” (they actually haven’t heard of Federa-
tion. and don’t know that the Commonwealth is made
up of six States, and they probably don’t know that
South Australia was not a “colony” at any time).
Anyhow, this bank, with, its head office in' the old
Teutonic colony of England, had the good fortune to
come through the bank smash marvellously well, and
beyond having to reduce the dividend on several occa-
sions (though never to less than 5 p.c.) it showed no
outward signs of injury. The co. has only missed
dividends for three years-—somewhere between 1840
and 1850, but since then it has paid up regularly.
Since the bank was founded in 1834 no less than
£10,744.750 has been paid to shareholders out of a
total profit of £13,600,854. Thus the English share-
holders have done very well out of “the colonies.”
When a bank can show a record like that, people
don’t need to be urged to trust it. It can pick and
choose its business, and what it doesn’t like it can
pass on to others. Consequently it has only the best
of securities. For the six months under review the
profits were at the somewhat extraordinary rate of
£lO 16s. 2d p.c. on capital (increased) and reserves
combined. Under ordinary circumstances such a rate
would be regarded with suspicion, and the securities
questioned. But the A’sia has large inner reserves,
which of course, earn interest though they are not
disclosed in the accounts. This comparison will give
some idea of how the business of the co. has moved
in 12 months:—
Oct., Oct.,
1912. 1913.
Notes .. .. £184,056 £168,818 Dec. £15,238
Bills, etc. . . 2,722,182 2,768,848 Inc. 46,666
Deposits .. 17,906,493 18,088,955 Inc. 182,462
Liquid assets 7,321,274 8,349.112 Inc. 1,027,838
Advances .. 17,225,792 17,565,435 Inc. 339,643
Premises . . 40,212 2,241 Dec. 37,971
Thus its deposits have increased in 12 months by
£182,462, and the advances by no less than
£339,643. However, during the same period its
stock of liquid assets has been added to by
£1,117,838. The bank’s liabilities to the public
amount to £21,026,621. of which just under 40 p.c.
is held liquid. Looking at it from another point,
there is only £5 2s. lOd. of outside money in the
concern for every £1 belonging to shareholders—
capital and disclosed reserves. The “premises,
£2241.” item is, of course, a joke. The co. has
more than 200 branches in Australia, and its chief
Sydney and Melbourne branches are both exceptionally
fine buildings. So it is safe to say that in this
respect alone the co. has a tidy inner reserve of
about £500,000. Each half-year the bank has written
large sums, generally £20,000, off its premises ; that
was a handy way of disposing of- its surplus cash.
But now that that item has practically disappeared,
the A’sia must find some other means of getting rid
of the burden. If it is allowed to accumulate it will
get out of hand, and one night it will
arise and sit heavily on the bank’s chest, and cause
trouble. And the easiest way out of the difficulty
appears to be an increased dividend. The balance-
sheet is still signed by “Jeans, General Manager,” and
“Blogg, Accountant.” There is a heap of solidity
about “Blogg, Accountant.”

Sundry creditors are certainly on the large side, and

£185,003 of sundry debtors, no less than £170,992

crease in this Section of the business has been re-
markable, and though sundry debtors have increased
by £36,043 since Sept., 1912, the capital and liabili-
ties have increased by onLy £11,340. During the
year Thomas Davis, who is mainly responsible for
the tremendous growth of the business, died, and the
co. will have to look a long way to find his equal.
Put once a co. like this is .properly established, it
can carry on. And given careful management and
provided there isn’t a very severe slump that will
leave it stranded with a large stock of second-hand
pianos, it should make large profits.
* * * *
When the Insurance Office of Australia
has,finished treading on its own toes, locking itself
out in the cold, and assaulting itself with intent, it
might dig ( out its share register, if it ever had
one, sweep the cobwebs and dust off it, and bring it
up to date. When the co. promised to be a law-
abiding citizen, the editor of this paper bought some
shares. But when the directors and shareholders took
to assassinating one another, instead of attending to
the co.’s affairs, and the whole concern threatened to
fall into a state of chaos, he got full up and sold
all his shares. That was montns ago. But he still
receives stamped and addressed envelopes and proxies
with 3d. duty stamps on them, and notifications that
various meetings will be held bo decide whether the
present directors will be boiled in oil or fried, and
numerous other petty documents. issued either by in-
dividual directors or by the co. His only hope is
that if the co. ever pays a dividend the cheque will
be sent to him as well. It might be good to enact
that any and every transfer will be null and void
unless registered within one month after date of
execution, and that the alleged transferee will be dis-
qualified. A lot of companies beside the 1.0. A. might
think over this point.

This co., which is a licensed dealer in corpses and
mangled remains, now embodies the late lamented
Albert Building Society, the Freehold Assets Realisa-
tion Co. (the ghost of the Freehold Investment and
Banking Co., which was formed in 1882 and sus-
pended payment in 1892, with liabilities of some-
thing- like £1,300,000), and practically all the
shares in the Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria. Tt
took over the last-mentioned institution a year ago,
and the profits were expected to show an improve-
ment on that account. But the improvement has
been mislaid, for a time, at any rate. The Land
Mortgage Bank paid a 7 p.c. dividend for the lat-
ter half of the year, amounting to £2679, and
the Freehold Assets Co. gets practically all
this. The properties -of the Land Mortgage
Bank stand at only £420, so the pur-
chaser will merely go on collecting the instal-
ments due. Besides this it derives revenue from
the repurchase of debentures below cost price, and
from sales —the amount realised in excess of the
written-down book value at which the co. took over
the properties in 1907 being reckoned as profit.
This table will show the progress the co. has made
in getting rid of the properties in hand:—
1908. 1912. 1913.

The liabilities are made up of debentures and

The sudden iump in the shares in other cos. is due,
of course, to the lately-acquired interest in the
Land Mortgage Bank. Assets (liquid or easily
convertible) amount to £153,949, and properties to
£52,867; total, £206,816. Liabilities amount to
£85,829, so that, after paying all debts, there are
assets equal to £120.987 to represent £74,245 of
capital. Assuming that the assets are worth 20s.
in the £, that Works out at approx.' 355: in assets
for each £1 of capital. But unless the co. adds still
further to its collection of corpses its income must
begin to dwindle. And then the proportion of
income that goes in working expenses must increase.
Elder’s Trustee and Executor Co.
(Adelaide) :
: Towards
June, Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.
1910 £458
Mar., Reserves.
1911 . . £468 Nil £468 10
1912 .. 425 Nil 425 435
1913 .. 1722 Nil 1722 2157
1914 .. 2435 6 p.c. = £l2OO 1235 3392
This co., which was formed in 1910, is under the
wing of Elder Smith and Co., of South Aus. in par-
ticular, and Australia in general. Like the majority
of the newer trustee cos. it decided that, though a
large authorised capital was handy to have on the
premises, it could do very well with a small paid
capital. Hence, although £500,000 is authorised,
£lOO,OOO is issued, and only £20.000, or 4s. oer
share, called. Thus there is a callable 16s. on each
of the 100,000 shares as an added guarantee of good
faith to those who leave their estates in its hands.
The co. has made wonderful progress. Naturally
it got the best of everything to start with —the
mere fact that it was connected with Elder Smith’s
is enough for South Aus. Two years ago the trust
estates in hand were £168,795; at March, 1913,
they were £525,269; at March, 1914, when the co.
was only four years old, they were no less than
£1,463,454, almost nine times as much as in 1912.
Commissions and interest have leapt from £863 in
1912 to £2923 in 1913 and £4567 for the past
12 months. What the directors don’t know about
running a business isn’t worth knowing, and there
are few men who could teach the manager much
about his job. So it is extremely risky to expect
a rise in the dividend for some time. With such
a rapidly-increasing business the present capital and
reserves will ~soorr - become inadequate : - then, unless
the co. has accumulated respectable reserves, it will
mean calling in more capital, and that is a thing
that should be deferred as long as possible. The
co. has no liabilities, and no goodwill; its assets
include S.A. Government stocks, £20,000; and cash,
etc., £4392.

*.* * *
Wheh the Utterly fat Private Enterprise
eo. produces its annual balance-sheet it speaks of the
profits it has been unable to conceal from a wicked
ahd envious world in a carefullv-subdued voice. The
tower of reserves looming behind it is referred to
sorrowfully as its poor hut of refuge against the
streaming wet day it expects next week at latest.
It utters no whoop of joy as it looks forward to the
golden years to come, but folds its coat-tails meekly
over the cash it has concealed therein, and expresses
the nious hope that an all-powerful and possibly unre-
liable Providence will be as merciful as possible.
But, in between whiles, when the tiger of Sosh is
not visible and can’t be smelt, and there seems to be
no special reason for concealment, it throws up its
helltopper and says something that shows it really
isn’t on the verge of bankruptcy. Last week the
giant Broken Hill Prop, paid its 167th dividend,
“absorbing” (beautiful word!) £59,050, and added
in a low voice that, up to May 20, it had distributed
£10,000,000_ in dividends and bonuses! And yet,
with all this. Broken Hill’s death-rate has been
lumping up of late owing to bad sanitation. Now
if there was as much Sosh about as Bruce Smith
thinks some of that £10,000,000 might have been
devoted to drains.
* * * »
The leading copper mines of the Common-
wealth are having a pretty sick time of it. Mines
are wearing a haggard look, and prices for the metal
nave been sagging at the knees. In N.S.W. the
echoes of the Great Cobar thud still drown the cries
of the Burraga and the Grafton Copper. In Queens-
onn*; Elliott looks like having shot its bolt,
as or| ly a dubious prospect of finding another
within the next several months. The Hampden-Clon-
curry passed the last dividend, and announces a creep
at the Macgregor mine, above the 100 ft. level, where
the new shaft threatens to be destroyed. The Mam-
moth, usually of movement, has been precipi-
tating of late. It has had to close down the Clans-
man mme, due to inrush of water. Chillagoe is still
Chillagoe, and Mount Perry is hardly more defunct.
Mount Oxide is shrouded in obscurity, though on the
other hand its neighbor, Oxide Creek—if this mine
can qualify to come into the list—cheerfully displays
.®. “test, development. Mount Morgan is battling
with the ,iob of switching off from an old-new plant
to one that is brand new, and one that is destined to
treat ore of considerably lower grade than did the
now moribund plant, and that will be a bitter pill.
Great Fitzroy shareholders are already beginning to
feel the chill of Outer darkness, though the deben-
ture holders are kept happy enough out of the pro-
ceeds of the last reconstruction and the future pro-
pects of the sale of the Laloki mine. In S.A., the
senile wallaroo and Moonta manages to arrest decay
tor the time being. Flotation and tailings is the
prescription. Lake Pernatty is too young and cheekv
to deserve serious mention as yet. In W.A the
Whim Well is unable to see visitors. Tasmania alone
affords the happy relief from gloom. The Mount
Lyell has only just emerged, with increasing outputs
and profitable superphosphates.
* * *
The Newcastle (N.S.W.) foreign coal
trade has been booming, and our four best oversea
customers- I —Chile, U.S.A., Java and the Straits Settle-
ments—took 213,846 tons more coal during the first
quarter of this year than for the corresponding period
of 1913. The foreign trade increased from 343,808
tons to 595,657 tons, a jump of 251,749 tons, which
has caused Newcastle to order six new 15-ton travel-
ling cranes.' The inter-State and Maoriland trade is
also healthy, having increased for the first quarter by
65,958 tons. The total export to Australasian ports
was 769.972 tons, and to foreign countries 595.657
tons, 1,365,629 tons in three months, valued
So N. S. Wales has great hones that it
will dig up its irreplaceable inheritance very shortly,
and sell it to foreigners for a song. And it gives
three cheers for the time when it will be reduced to
burning cow-dung.

N.S.W. Assistant Government Geologist
Carne’s report on the Wellington (N.S.W.) phosphate
find is a damaging document. These are some frag-
ments collected from the report: On Jan. 13, 1914. a
couple of shallow shafts had been sunk, but revealed
no commercial phosphate, the best result being less
than 1 p.c. Later' on, when one of the shafts had
been deepened to 55ft. from the brace, and a drive
put out, an inspection was made and occasional
nodules and boulders of white rock phosphate were
found. “About half-way in the drive what appeared
to be solid phosphate rock proved, on firin'? a shot
at our request, to be a mere skin on the solid lime-
stone wall of the cavern.... From present develop-
ments it would appear that the rich white phosphate
rock is in comparatively very limited quantity, whilst
the value of the remainder depends on the richness and
extent of the cave fillings and bone breccias.” At
Newbrigyn cave nothing of value was discovered.
The Borenore limestone caves, in the Orange district,
were next examined. “Here rich and extensive phos-
phate deposits have been reported, but examination
failed to disclose them.” The prospects of the Gam-
boola Cabonne Phosphates Ltd., at Molong, did not
inspire Carne’s enthusiasm.
