B. H.' Proprietary announces a shilling
dividend. The four-weekiv report states that 25,414
tons of crudes were raised, which brings the mine
back into line with the North and the South. These
three mines produce about half the lead and pay
more than half the dividends from Broken Hill. The
run of four weeks at the B.H.P. mill gave 4570
tons of lead concentrates from 23,400 tons of
crude ore, and 19,591 tons of tailings. Tailings,
after being reground and concentrated for lead, go
to the zinc separator. No less than 6450 tons of
zinc concentrates were produced in four weeks.
Butler’s Tin, Torrington (N.S.W,), is to
be resuscitated by Melbourne capital. This mine has
been idle for some years, a fact that is not much in
its favor if N.S.W. mining history is anything to go
by. However, one .1. B. Lewis declares that there is
sufficient ore in sight to keep the battery going for
two years, “which should pay working expenses and
capital outlay, and leave a considerable profit.”
Capital is £50,000, in £1 shares. Vendors and pro-
moters are to receive 15,000 shares, with a further
cut for the vendors of £2OOO cash and £3OOO from
the promised profits.
A co. with the impressive title of New
South Wales Consolidated Diamond and Tin Mines
Md. is about to be incorporated. It has under out-
right offer, the Bound Mount mine at Copeton. The
vendors reckon they made this mine pay for eight
years, during the early portion of which the diamonds
that were won realised 6s. per carat. This price is
now elevated to over £l. The mine is claimed to
have yielded 80,000 carats of diamonds and 150 tons
of tin oxide from one-tentli of its workable area. As
in the rest of the diamond mines in this district the
stones are found in river washes of tertiary age.

Eleanora Gold (N.S.W.) reports a clean-
up of 536A0z. gold from 940 tons of ore. At No. 7
level the No. 3 reef shows a width of 18in., assaying
30dwt. ‘ No. 9 level is in 60ft. from the crosscut, the
face being 72in. wide, assaying 27dwt.
Trong Tin (Perak), another candidate
for Australian affections, holds an area of 720 acres
under a2l years’ lease. Boring operations in the
northern valley testify to 150 acres of granitic sand
lift. deep, containing 2.361 b. of tin oxide per cubic
yard. The karang, or wash, is covered by 10ft. of
silt and fine sand, reducing the average of the material
to be handled to ljlb. tin oxide. In the southern
valley wash has been proved over 180 acres, with a
thickness of 10ft. and average value of 1.161 b. The
sandy overburden is Bft. thick, making a depth of
24ft. of alluvia], averaging o.Blb. AVater is said to
he plentiful and the tin coarse. In fact, for
dredging purposes the deposit is branded as “ideal.”
The hearse is not . yet out for Cathcart,
Ararat (Vic.), though the plumes have been airing
for the past two years. Consequently Harry Dodd
lives on as chairman, George Bryant draws his bit
weekly as mine manager, and Alex. Peacock his £ 260
a year-as legal manager. All told, 3s. in divs. went
to shareholders last half-year, which, from a supposed
dead-up, was a remarkable recovery. George Bryant
reckons 150,0 fathoms remain to be taken out, and,
by rule of three, if 2861 fathoms give 46230 z., then
over 24000 z. ought yet to come. So there ought still
to be a shilling or two left in the claim. Costs run
well on to an oz.
* * * •
Duke and Main Leads, Maryborough
(Vic.), grinds on at a small profit oil the Duke gutter.
As that gutter is a big lead, costs ought to be a
guide to those who argue that the new Langi Logan
cannot work at below 80s. per fathom. The Main Leads
Consols’ record for last two half-years is 495. and
465. per fathom. About 71 p.c. of that 495. is for
mine wages, and another 19 p.c. for profits, laths and
timber. This shows how the alluvial mining industry
finds bread and bacon for the worker. Co.’s dividends
last.half-year were nil, against 9.4 p.c. for the pre-
ceding term. Co. now pays another 6d., making the
total to date 225. per share. Since Jimmy McCrorey
floated the co. in the days when the Main Leads
looked like becoming a mine, gold worth £308,000
has been got. _ Costs have kept well below the 13dwt.
mark, otherwise the £54.000 of divs. would have
had to be shaded off. That sum has come out of
11,520 ft. of gutter. As much ground is left in the
claim as has been panelled, but north 1500 ft. is too
poor to raise for road metal, and beyond that the
run of gold for 1200 ft. is a streak in the gutter.
Beyond, the lead makes west into what to-day is No
Man’s Land. If gold gets into the deep ground there
Main Leads Consols will get on to the div. list again.
Failing that, up will go the shutters.
Victoria United, Ballarat, is very groggy
on what is rather a groggy record. Co.’s mine output
is 441,000 tons, and its gold record 132,4000 z. Divi-
dends have been £51,000, or 255. 3d. per Bs. Id.
share. But the co. is now down to sdwt. stone, which
shades off into a loss. John Bull has a big interest
in the Victoria United, and his man in Ballarat likes
the outlook so little that he wants to wind up. This
can save only a few pence per share, and it isn’t
likely that Ballarat will let one of its last mining
shows be snuffed out that way! The old plan of let-
tmg the working miner take blocks on tribute will
possibly be the expedient adopted—for a time, at any
rate. Those who handled the mine are to blame for
not having kept the dividends down until a fair
working reserve was established. But Ballarat mining
me i l /r ww a ys hve U P to the last penny of their income,
-ind then wonder that calls won’t come in when they
are needed.

Queensland’s production of copper for the
first three months of this year fell away to 5344} tons
compared with 6389} tons for the first quarter of
1913. A drop of over 1000 tons in the quarterly
yield as well as a decline in the market price of the
metal explains why the mineral return (apart from
gold, coal and precious stones) is only £441,160
compared with £536,637 for the first quarter of
1913. Copper constitutes about three-fourths of
Queensland’s total base metal production.
For the first three months of the year
Stanthorpe (Q.), once the most productive tin field
in the State, turned over 121,711 cub. yds. for
148,1381 b. of black tin worth between £7OOO and
£BOOO. ’the average return per yard pans out
1.211 b. of black tin, but like other dredging concerns
in N.S.W. the profit margin is becoming narrowed
by the fall in the price of the metal. There are
six dredging and sluicing plants at work, and others
are to start very shortly.
Chillagoe (Q.) is the most important
molybdenite field in Australia, although there are
indications that the border of N.S.W. and Queens-
land and parts of Westralia will become producers.
For the first quarter of this year Chillagoe produced
16 tons of molybdenite, value £6623, an average of
£413 per ton.
Charters Towers is still depressed, for
although the aggregate return for three months was
£54,963, dividends come along very slowly. The
principal gold mines on the field crushed' 13,593
tons for £46,643, average £3 Bs. 7d. per ton.
The field can live on that, but it must be remem-
bered that old mines like Mills United, Brilliant
Stockholm and Brilliant Central are now worked to
a large extent by tributors. Mills United has about
90 men on tribute. The return for three months
was 640 tons for £1965. For the same period
Brilliant Extended crushed 5004 tons for £12,649,
averaging over 50s. per ton, but costs are high and
a small loss was reported last month.
* * * *
Mammoth Copper (Q.) reports a bore-
hole development in the hanging wall of No. 2
lode at the 230 ft. level. This drive is in from
30ft. to 40ft., and has had a width of sft. 6in.,
assaying 7 p.c. The bore assays up to 12 p.c., and
as it extends 4ft. into the hanging wall side of the
drive, the proved width is now 9ft. 6in. The end
of the hole is still in ore and some of the stuff
passed through is dense pyrites, which will come
in very handy when smelting is started. This No.
2 lode was cut quite unexpectedly in the crosscut
from the shaft. The main lode was poor in the
crosscut, but is now a fine ore-body at the 182 ft.
fnark, going south.
*.* * *
Under the amalgamation scheme, Mount
Elliott (Q.) parts with £70,000, and only £53,000
of this is ear-marked as working capital. Remem-
bering the struggle Mount Elliott and Hampden had
before getting on to the dividend list, £53,000 looks
very small. Much expensive development work is
necessary, for there isn’t a well-developed show
among Elliott’s new acquisitions. There are 300.000
shares to. be issued, and Mount Elliott gets 190,000
and retains a call over 100,000 shares at par for
two years. That option will cause it to stick to
its surplus cash for some time to come, and if the
option is exercised it will hold 290.000 shares out
of 400,000. Mount Elliott paid £202,837 in divi-
dends last year, which was much more than its net
profit.
* * * *
Mammoth Copper (N.Q.) is employing
40 men at its Chieftain mine, and, according to
Warden Tregear, good progress is being made with
development work and the construction of the new
plant. At the 230 ft. level the southern ore shoot
is showing 15 p.c. ore over a width of 3ft. 6in.
A sft. lode is being driven on to the north, prospect-
ing 7 p.c. These two lodes are quite distinct and are
being driven on in opposite directions.
* * * *
Warden Tregear’s report on the Mount
Mulligan (Q.) coalfield requires to be read in con-
junction with the report from the superintending
engineer. The Warden states that a fault occurred
m the carboniferous formation disturbing the coal
seam, but Chillagoe’s engineer says that the end of
the tunnel—beyond the influence of the fault—is
showing the best seam of bituminous coal he has
seen in the mine. The face shows coking coal
6m., splint coal 3in., coking coal 6in., fireclay 12in.,
overlying 30in. of good coking coal. It is expected
that by the time the railway is through, in Sept.,
1000 tons of coal will have been raised from
development work, and that the mine will maintain
an output of 100 tons per day. A profit of from
2s. to 3s. per ton is considered satisfactory, but
Chillagoe expects to gain indirectly by using its
own coke for smelting purposes. The Mammoth
copper smelter will also require large quantities of
poke.
Kelly’s Queen Block (Charters Towers)
is striving hard to pull off a reconstruction scheme
before the whole of its capital from contributing
shares has been paid up, which is a very unusual
procedure. There are 48,000 contributing shares of
10s. each, on which Bs. 4d. has been paid, leaving
Is. Sd per share —or £4OO0 —callable. But more
than 40,000 are on the co.’s hands (28,600 forfeited,
11,000 liable, to forfeiture, and 1000 surrendered).
Obviously it is impossible to carry on when contri-
buting shares are thrown up in this wholesale fashion,
so the proposal is to reconstruct on a contributing
basis. Still a liability of ss. per share with an issue
of 48,000 is pretty stiff, particularly as the new
undertaking at the W’elcome lease is only a prospect-
ing venture, and the ground is very wet. In these
times people don’t care to shoulder a liability of ss.
per share. The scheme has been coldly received on
the Towers, and there are numerous sellers of the
old paid-up shares at 2d.
Lode Hill, near Cooktown (Q.), got the first
bump of its short life last week when it went topsy-
turvy under the sort of shock that threw the Briseis
Extended on to the dustheap. Frank Powell was
known to be on his way to inspect, and it seemed
as if the selling was due to anxiety as to how he
would measure up the claim. So far, a cold-blooded
inspection of the claim has been wanted to test esti-
mates, and to check bores. Buyers have taken dis-
tant pastures as green, and now in their haste and
shock they regard the same pasture as pure road-
metal. Of course, one innocent round the Melbourne
Stock Exchange muttered when asked, “H’oh, go to
Pernatty. ’Ow could I get noose.” But his fat figure
hasn’t dwindled over the slump, so he hasn’t let
absence of knowledge interfere with his daily square
meals.
Brilliant Deeps (Charters Towers) re-
ports, a crushing of 464 tons for £1363, which is
the poorest lot put through for some time. The
mine will now be worked with two shifts. In two
years (1912-13) this mine has produced £60,000
from 12,144 tons of stone, but some of the money
has gone in development and machinery. Develop-
ment has been costly because the mine was badly
ventilated, and an agreement was made with the
Brilliant Block next door to sink a shaft some 700-
odd feet in order to connect up the workings from
ao. 4 level. Brilliant Deeps agreed to share the expense
of sinking and to drive No. 4 level to a position
near the shaft. The shaft and the drive are com-
pleted, and the crosscut between them measures
100 ft., which is nearly double the estimated distance.
No doubt the connection will be made and the ventila-
tion improved. Brilliant Deeps has given more than
one surprise, so it may shake things up again some
time.
* * * .
A Victorian syndicate with £BOOO to burn
is said to have taken possession of the old Mount
Jackson (W.A.). This show was one of the first
worked in the State, and has swallowed up a good
deal of money in its time. Several attempts to
run it at a profit have failed. One co. raised and
crushed 26,000 tons for a return of 17,0000 z., but
somehow could not earn anything for shareholders.
Ultimately a local co. was formed to work it, but as
sufficient capital could not be obtained, operations
ceased and the mine was abandoned. Want of gold
at depth seems to be its failing.
The Augusta mill (W.A.) has started
crushing at last. This is the Melbourne-financed
property which has a length of shoot about the width
of George-street, and which has under present man-
agement taken a long time to reach the producing
stage. Full accounts of its previous history are to
be found in back numbers of this paper.
* * * *
Marie’s Find at Golden Valley on the
Yilgarn field (W.A. 1 has been taken in hand by a
Melbourne co., with a capital of £4OOO. A narrow
reef carrying good values has been followed down to
shallow depth. A couple of able-bodied miners
might make £lO or £l5 a week out of the show for
a but it is altogether too small for a co.
„ r^ a ?ch was a good dividend month in
nestralia. Altogether £116,593 was distributed by
gold-mining cos., making the total for the first quar-
ter of the year £203,788 and the aggregate to March
31 £24,106,465. Dividends amounting to nearly
£lOO,OOO have been declared payable in April. As
has been the case for some years past the bulk of the
cash paid out is furnished by a few of the big
mines, and, as is well known, these props are grow-
ing less sturdy and prop-like every year.
*** ■ *
Mararoa (W.A.) has sent its managing-
director Henderson to superintend boring operations
at Holden’s Find. The determination of the co. to
spend some of its surplus cash in this way is still
being grumbled at locally, and the allegation that
there are plenty of better shows on the eastern
fields, without going into the far-off fields beyond
Meekatharra, is being rubbed into the directors.
Holden s Find is about as popular in the West as
Home Rule is in Belfast. Its detractors say that
picked parcels will be gouged out of the few leases
showing gold, and for a time may make a fair show-
ing, but in their opinion the field isn’t any good at
all. The best report about it to date is that fur-
nished by an assistant Government geologist, who
said, somewhat guardedly, that Whittaker’s line may
Rve • at depth, and that there was a possibility of
other payable reefs being found. Those interested
in the district should have clubbed together at the
outset and paid Dr. Maclaren, or some other expert
of standing, to inspect and give them the real
strength of things.
»** • . »
Eastern investors had better be careful of
Yilgarn (W.A.). The weirdest and queerest ventures are
being boomed for flotation purposes in Melbourne, Syd-
ney or anywhere else where the public can be induced
to bite, and imaginative stories are being told about
their prospects. Some can, no doubt, be made to
furnish ore for a few crushings, but as alreadj"
pointed out on this page, there will be no real profit
on the work. Writer recently looked at a number
of so-called gold-spangled formations on this wide
and expansive field, and saw none with any signs of
permanence. Some were only fit for the rubbish-
heap. Others ought to have been dumped there
before now.
* * * *
Great Fingall (W.A.) has stopped develop-
ment work pending the completion of timbering and
taking out leading stopes at the 15th, 16th, 17th
and 18th levels. The object of this move is to put
these deep workings in a condition to permit of the
required tonnage being mined. Co. has abandoned
its option on the Big Bell leases, which the local
papers were booming some time back, and which
back numbers of this paper stated sampled far below
what their admirers estimated.
Lake View and Star (W.A.) cheerfully
reports that the lode intersected some time ago at
2100 ft. in the Lake View lease has averaged 51s over
a width of 66in. for the first 43ft. of driving. The
co. attaches much importance to the development on
account of it being in the deepest part of the mine,
and In a property which was expected to have been
worked out long before this.
* * * »
A number of tributors are on gold in the
Boulder No. 1 Co.’s Ivalgoorlie lease. One party has
just taken out a crushing which is expected to return
nearly 50s. per ton. Another is breaking ore of the
same grade, a third reckons that it has stumbled
across a patch of lOoz. to 12oz. stuff, while several
others are reported to be getting good prospects. The
whole of the eastern portion of the property extending
a distance of 1300 ft. from Albert’s shaft to the Great
Boulder boundary is now being tested, and a lot of
pickings are expected to be made by the co. out of
the work. Oroya Links (W.A.) announces that it is
now busy driving at the 900 ft. level of Croesus Pro-
prietary workings, and hopes before long to pick up
all the shoots of ore opened up in the levels above
and particularly in the winze sunk below No. 7 level,
where a fine body of ore was disclosed. Nothing
worth noting is at present taking place in the other
sections of the co.’s mostly worn-out holding.
Whim Well Copper (W.A.) has a habit
of computing its ore reserves, proved or otherwise, in
tonnages that appear to have millions for units. A
while back the mine was visited by a couple of
directors who returned to London with seven-figured
statistics and elongated assay values. A Murex plant
of moderate dimensions was put to treating the car-
bonate ore that is alleged to be there in more than
plentiful abundance. About 130,000 tons of ore are
reported as having been mined since operations
started, half of which is said to have been shipped.
The average value of the ore mined is about 8 p.c.
copper. The richest ore was dressed to 17 pc
copper and bagged, while 11 p.c. copper ore was
shipped in bulk. Yet, somehow, the co. has lacked
spare capital for development, otherwise the mine
should already have proved itself capable of produc-
ing 1,000,000 tons of carbonate ore, neglecting, of
course, the odd millions of sulphide ore that may be
supposed to exist below the water-line. This latter
is said to be comparatively deep, and the lode com-
paratively flat. The Murex process was recently
announced to be a technical success, but figures as to
costs, recoveries and profits are very slow in coming
forward.
Golden Ridge, (W.A.) has been having a
look at the old Cra'iggiemore at Laverton, and may
unwater some of the ancient workings with a yiew to
sampling. Dr. Laver and others worked thjs show
on behalf of a London co. for some years, but a series
of unpleasant developments which occurred at depth
first disheartened them, and finally drove them off
the premises. It does not appear to be the sort of
mine that would suit the Ridge or anyone elsfe ii>
search of profitable undertakings.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 27, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
The Shakspeare Festival filled in most of
last week. It was housed in the Town Hall
and turned that large municipal barn
into a medley of Austrian chairlegs
and the tourist’s notion of Stratford-
on-Avon. Organising sec. Grace Bur-
rows, who has been slogging steadily at
this event for months (placating rival
Shakspearian fanatics, and shaping poetic
chaos into a humdrum business proposition)
can now put her feet up and let down her
back hair for five minutes. When last seen
on Saturday night she was carrying off some
of the scenery under one arm, by way of
goading the male person who was suffering
from a tired feeling to look for work.
Mrs. Boult put in a strenuous week in
charge of the amusement column—mostly
dancing—at the Shakspeare Festival, and
hon. sec. Farmer Whyte spread himself over
the daily task, the uncommon round, etc.
The affair was opened with an oration from
our sub-royalty, and the Town Hall plat-
form was filled with officialdom to watch
the living chess parade the floor. Queenie
Boult, in fantastic garb, danced round the
moves, which proceeded to suitable music.
The White King (Norman Zions in private
life) and the Black Queen (Mrs. S.
Maguire) were the handsomest figures on
the floor. The Bishops were so severely
petticoated that they looked like Mrs.
Bishops, but the Rooks were imposing dames
with large watch-towers on their heads.
Frantic excitement, started by a juvenile
prig aged five years, seethed round portion
of the Shakspeare Festival. Some of the
Cleopatra set had stained its arms and legs
to look as much like ancient Egyptians and
Assyrian slaves as possible. When the
Y.W.C.A. infant in a shrill treble asked
her adult guardians to look at the naked
men walking about (on opening day) the
scare began. They looked, and saw in the
distance some highly respectable citizens
who were clad in garb of the same shade as
their skins. No one shrieked for help, but
the dreadful news fled round.
Miss Badham’s young ladies (of the C. of
E. premises) were warned off the establish 1 -
ment by clerical busybodies who hadn’t been
present. The ’phone rang furiously.
Organising Sec. Grace Burrows placed her
spectacles firmly on her nose, to see what
she could see, and tried to track some Adam
to his lair. The Cleopatra set fled to drape
itself in heavy overcoats and top boots, but.
the worst offender of the lot was so fully
clothed —barring his arms and shin bones—
that the rumpus simmered down to Much
Ado About Less than Nothing.
Commissioner Ivempster, of Rhodesia,
went off to Maoriland on Saturday. He
is the man who has been peering into the
closer settlement movement here, and hid
himself so successfully from the society
lion-hunters that they couldn’t even find the
apnroximate number of his telephone. It
was said to be 00.0001. Commissioner K.
has been writing reams of stuff for the Im-
perial Government concerning his flight
through N.S.W.
A new Help is now brushing his hair at
State Government House. He fills a gap
that has been yawning for him since the
departure of Forbes, A.D.C. He is a Cap-
tain Wallis, of a Wiltshire regiment, lately
stationed at Gib., and he came this way by
the Morea. I don’t think a Wiltshire regi-
ment is very heavily upholstered, but no
doubt the recent arrival will make an Im-
pression in our Best Suburbs.
Chamberlain Nevill let the news of his im-
pending departure leak out during his
hurried visit to this port. He didn’t seem as
if he wanted to let it leak out. but a hole
broke in the bottom of the informa-
tion, and the facts fell through. He
leaves Australia in the Niagara (June
29), after giving the Munro-Ferguson team
a helping hand. This time his farewell is
to be finally and ultimate and for the last
time.
According to Report—a frequent liar, by
the way—ex-Aide Verney has been carefully
sounded since his return, re his desire to
acrain stand behind vice-regal backs in Aus-
tralia. But he goes, with his sharp little
lady, to India in July, to rejoin his regi-
ment for a few months. At the end of the
year I fancy Captain Yerney can retire on
Naif-pay, if he chooses—a state of beatitude
combined with other easy circumstances
whioffi makes the old Commonwealth soldier
grit hxs teeth. There is a regrettable lack of
provision for the old Commonwealth soldier.
Minister for Instruction Blair, of Queens-
land, has gone north again, after putting on
his specs at the Premiers’ confabulation in
Melbourne. Mrs. Blair is reputed to be the
beauty of the Queensland Ministerial team —
in its relation to the domestic hearth—and
•she snatched a posy of compliments during
her brief assistance to the Easter Rag
parade.
Ella Caspers, the Goulburn contralto,
ought to fill the Town Hall at her forthcom-
ing chirrup in this town. She has been away
for over six years, doggedly chasing
technique in the British capital, and should
be able to pour forth a song calculated to
give the public its money’s worth.
The Rupert Clarke quest of the Fly River
in Papua started from Circular Quay, per
Dutch packet Van Cloon, on Saturday. The
Kismet —a helpful name for a river craft
that is going to need a lot of luck to see
it to the source of the cannibal-haunted Fly
River —was tucked away somewhere on the
same steamer. The Van Cloon will drop the
Clarke expedition—this side up with care,
at Port Moresby.
There is a Royal Society of St. George in
this town. It breaks out once a year in
Old Tradition, and sits down to solemnly
pretend that it lives in the Middle Ages,
when dragons walked through London, and
set the Thames on fire by breathing at it.
President Lethbridge King took the chair at
the annual banquet the other night, and Gov-
ernor Strickland was induced to explain
things. Women —a few of the official caste —
are now admitted to eat roast beef under the
patronage of St. George (said to have been a
dishonest army contractor in Asia) and in
the presence of a Yeoman of the Guard (the
Guard hasn’t been dead more than 300 years,
so its burial isn’t yet arranged) and a pike-
man. These haughty vassals attended the
baron of beef which was accompanied by some
very wet gravy. There ought to have' been
archers and beefeaters, and men-at-arms, and
halberdiers, and heralds, and armor-bearers,
and vikings, and slingers, and the Roman
legion and the Macedonian phalanx and some
Greek hoplites and sundries, but you can’t
expect everything. Still I think there might
have been hoplites and sundries.
Assertions that the Women’s Handi-
crafts’ Society recently breathed its last,
and had been buried in the cemetery of
Lost Hopes, are unreliable. According to
a press report, the distressful Handicrafts
has only given up a former depot, not the
ghost, and elected a new committee.
Dolores tours Queensland after her Syd-
ney warble in June, under the management
of Birch and Carroll, who have taken over
the Bananaland dates.
Mrs. Elsa Buhrow, for a long time resi-
dent here, left Sydney for Europe the other
day. She was formerly a well-known light
comedy actress on the Continent, and local
amateurs here sometimes had her inimi-
table example to drag them through a charity
entertainment. The German Benevolent
Society handed Mrs. Buhrow a sturdy little
watch, bv wav of thanks for her services to
the Cause, though a German Australian in
distressful circumstances, or without a bit
of sausage stowed away for a droughty day,
is fairly uncommon hereabouts.
The veteran elocution teacher, W. A. Hol-
man, is to have a matinee benefit thrust upon
him by the theatrical profession, of which
he is a member. May 14 is the date. The
Theatre Royal will house this event. Hol-
man senior is the father of that Holman
junior who has the stormy billet of Premier
in the present N.S.W. Cabinet.
The recent Ren6 Maxwell concert in this
hamlet where the rude forefathers sleep
cleared about £350. This makes something
of a record for an affair which was practi-
cally a send-off to a promising student of
vocal gymnastics.
The N.S.W. branch of the Olympic Games
Fund is giving the ball, in aid of the cause,
a hearty push. May 20 is the date of this
frivol, and the place is the Town Hall.
Mrs. Mayoress Richards holds the fort at
innumerable meetings, with Mrs. W. A.
Holman, the Carruthers dame and Mrs.
Charles Wade as chief fellow-conspirators.
Hon. sec. Mrs. Agnes Deery signs her name
to reams of correspondence inviting
presence, your cash, your sympathy and
anything else you have about you except
your debts. Sporting sets are spoken of
in italics as a feature. Swimmers will be
heavily disguised as mermen and mermaids,
and neck-to-tail costume will waggle round
the tango.
J. J. Virgo, formerly secretary or some-
thing of the Y.M.C.A. here, is now in
charge of a similar institution in Lon-
don. Pie wrote to a Sydney friend the other
day in a glow of royal reflection, having
recently trotted the King and Queen round
his Christian Young Men’s premises. Virgo
could get a subscription out of a confirmed
atheist if he had the floor to himself for five
minutes, so he probably found George’s good
lady, Mary, an easy mark for a new set of
hymn-books.
A Maorilander named Chamberlin-
Chamberlin (this is his spelling of the name,
but other people of the same clan spell them-
selves Chamberlain or Chamberlayne), who
is filled with high ideals, arrived here from
England the other day. The ideals are
loaded with notions for the uplifting of the
Maori —according to European codes of
morals. Mr. Chamberlin-Chamberlin, who
once owned the plain, unvarnished : ame of
Tims or Timms, was reared at Te Puke, a
small hamlet about 30 miles from Rotorua.
Timms senior owned a farm thereabouts, and
had a hankering desire to go to England
and change his name to Chamberlin-Chamber-
lin. But he died Timms, and was entered
to that effect in the Recording Angel’s debit
and credit account. So he was in the same
category as Job and Eli and Moses and other
short-dated people of ancient history. His
son, however, set forth on the quest, taking
with him his mother and an adopted Maori
son. These interesting folk passed this way
on their return to M.L. after eight or nine
years absence in England.
Chamberlin-Chamberlin ( ne6 Timms), his
mother, aged 85, and the Maori youth make
as quaint a triplet of quixotic travellers,
burning with a desire to turn a brown man’s
traditions pale white, as we are likely to
bump against in these sordid times. They
are going to make Te Whaiti, about 60 miles
from Rotorua, their headquarters. This is
on the edge of the Urewera country, where
there are still natives who, so the yarn or
narrative goes, have never seen a white
skin. In the inwards of Urewera wilds
lives old Rua, part Tohunga, part
humbug, and chiefly Brigham Young or Solo-
mon. Rua once tramped through Auckland
like Jacob of old, with long curly black
hair and 10 wives at his heels.
Paora Chamberlin-Chamberlin, the brown
hope of his foster parent, will have to take
heed lest he slip back into the old creed
whereof the symbols are a Maori mat and a
coffee-colored wahine.
Ralph Stock, whose “Confessions of a
Tenderfoot” has had a prosperous run
through various editions, is just back from
his native England. His present plans in-
dicate a trip north—thence on a cruise in
search of possible pearls and certain copy
amongst the lovely, lazy Pacific Isles.
The Bush Book Club, which continues to
pour carefully-selected literature into the
tents of the Outback, holds its annual meet-
ing in the Town Hall before this reaches
you. The society points out that it hungers
and thirsts for more cash, and will assuredly
spend as many shillings as it can beg or
borrow on more books for the bush. Lady
Poore gave this club a breezy start, when
she stunned our Best People with the state-
ment that Australians had little or no imag-
ination. Hence the necessity for a Bush
Book Club. The former unswerving deter-
mination to fill Bullocky Bill with letter-
press of the Ella Wheeler Wilcox type has
weakened before a broader sense of Bill’s
forceful way of devouring sporting news.
The selection is less heavily censored and
the all-story magazines are allowed to trickle
through the parcels post. Barring the lurid
outpourings of Victoria Cross and Elinor
Glyn, the club committee is prepared to
pass on any books you can spare—with
many thanks —to the Outback.
Mrs. Walter Elkington, who left Sydney
a while ago, has written to a friend here of
sundry impressions at Monte Carlo during
the gayest month in that dizzy rouge et noir
resort. She says that high, wired collars
are in again. “Everyone wears a neck car-
pet. Yesterday I saw Mrs. Langtry (Lady
de Bathe), Liane de Pougy and Otero at
the same table, gaming. They looked aw-
fully well, for girls of the Victorian era.
But Otero is still lovely. They had their
respective hair done up on top and wore
rabbit-proof collars. The Russian Grand
Duke Cyril (who married a daughter of the
late Duke of Edinburgh) is a startlingly
handsome person, and he games in style. That
is to say. he never puts down less than a
thousand francs at a time, then walks off.
leaving the croupier to tell him the result.
When he wins he always puts one of those
nice, fat gold 100-franc pieces into the
croupier’s box. Thieves abound. I had
my new and lovely blue fox fur taken off
my back while I was reaching over for a
small win.”
The Imperial Dance Salon, off William-
street, is drawing our Most Expensive
People to caper round its ballroom in the
very latest clothes. Its decorations are cal-
culated to make you sit down and think.
Supposing you could grow a garden of un-
fading tropic blooms —gigantic flowers of
red and yellow—and then rear a huge trellis
roof and command your head gardener
(who’d do as lie was told, which is unusual
in a head-gardener) to go aloft. While
aloft —in your roof garden—he would plant
Chinese lanterns and electric bulbs. The
whole would then bloom together, and you
would tango under the results. That’s the
new Imperial dance salon, which grows, in
addition, an orchestra of picked performers.
The Ernest Raleigh duet, the Society Lit-
tles, Consett Stephen, one of the Laidleys,
Marjorie Street, Helen Faithful, the Mac-
neill Simpsons, Gracie Noble, the Frank
Bethunes and a number of frisky young
men who trail R.N. behind them, hopped
round to their own great satisfaction on
Saturday eve.
Carl Bentzen, the aesthetic young Ameri-
can tango expert who wears a soulful ex-
pression over an Abe Lincoln black necktie,
is the teacher who put President Wilson’s
daughter through the tango-tangle a while
ago. He floats round the Imperial ballroom
with a pupil at arm’s length most after-
noons. The fickle Young Person —who once
adored Andrew Higginson and Julius Knight
—is transferring her affections to Bentzen.
The ball on H.M.A.S. Australia occurred
after my last letter was gummed up. It’s
worth a few belated lines, for it reminded
one of a criticism made by a U.S.A. officer
when the Amurkan fleet was here. He said :
“I never go on board a R’yal Navy cralt
without getting cold feet. They always
seem so mighty sorry to see you, and so
almighty glad to get rid of you, that the
American sailor, who’s a genial host, can t
figure out why the R’yal Navy ever re-
quests the pleasure of your presence.” -
The Australia had most of its ironmongery
tucked away under bunting on the night of
the dance. It happened to be calm, but the
visitors would have arrived intact in a gale
of wind, for the gangway was like a long,
covered tube. You fell into it at one end
and were hauled out of it at the other.
Admiral Patey and his slender, dark-browed
lady gave a cold official finger to the herd,
near the top of a stairway. Mayor Richards
and Mrs. Mayoress, and the W. G. Laytons,
got up the steep stairs without falling over
their feet. Others who followed were less
lucky, and bounded on to the deck
like indiarubber dolls. The melancholy
visage of Captain Henderson, R-N.
(isn’t lie in charge of Garden Island:)
lurked near the official group. Lurk-
ing is a fine art and should be studied.
The Stricklands (the Governor and his eldest
treasure) came along, and were headlined,
so to speak, on a platform. Mrs. E red
King’s diamond tiara was one of the events
of the evening. The cheerless-looking Sena-
tor Gould (for Heaven’s sake don’t misprint
this Ghould) brought a nice-looking daughter.
The A. F. Lloyds, of Rose Bay, chaperoned
their tall and pretty girl, a debutante bud.
But it was the night out of the Society
matron. The mere youngster can only hope
to be noticed by something very young in
uniform at a naval hop.
An All England Women’s hockey, team,
led by Miss Gaskell (I wonder if she’s re-
lated to the lady who wrote “Cranford” V).
w r ill dome this way early in July. Later
they go on to the Dominion of Massey to
annihilate (or otherwise) weaker, feminines
who smite the flying ball. The inter-State
hockey week is predestined for July 11, and
(he visitors will not only be able to biff
the ball themselves but will swamp up
rivers of tea between times. The N.S.W.
Women’s Hockey team recently acclaimed
as chairman Miss Winifred West, with
vice-chairman (this sounds complicated)
Cecilia Stephen, lion. sec. Mary Perry, and
hon. treas. Thelma ijong to form a strong-
handed background.
A good show of brush and chisel work has
been enticing even the thrifty Potts Pointer
to spend a few coins on Art. The wares
were exhibited in Artist Ethel Stephens’s big
studio in Kirkton-road, Darlinghurst. Judge
Backhouse was one of the. first buyers, and
marked for his own a gem in oils, “Elizabeth
Bay at Dusk.” This is one of. a series of
small and charming pictures, painted in the
free and slightly impressionist French man-
ner Miss Stephens caught during her stay
at Etaples. The little red blob that spells
“sold” was liberally distributed over this sec-
tion. In still life, a study of zinnias, full
of sunshine, and a big canvas covered with
roses were good enough to steal.
Dorothea Barclay, a Tasmanian girl who
studied sculpture in Melbourne some years
ago, and then bustled off to Paris to catch
the higher technique (on a stepladder, I sup-
pose), returned here to settle down a few
months ago. Settling down, in Sydney as a
sculptress—unless you get into the monu-
mental mason business —is more down than
settle, in a financial sense. This daring
young woman, who showed some fine samples
of her craft at the Ethel Stephens exhibi-
tion, must have the courage of a cage full
of lions to tackle the sculptor’s trade in this
busy land of ever-sprouting bricks and mor-
tar.
Contralto Eileen Boyd has not—at date
of writing—revoked her firm intention of re-
turning this way with the Peter Dawson
concert co. The Australian girl has let down
a tuck or two in her frock since the days
when her childish lilt floated over the foot-
lights of the J.C.W. Co. She seems to have
melted the heart or pen of the usually cold
London critic into an Indian summer warmth
of adjectives, such as “rich contralto voice,”
“artistic singing,” etc. Dawson, the strap-
ping young Adelaide baritone who has done
so well in England, brings out a soprano,
tenor, pianist, and an odd man out, who is
headlined as “entertainer at the piano.”
Assistant Under-Sec. of State for Colonies
Just leaves Melbourne for Canberra on May
1. After looking up the capital city’s
waterholes, and rushing round the firearms
of the Duntroon cadet, he arrives here on
Sunday. May 3. He stays at Govern-
ment House for a week or .10 days.
Intervals are to be spent in tearing
up and down this State in company with
Governor Strickland and Premier Holman.
The Assistant Under-Sec. visitor will be offi-
cially hand-fed by the Mayor and other
luminaries during the snare moments of his
sojourn here. Sir Charles Lucas —Hart-
mann Just’s predecessor —had a look round
the fringe of this continent in 1909, and it
never did us any harm. Also it did him a
lot of good.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORLAN.

For the Americans to force their brand of
order upon Mexico may or mav not be wise.
But it is alwavs wise to seek inspiration
from the royal brand of wisdom —King
George IY. topnotch whisky.*
There is on view at Anthony Hordern &
Sons’ art gallery In the. big building on Brickfield
Hill an interesting collection of artists’ proof etch-
ings and engravings printed in colors by members
of the Society of Graver-Printers, London. Each print

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 27, 1914.
My Dear Yariue, —
A swallow flying northward tells me that
■Hobart is cleaning up the bones of the last
■of its flock of summer visitors. The season
started late, but the warm weather on the
mainland crowded the old city before
Christmas. If the Hobart visitor really
went to see things he would either hold on
■or hold off till the autumn. George Sydney
Jones, the perennial president of the Sydney
Architects’ Association, is still recuperating
there, and is sweeping the gold of the pop-
lars, the copper of the oaks, the blood-red
■of the maples and the emerald of the re-
freshed hillsides into his artistic vision. He
knows the Rhine and other famous European
■ditches, and is not prepared to put the Der-
went Valley second to any of them. His
comments are mostly ecstatic adjectives.
Old Hobart is, however, too weary for
■ecstasies. If it ever had any civic sense it
has mislaid the goods. It seems a city with-
out pride and destitute of ambition. In
20 years it has merely grown 20 years older.
Launceston ought to teach it a lesson.
But then Launceston is not afraid of the
terrible bogey of Socialism. One result is
that Launceston has the most noiseless tram
service in the Commonwealth, owned by the
municipality, while Hobart has the most
racketty, laid down by Private Enterprise.
Twenty years ago the “express” between
the two cities was a joke, and it maintains
its old humor. It does the 100 miles or so
uncomfortably in nearly six hours, and is
so bustled that it cannot stop for more than
11 minutes at the one refreshment station —
where, by the way, refreshments are not
sold. Every Maoriland long-distance train
has a dining car. That might be too much
to expect of Tasmania’s one express, still
even the little Speck should by this time be
civilised enough to provide lavatory accom-
modation on its train-de-luxe. Another 20
years and even that may be done.
The G.-G. Denmans give a final evening
party at Federal Government House this
(Thursday) evening, when they will feed
the hungry and drink the thirsty for the
last time. Men are commanded to appear
in any uniforms they possess. A few tight
Windsor uniforms will have one more air-
ing and try the tempers of their wearers.
(The owners of Windsor costumes here-
abouts have invariably grown too fat for
them.)
The Gaud Mayor and his Gaudier Mayor-
ess are sending forth the gilded pasteboard
which is worshipped almost as fervently
as the Golden Calf. This time it is for a
reception at the Town Hall to say good-bye
to the departing Denmans. All the people
who think they have been overlooked are
waving their arms at the Town Hall and
suffering severely from “Hey!” fever.
Mrs. Shipman gave a tea party to her
friends, at the Oriental Hotel, last week;
and her friends proved to be so numerous
that they made a long, dwindling double row
right down the length of the Winter Garden.
Placed end to end, they might have made a
long, single line out to Prahran. But these
things simply can’t be attempted when
people are in their store clothes. The host-
ess’s cute little turban and Titian hair
(“Titian” hair is sometimes known as “red,”
“ginger” or “Fire Brigade”) showed above
the table at the head (I said they were
drinking tea, didn’t I?), and Miss Pauline
Bindley, guest-in-chief, looked a demure
little mouse alongside in grey. (Miss Bind-
ley, though thoroughly mouse-like, was also
above the table. This, I think, showed com-
mendable self-restraint.) She takes her
singing honors very meekly. The good-look-
ing Paul Dufault was not on view, and some
female worshippers who had come to yearn
wilted terribly with disappointment. Plalf
the women at the table were inside the new-
est veils, which cover the nose and mouth
thickly, but have a fine, transparent mesh
for the eyes to look through. They are
fascinating things, but awkward for taking
refreshment. The guests managed by lower-
ing the fine upper section and wearing them
as immature false beards while they ate and
drank. Fashionable women achieve some
weird effects.
The University students took possession
of the Druids’ Easter Bazaar (Yes, it is
just over!) at the Exhibition, the other
evening, and spent a carefully wild night.
The inevitable fancy-dress procession pre-
ceded a concert at the far end of the Great
Barn, to an audience of open-mouthed flap-
pers. The going home was the carefully-
uproarious climax to a tremendous evening.
The little boys climbed on ton of cable trams,
where they balanced like apes and advertised
themselves as perfect suburban devils, by
howling like hyenas and making discordant
noises. About one in every hundred lads
does this sort of thing because it is natural
to him; the other 99 carefully follow his
lead because it is the sort of thing they
think the public expect of them. And they
are a lamentable sight while they are at
it. The University reveller is a slavishly-
conventional little soul.
The Pankhurst girl is to talk on May 4.
Vida Goldstein is steering her bark, and the
prices are 55., 3s. and 2s. The young lec-
turer on forcible feeding, or whatever it is
she intends to discourse about, looks as meek
and unimpressive as a weary nursemaid ; but
there may be some fire and eloquence inside
her. Muriel Matters, the Australian girl
who got herself chained to a grille or pork
chop in England, and who lectured here some
years ago, arrived with a theatrical flourish
of paragraphs. She had a strong liking for
the limelight; in fact, she was on the stage
in London with Brough for a brief spell
many years ago. Her minor parts, however,
did not satisfy her like her militant suffra-
getting. Someone who knew Muriel in her
most youthful days describes her at that
time as a fanatical highbrow or attempted
brain buster, and relates how she went to
Norway to study Ibsen on the spot, then,
back in London, expounded Ibsen and his
spots in any tony drawing-room that could
stand that sort of thing.
The Repertory Theatre Ball, about which
the Best People are beginning to get very
excited, is to be run on good theatrical lines.
When the double vice-regal party arrives, a
procession of sets will be formed and will
follow the Governors and their suites round
the room for the benefit of the half-guinea
gazers in the balcony. A dance, in which
the set grouping will be preserved, is the
next turn, and, after that, 10 tableaux will
be shown. Ellen Terry and Lauder will
both attend, and every other theatrical per-
sonage who can rush to the scene will be
there.
An American named Wilson gave a supper
party at the Francatelli, on a recent Satur-
day night. The Ada Reeve Cottons were
there, also Daisy Jerome. Towards the
early hours the party indulged in some
music. Ada Reeve, suddenly becoming con-
scious of an appreciative but non-paying
audience of supper people, suggested a col-
lection, and £5O was gathered in. One man
offered a sovereign for a Jerome kiss, and,
the lady agreeing, the buyer hugged her for
about three minutes like a grizzly bear.
Daisy was almost weeping with rage at his
greediness; but Ada saved the situation
more or less by paying another sovereign
and giving her ruffled companion a gentle
sisterly kiss in exchange. The lovely spec-
tacle of one actress osculating another with
butterfly lightness, in order to show the
rude world what a small amount of kiss
ought to be expected from any actress for
twenty shillings, carried its moral with it.
The rule of the stage world, of course, is to
treat every man who spends £1 on stage-
struckery as a fool who deserves nothing for
his money, but should be earnestly encou-
raged to increase his expenditure for
a diminishing reward. The awful man at
the cafe didn’t apparently understand the
rudiments of Thespian philosophy. He was
estimating the value of actresses’ kisses by
his recollection of some pretty typewriter
who had let him lean up against her sweet
young cheek on payment of two pairs of
gloves.
The small monocled Major who walked
in the shadow of tall lan Hamilton was the
scion or scone of a “great” house. He wore
his honors meekly when on duty. In fact,
he was so mouselike when in the shade of
his military lion that acquaintances here
wondered if he could be a mere younger
son. Others stoutly maintained that he was
a cute and knowledgeable officer who was
hiding his brains under a mask. However,
when off duty, the meek Major used to come
out of his shell or mask, and go to Savoy
suppers and be as gay as a bird. That was
when the length of his pedigree and the
height of his incomparable family tree used
to be revealed.
John Pym, of Burgoyne’s, is to be married
on May 20 to Beatrice Derham, daughter of
F. T. Derham, of Kew, who wears the prefix
“Hon.” because of his ancient connection
with the House that was never known to
“Go Hon.”
Mitchell Tovell, a University young man
of hereabouts, is off to Launceston as head-
master of the local Scotch College. It is
cheering to see a local man getting a local
billet. The bigger schools seem to run
abroad when they are short of a head.
Tobacco-importer Ivauffmann died sud-
denly the other day in his sunny middle
age. The bereavement will sadden a joyous
occasion. Mrs. Kauffmann had left
daughter Marjorie in England preparing for
her wedding, while she returned hither to
fetch her husband and a young schoolgirl
daughter.
A lady of some social importance, now
gone Londonwards, lately boasted that she
had acquired for a mere eighteenpence a
nair of valuable antique candlesticks. The
guileless vendor was a Victorian farmer who
thought the relics were made of tin.
The little transaction or job is really
nothing to be proud of. But your true col-
lector’s conscience works that way. He or
she is as ruthless as the little stay-at-home
stamp maniac, who prays for remote revolu-
tions and foreign rapine and slaughter, just
because such catastrophes will probably make
some existing stamp rare and valuable and
bring a new variety on the market.
Maidie Henty and Ronald Cumming sur-
vived a fashionable wedding, on April 23,
at St. John’s, Toorak; also the usual cham-
pagne-confetti scramble, which occurred
later, at Glen Druid, the Toorak house. The
Hentys are the most ancient blue-bloods, so
to speak, of Victorian squattocracy. Their
camp fires are historical and their whales
(they used to shear whales, didn’t they?)
blow in all the records. The Cummings are
a good second up the family tree, and the
alliance set going the tongues of many grey-
headed friends of the respective families.
The Albert Millers’ son William and
Frances Hogg, of Toorak, are the latest
pair to invite congratulations. Some dav in
the not too distant fut-ure, when the birds
are singing and every living creature is mak-
ing its own appointed and appropriate row,
they will hire a reverend gent, to do a sum
in holy subtraction and make them one.
Beautiful as it is to be two, they think it
would be still more exquisite to be only half
as numerous.
Doris Madden gave a sparkling classical
piano recital at the Auditorium on the 23rd,
when a select few of the people who like to
drink mostly with their ears wajidered in,
while another lot went over the road to be
spellbound by Alexander Watson. Doris is
a gentle little girl in spectacles; but she
possesses the strong Carreno brand of arm,
and her spectacles hardly ever get in the
way when she is playing. The composers
dealt with were Beethoven, Chopin, Schu-
mann, Rosenthal and Senabine —a dainty
dish to set before an audience that might
have been a lot more numerous than it was.
Miss Jule Layton, a contralto with a fine
voice and a foggy enunciation, allowed Doris
to have a rest at intervals.
Sheldon, the cherubic-faced conductor of
the Harry Lauder orchestra, began to make
inquiries as soon as he got here about an
unknown uncle. (No! On second thoughts,
he couldn’t have been entirely unknown —
he must at least have been a suspected
uncle) Well, this suspected uncle was
supposed to be pruning a vine or two at
Lyndoch, near Adelaide. He had retired
from the stage, and (to make a long uncle
short) he turned out to be Clarence Leu-
mane, the tenor who was with Williamson
and Musgrove in their early days of man-
agement. He played Faust to Nellie Stew-
art’s Marguerite.
Alexander Watson, the tall, severe-look-
ing reciter with the everlasting memory and
the genius for spellbinding, unwound John
Masefield’s remarkable poem of drink and
salvation, “The Everlasting Mercy,” at one
sitting without prompting or notes. This
feat was performed for the first time at the
Athenaeum the other evening, and, at time
of writing, he is still at it. Watson quite
fearlessly threw the primitive, forceful thing
at the heads and bare shoulders of a crowded
house. Some of the intensely respectable
suburban squaws who shudder when they
overhear loud, coarse toilers urging tired
horses uphill with such language, got the
shock of their tame, well-ordered lives when
the vivid Universal Adjective hurtled across
the polite footlights. Daughters of the said
squaws shot doubtful side-glances of dismay,
then, seeing that mother still retained her
wonted air of massive calm (save for a
slight dilation of the eyes), giggled fur-
tively one to the other. For myself, I
thought of the neat verse attached to Max
Beerbohm’s clever cartoon of John, the
Plain-Speaker:—
A swear-word in a rustic slum,
A simple swear-word is to some,
To Masefield something more.
From my Adelaide correspondent: —
Our new Excellencies have settled down grimly to
toil. If the Empire bursts —well, the tragedy will
be due to no derelictions from duty on their part.
On Wednesday they went to a picture show, to view
on the screen their own arrival the previous Satur-
day. Then there were the Oakbank Races on
Easter Monday; on Thursday a levee and the annual
dinner of the St. George Society at the Grand
Central; on Friday the theatre; and on Saturday a
bulldog show, a bazaar and intercollegiate sports.
Do you wonder that a Yiceroy’s Union is being dis-
cussed, which after affiliation with the Trades Hall,
aims to come out boldly for shorter hours and less
harassing diversity of toil?
By the way, talking of the proposed new union,
Sir Day Bosanquet was paid an instalment of over-
time, voluntarily, by the State last week. It took
the form of having him made a life-member of the
R. A. and H. Society.
Did you know that Lady Galway is a Catholic?
Even so, my dear. And last Sunday she went to St.
Xavier’s Cathedral to mass. As the entire village is
Methody to all intents and purposes, nurtured on
Hocking and cradled in the War Cry, so to speak,
I don’t quite know what we are going to do about
it. The only hope is, it seems to me, that when she
sees how holy we all are she may become converted.
The Winterega Murphys are back from England.
With them are two of Mr. Murphy’s sisters, who are
facing the perils and hardships of Australian bush
life on his station out Broken Hill way. Who said
that the gallant pioneer spirit was dead? At Mr.
Murphy’s station they have to go without iee some-
times for days at a time!
Tattersall’s Autumn Meeting drew a big crowd to
Morphettville on Saturday. Furs, those sure har-
bingers of winter, were much in evidence. My heart
bled for the widowed rabbit and the motherless rab-
bitlet. This happened regularly between each race.
However, the assemblage wasn’t entirely suggestive of
some vast rabbit-battue or coney-drive. Here and
there might be seen some truly noble stoles and
muffs and a few opulent coats.
Most of those who weren’t at the races on Satur-
day or at the swearing-in of the new Ex. were at
the St. Peter’s College oval, watching the sports.
One Pellew captured jboth the lOOvds. championship
and the College Cup, and had various medals given
him by the C.J.’s lady.
The appointment of Mr. F. W. Young as Agent-
General surprised most people. His was about the
only name that hadn’t been mentioned as a “cer-
tainty” for the billet since Mr. Kirkpatrick’s term
of office expired. He—Mr. Young—was born at
Blyth in the Lower North in January of ’76, and at
the age of 13 was despatched to Prince Alfred’s
College. He served his articles in Mr. W. Pope’s
office, and earned his LL.B. degree. In ’99 he set
up on his own account, as senior partner of Young
and Newland, and in 1902 was elected as member for
Stanley. At the next elections he was defeated, but
got in at a by-struggle for Wooroora, which seat
he has held ever since. His attractive wife is a
daughter of the late John Darling.
Sir Hartmann Just arrived from W.A. by the
Warilda on Sunday, and left by Monday’s express
for Melbourne. The Adelaide Club and the Wavs
kept a kindly eye on him while he was here.
Mr. Charles Reade —of the Garden Cities and Town
Planning Association —arrived by the Orvieto on
Saturday, and was met by various citizens at the
Mayor’s parlor on Wednesday afternoon. I don’t
know what occurred in the parlor. I can but dimly
suspect.
Kathleen, younger daughter of the John Gordons,
is engaged to one of the Alan Campbell clan. The
bridegroom-elect is a nephew of Chief Justice Wav’s.
He went with the Fifth Contingent to the Boer War,
and since then has not been much in South Aus-
tralia, mining engineering having called him far
afield.
A “pauper lunatic” ordered t’other day two motor-
cars, a motor-lorry, a motor-cycle and a side-car.
He acted entirely without mala fides, being under
the delusion that he would receive £21,000 during
the day. Thus was he entirely happy during at
least 12 hours. In fact, he was happy even longer
than that, as he expressed contemptuous disbelief of
the Law’s exasperated assurance that he had no
money nor any prospects of the same, and was borne
off to durance still satisfied that all was well with
his banking account. There are points about being
mad, if you only regard it philosophically.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

“disculapius”,: In ancient Greece - they used to-
erect temples Over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. We moderns are ho less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of, a mineral water—only our
methods differ somewhat.; ' For instance, up at the
famous Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to .the
Earl of Hopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
f’Figaro”; Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in N.Q.,
but close beside it the trep' whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa.
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of “Helidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night's rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, “Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom' is always to
SPLIT IT WITH
HELIDON.
ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Moana”: An industry peculiar to the
Southern Isles of Maoriland is mutton-birding. This
is in the hands of the Maoris, who go down to the
islands from The Bluff and Riverton during March to
pursue the helpless mutton-bird in his windy lair.
This strange creature (it can hardly be called a
bird) inhabits burrows in the ground, and has to be
dug out before it can be captured and killed. It is
a silly, brainless sort of thing, and doesn’t put up
A TALE WITHOUT WORDS.
any kind of a fight. The birds, which mostly consist
of a blubbery kind of fat, are done up in parcels in
Maori mats after being plucked, cleaned and smoked.
They are considered a great delicacy by the Maoris,
and sell readily wherever there are native settle-
ments, especially in the North Island. The wild
Scawteh sailors of southern M.L., when they’ve taken
on board an excessive cargo of whusky, have a habit
of consuming fried mutton-bird for supper, just as
the inebriated individual in more civilised parts eats
“cray.” Fried mutton-bird is not exactly like any-
thing else on this planet, being a cross between tough
wild goose, smoked wild pig and dried shark fried
in blubber.
“Alo.vsius”: “Leo Lear’s” par. in B.
26/2/’l4 on the bushman’s food and water supply
merely shows the average man’s adaptability to cir-
cumstances. This last dry spell we were camped
near an alleged creek. There being no proper super-
vision, camps were pitched anywhere—near the
waterhole for preference, so as the water would not
have to be carried too far. With no rain worth men-
tioning for a couple of months the water became
brackish and discolored from the leaves and decayed
vegetation. The track leading down to the hole was,
to say the least, in a very insanitary condition. The
only complaint registered for a time, was that the
local storekeeper’s tea was of indifferent qualify,. Then
a couple of the men developed dysentery, and
another was sent off to the hospital and subsequently
died there of typhoid. We decided after this to
approach the Department for a rrv water supply,
and are now getting our water cai d from a river
five miles away. The new brew seems good enough,
but the other day I took a stroll along the river
and found a nigger camp on its banks under half a
mile above whence - our water is drawn, and was
informed by one of the locals that Mary and her
offspring may be seen disporting themselves in the
shallows at any hour of the day. So far I have not
been inconvenienced by the taste of Mary in my
tea, but perhaps that’s because I’ve become used to it.
* * * «
“Vakuta”: Thu remarks of “McNorth”
(22/l/’l4) re the barbarous treatment meted out
to animals by natives both in Australia and Papua,
are hideously true. Take the Papuan’s methods with
turtles. If the unhappy reptile, or whatever it is,
happens to be caught some time before a feast it
is dragged up to the village and left on its Ifack
exposed to the heat of the sun, and so on for an
indefinite time. While slowly dying of starvation it
bores a deep circle in the sand with its agonised
flappers. Its eyes fester. After a few days it goes
blind. Its parched mouth gapes for water. This
grisly spectacle may be witnessed anywhere at any
time in (he coastal regions of the Territory and
Papua. The pig has a swifter but almost as dread-
ful end. He is dragged howling to the feast. A
spear is poked slowly into his anatomy. He howls
louder than ever. This noise is sweet music to the
natives, who join in and keep time blithely with the
infernal uproar. This infamy often lasts for hours.
All such game as pigeons, ducks, etc., are villain-
ously treated by the natives, who show no mercy to
anything alive. Surely the Government officials who
swarm in these parts could instruct the brown and
black devils of their districts in the elements of
humanity, and, if the said fiends proved recalcitrant,
deal with them accordingly?
Bad Australian” (from Argentine) : It
was in Buhdaberg in the bad old days before the
nigger was fired out that I first struck a really
flourishing alfalfa patch. I saw that patch cut four
times, in five months, and it struck me at thq time
that alfalfa was a darned fine crop to grow on the
Burnett flats. Since then I have found that it is a
topnotch vegetable in almost any
old place, provided the soil gives
the roots a chance to get down. I
have met it at intervals from sea-
level to the summit of the Andes,
and through all the varying cli-
matic changes which altitude gives
this plant flourishes more per-
sistently than lantana in Queens-
land. In some parts of Bolivia
alfalfa is grown on drained salt
marshes, irrigated by the most
virulently brackish water on the
planet. This result has been
attained by selecting the best seed
and planting on land with a low
percentage of salt in it, using the
seed resulting from that crop on
land containing a trifle more salt,
and repeating the process until a
plant has_ been evolved that would
sprout on the pillar of salt that
testified to the inquisitiveness of
the late Mrs. Lot, of Judaea. The
staple fodder of this continent is
alfalfa. Oaten or wheaten hay
is practically unknown, and the
amount of solid work the South
American mule, burro and horse
get away with on a few kilos of
alfalfa per day is about the worst
kind of a surprise to folks who use ,
grain-fed animals. The highly - -
nutritive qualities of, alfalfa, and
its wide range, from sea-level to
ll,oooft. above, and possibly
higher, give it an easy first place
as the most valuable fodder plant
yet known. Australia has thou-
sands of square miles of ideal
alfalfa land, and it is up to the
man with the hoe to give the
light kind of “lucerne” the serious attention it
deserves.
The long arm of coincidence got a man
named Evans on the nor’-west coast of Tas. recently.
Years or so ago one Meaves, camped at the Arthur
River, was killed by a splinter, from a falling tree.
The splinter pierced his heart and dented the silver
watch he was carrying. Eighteen months after Meaves’s
death his widow became engaged to Evans and gave
him the dead man’s watch. Evans was camped on the
Arthur River within 100 yards of the camp of Meaves.
Evans was killed also by a splinter from a falling tree,
and a flying fragment Of wood again dented the
watch case.

“Moaria”: Wandering through Maoriland
I struck a fine sample of a “laundress” in one of the
famous, Wowser-bossed, “dry” townships. He was a
fine, strapping, able-bodied sort of chap, and he- was
making the soapsuds fly at the washtub when I
dropped in with the collars. I discovered in the
course of conversation that he and his wife had run
the local laundry practically alone for some years.
“I used to collect the duds, take home the clean stuff,
collect the cash and generally chase business while,
the missus did the washing and ironing,” Bill ex-
plained. “But a couple of years- ago she got bad,
and I had to take on r the whole job for a while.
After that, no more-of the business side for me. I
shoved the missus on to that. She’s better can-
vasser than me. I couldn’t stand' it, anyhow. Scrap-
ping over a penn'orth of collars wasn’t in my line.”
I met the "business end of this-strange partnership a
few days later, and a ’smart, busiffessdike little
woman she was. Thy last I'saw of Bill he was puff-
ing contentedly at his pipe as he stood at the wash-
tub soaping a shirt.

OUR AVIATING RIVERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A most remarkable feature is that the Belubula
River has not flown since November last, and it is
now, and has been for months, nothing hut a bed of
sand. —-Sydney P.T.. 13/4/’l4.
There are thousands of rivers all over the earth,
Like the Amazon, Nile, Orinoco and Forth,
lloang-Ho, Irrawaddy, St. Lawrence and Seine —
lo mention the rest is too much of a strain.
But large, small, long, sluggish or swift though they
be,
As they rush, or meander, along to the sea.
There’s never a river among the whole lot
That can do things like some of the rivers we'cc got!
Their ways are surprising! For instance, some years
The Federal water-supply disappears!
And even the experts can’t very well say
How long the Molonglo is likely to stay;
Or whether the turbid, okl Murrumbidgee
Will stop in the back-blocks or leave for the sea;
IV hile even the Cotter—once reckoned a cert.—
ilas cleared out for weeks when its feelings were
hurt!
Like genii our rivers assume every size—
The mighty Ashburton at times nearly dries ;
While, several days later, it swirls in a tide,
To the Indian Ocean, full thirty miles wide!
And the old-maid-like Murray will shrink in its bed,
Or rage in a tantrum, by snow waters fed !
Oh, the rivers are few and remarkably queer
That just stick to rivering all round the year!
And, queerest of all, you may take it from me,
On the word of the accurate Sydney D.T.,
Is the River Belubula, known near and far
As the Champion Flier of rivers that are 1
But the river’s turned sulky—and no one knows
why,
For, though able to do it, it simply won't fly !
Sonic say its refusal is just due to spite
At the fast-flying stream of the Dryberra Site.
(Oh, clearly that must be the reason all right!)
N.s.w.

“Jeroi”: Writing of N. Coast (N.S.W.)
water vines, “Gil Gunnia” (B. 5/3/’l4) says the
young vine is best. Have had a good many drinks
from water vines, and always found that the stouter
the vine the bigger the drink, and just as good.
Know three kinds of water vine on N.C.—wild grape
(the best), blood vine and another that I have no name
for. It's not much good, anyway. Have taken more
water than I could drink from three feet of blood
vine. These vegetables grow to about six inches
thickness. Have heard them blamed for poisoning
cattle, but I don’t believe the yarn. But for water
properties I have had as good from the Maoriland
water vine (black lawyer).
The latest lunacy to be imported into
Maoriland is heather frae Scotlund. It has been
planted in the wild country around the volcanoes
Tongariro and Ruapehu, and is reported to be growing
just about as furiously as every other imported weed.
Already a number of wild sportsmen are beginning
to yell loudly for grouse, the shootable bird that
flourishes in heather. When the Scawtch shrub starts
to and leaps all over the Borrowing Isles,
there will he a different variety of yell. It will be
for the importation of some kind of wild animal
that lives oh heather. The lion and the tiger,
together with most of the other animals of the Ark,
will be dragged to Maoriland eventually.

“Thode F.”: Re “Q.1.8.’5” query as to the
making of jerked meat (B. 2/4/’l4). The late
R. L. Moore, manager of Angledool station, on the
Narran, for nearly 40 years, speaking of his jackeroo
times on the Paroo, told the writer that in the days
ol the cattle slump it was more profitable to send
the tallow packed in the hides than travel the beasts
to market. Apparently the lean meat was sun-dried, as
he spoke of the great number of brown hawks that
swooped at the strips fastened on lines.- The black
boys used to dip two-thirds of a raw strip in the
tallow-melting vats and throw it high from the
end of a pointed stick, to be grabbed at in mid-air
by the hawks. They dropped it with a shriek as
their talons closed on the sizzling part. Wonder
whether Joe Cook or Andy Fisher will evolve some-
thing as effective to make the ’Murkan eagle leggo
our meat ?
Nuki” : Te Renata, of Waimahana, went
down to Auckland recently and returned to the
kainga with a watch. He consulted it on an average
once in 20 seconds, but the base rumor was circu-
lated that Renata didn’t know how to read the
time. Of course, our local humorist got hold of
the yarn, and when he met his nut-colored brother
at the store the proceedings went off like this:
“Hallo, Renata, what’s the time?” Renata gazed
fixedly at the timepiece. “Half-past,” he said
cautiously. “Half-past what?” Renata gazed again.
“JMo, trenny to,” he corrected. “Well, 20 to what?”
A pause, while Renata had another bad 10 seconds
with his latest purchase. “Taihoa,” he said
guardedly, “nine minutes.” “But that’s no use,
Renata. Nine minutes—what else?” Renata closed
the watch case with a furious click. “Ko to town,”
he shouted, “an’ puy a plurry watch yourself!”
“Bullaganang”: Peter was an outlaw,
lie was a nigger- with a knowledge of white men’s
ways, who had gone with the warrigals, and was up
to any mischief, from spearing a lone white to kill-
ing and eating station cattle. Certain N. Territory
stations were in mortal fear of Peter. One evening
a trusty nig came up and told the boss, “By Gripes,
boss, Peter been come.” The boss passed out a Snider
and a few cartridges. “You savvy?” he said,
lominy grinned and went off. Some time afterwards
there was a shot fired, and later on Tommy brought
the rifle back. “How is Peter?” said the boss.
“Peter dead,” said Tommy. “Well,” ordered the
boss; “you put him in river for the alligators first
thing to-morrow.” Early next morning Tommy came ’
up a queer expression on his face. “Did you
put him in the river?” asked the boss. “Yowi,”
replied Tommy, “by Cripes, boss, me been makem
mistake. That pheller not Peter.”

“Stone”: Met in the bush, the other day, a
peculiar-looking reptile. It was apparently a cross
between an iguana and a frill lizard. It was about
2ft. 6in. in length, and four or five inches in the beam,
and had the tapered body and tail, with the dark and
yellow markings of the “go.” The head was that of
the frill lizard, with an extraordinary large, yellow-
lined mouth and only half a frill, and that under
the chin, like the throat-weed of an ancient Hayseed.
A new-ehum from Gawkshire, who is a mountain of
information, was with me at the time, and he
could throw no light on the subject. He said that
he had never even heard of the bally thing, so I
consider it must be an entirely new thing. I’ve
named it the frillana.
“Oimai”: Recent legends of homing cats
and dogs in The Bulletin move me to tell of
Bushi’s cockatoo, who once dwelt in a village on the
banks of the Old Swanee (or something like that)
River in Papua. He was very popular with the
miners and traders of the Yodda, and whenever Bushi
accepted a retainer to paddle a storekeeper’s boat up
the river, Bauwa always went as pilot. His job was to run
up and down the boat-awning when a stiff rapid was
reached, and yell “Bara!” (paddle) “Bara, you blue-
stone black blanks.” His master came to an un-
timely end and Bauwa was sold into slavery by an
ungrateful community. His new master, a Govern-
ment official, took him 40 miles away as the crow very
rarely flies. A week later he was reported as back at his
old job of pilot, with his expletive powers much
strengthened by his holiday. It may be as well to
say that any homing instinct illustrated in this tale
should be credited to the nigger who travelled 40
miles and back, across country, to steal the bartered
fowl and restore him to the bosom of his family.
“Gouger”: Some five years ago we were
sitting round the fires at the “old town,” Lightning
Ridge, when from a distant camp came the cry,
“Horse down a shaft.” The miners’ horses often
toppled down the scattered shafts, though whether
with the idea of doing a bit of opal-gouging on their
own, or of merely committing suicide, I don’t know.
Soon a motley crowd of 61 men, various dogs, many
opinions, some lanterns, certain slush lamps and a
huge rope scrambled along to the orifice where the
animal was wedged 20ft. down. “Sailor” climbed
down and managed to knot the rope around the
horse’s belly. Before he could climb out, some fool
yelled, “Every man on the rope.” Now the sudden
and combined strength of 59 men, lugging at a rope
which is sawing against hard rock is pretty con-
siderable. The rope parted. One end caught
“Sailor,” who was almost out of the shaft, across
the neck and sent him flying down on to the un-
fortunate horse. The last man on the rope’s end
followed his heels down a hidden shaft behind him,
while 58 men sat down hard and sudden. Only one
man stood up. He was the usual loafer, who had not
been pulling at all. When the excitement calmed a
bit, and the 59th man was saved, and the 61st man
had ceased loafing, we hauled the 60th man
(“Sailor”) from the shaft. Save for the loss of a
penn’orth of skin he was none the worse, though
to hear him talk you’d have thought he was dead. It
was then found that the horse was defunct. Our good
intentions had pulled the rope almost through him.
With a dismal feeling of failure we filled in the
shaft.
“John P.”: The necessity of culling the
various herds of deer in Maoriland has been respon-
sible for introducing a new and well-paid profession.
A number of expert shots have taken up this work
and there’s more of it than they can do. The herds
in different parts are being carefully culled of all
weedy animals and stags with malformed heads. In
the Otago district alone over 700 creatures were taken
out this season. The cullers also report on • the
herds generally, and on the number of rifles the
country will carry. Anyone mad on deer-stalking,
who is a good rifle shot and fond of an outdoor life,
would find the new profession an ideal one.

CIGARETTE CARDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He believed the craze for the collection of cigar-
ette cards and other emblems had more to do with
the early assumption of the cigarette-smoking habit
by juveniles than anything else. They all knew, he
said, what a hold this craze got upon many small
children. They had seen them stick people up in the
street and ask for cards. —Speech before the Confer-
ence of Parents and Citizens’ Associations.
“Got a cigarette card, Mister?’’
Down the quiet of the street
1 beheld the dark words blister
Moral purpose with their heat.
'Twas a child of ten who spoke them
To a youth of twenty-one,
And my fancy seemed to yoke them
To a hell on earth begun.
Yes, I saw the wicked glitter
In the urchin’s waiting eyes,
And I brooded on the bitter
Days that would for him arise.
Goaded by his fatal passion—
’Twas the cards that did the trick —
I beheld the pale, green fashion
In which smoking made him sick.
I beheld him swiftly growing
Into manhood tall and fair,
Blowing, blowing, always blowing
Cigarette smoke on the air;
And I marked the changing season
When his sins were purple ripe,
And, in awful scorn of reason,
He began to smoke a pipe.
Farther down the track I watched him.
Counted every match he lit —
No kind angel came and snatched him
From the burning of the pit.
Oh, his sins were like the ocean!
T observed him from afar,
When he got his first promotion,
Waving wide a cheap cigar.
Thus to early degradation,
Sin and shame and moral blight,
Go the children of the nation
Keeping various weeds alight.
Oh, my heart is full of sorrow !
On my waistcoat drops a tear
When I think of the to-morrow
Of our infants small and dear.
“Got a cigarette card, Mister?”
Oh, the anguish and the pain
Of those words that scorch and blister
When I hear their sound again!
Well I know that Hell is stoking
Her red furnace hotter yet
For the urchin who is smoking
His first, sad, sick cigarette.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
