THE INSUFFICIENT ARMY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little side-drums were hammering at the
walls of the big buildings, splintering echoes
from them, and the brasses were chanting a
song that was never to be forgotten after
that day, and the reeds were singing a
melody that, from the beginning of things,
was fated to be the last melody some men
would hear.
Heeds and brasses and drums—they went
along the street, knowing well that Tragedy
marched to the music.
Someone said: “Who are they?”
And someone said: “The Last Regiment!”
To the lilt of the band the Regiment
swung along. In column of fours the men
marched, but not with the shouldered arms
that look so nice on the moving pictures.
They carried their rifles at the trail, and the
bulging bandoliers evidenced that they were
“out for keeps.”
The civilian understands that somewhere
in the books it is written that shoulder
should touch shoulder on the march, but on
that drab, fateful day it was not so much
the touching shoulder that counted as the
steady wrist that could hold the rifle to its
purpose, and the arm that could put the
bayonet to its destined end.
Someone said: “Where are they going?”
And someone said : “Out!”
On balconies women were holding up little
babies and trying to show them the men who
were marching down the desolated street.
A woman asked: “Who are they?”
And someone replied: “The Last Regi-
ment !”
So they swung along, with the field-service
helmet cocked rakishly over the right eye,
because the marksman has an almost finicky
fondness for his right eye, and doesn’t like
even the sun to look at it. And the officers
walked easily alongside, because they knew,
they could trust the Regiment, and that
they were all booked for the same terminus.
And someone asked: “Who are they?”
And someone replied: “The Last Regi-
ment !”
So in the gloaming of that rainy, wintry,
shot-driven day they swung along in the
plain, drab uniform of the Australian infan-
try, and every man knew that he could never
come back, and every man and woman who
were shouting “GoD-speed” to the Regiment
knew that not one man could ever come
back, and every man who carried the trailed
rifle knew that all the people knew that he
could never come back.
And in the distracted crowd again some-
one asked: “Who are they?”
And again someone replied: “The Last
Regiment!”
And the little side-drums were still ham-
mering. .. .hammering. But they didn’t
seem to be despondent.
P. T. FREEMAN.
The Excessive Cost of Justice.
The writer got involved in an Equity suit
the other day, and one effect of the experi-
ence was to sheet home to him the fact that
in Australia, as elsewhere, the free born
citizen has a particularly thin chance of
getting justice unless he has sufficient money
to buy it. Consider the tremendous proce-
dure in this jurisdiction. The person who
considers that he has been wronged has first
to find out from a barrister, through a soli-
citor, whether he has been legally wronged.
If the aggrieved one is able to pay the bar-
rister’s fee, the latter directs his attention to
the matter, and in due course supplies his
opinion and collects the money. If the bar-
rister’s opinion is favorable, and the litigant
is solvent, the trouble begins forthwith. In
the jargon of the profession, the plain-
tiff files his Statement of Claim. Unname-
able perils surround this ceremony. The
selection of an unskilful or reckless claim-
drawer may mean ruin. Amidst the tense,
strained atmosphere that one notices when
the favorite is staggering home a winner by
a short head the claim is drawn. Later it is
filed—this being the job of the solicitor.
Three weeks pass. The assailed party
weighs in with his Statement of Defence.
The Statement of Defence may be either a
wild denial of every single allegation made
by the plaintiff—a denial that the plaintiff
has any cause of action ; that he ever had
any cause of action anywhere, at any time,
against any human being on earth; a
denial that he is sober, or alive, or dead, or
anything else —or it may take the form of a
cross-claim, or it may raise some issue that
was not anticipated by the plaintiff’s legal
advisers. In either of the two latter events
the entire cavalcade of litigants retraces its
steps and starts all over again. The State-
ment of Claim is amended. Another month
drifts by. The defendants reply to the
altered claim. If both parties are lucky
they will have won by this time to a point
where there is some likelihood of their getting
to actual business. A conference ensues.
Have you ever attended a legal conference,
gentle reader? It is a highly impressive
occurrence. There are gathered together the
K.C., his juniors, the solicitor, his expert
on the matter in hand, and, crouching worm-
like in a corner, the litigant. The moment
is a solemn one for all concerned —and more
particularly for the litigant—since, in the
event of both parties deciding to proceed,
briefs must presently be delivered. For many
a luckless person the deliverance of briefs
has represented the birth of insolvency. Up
to this stage, Costs have been a mere baga-
telle. The writer, happening to be a bar-
rister, and being represented, furthermore,
by friends, got so far by the outlay of a
paltry £lOO. Had he been a layman the
figure would have been at least £2OO. As a
matter of fact, he got no farther. At this
absorbing point a settlement ensued which
was satisfactory to all parties, and the en-
tire unpleasantness vanished as though, it
had never been. TTad it passed the brief-
delivering stage and got into Court, one side
or the other would have had to pay ulti-
mately for three K.O.’s, at so much down
apiece, and 25 guineas a day ; for a propor-
tionate number of juniors at so much down,
and so much per day ; and for the services,
during a period of about six months, of two
firms of solicitors —say, at a moderate figure,
£2500. How many people can afford to put
up a sum like that in defence of what they
consider to be their rights? Probably not
one in 500. The obvious moral is that the
procedure in Equity needs simplifying. It is
too vast a subject to be tackled casually at
the tail-end of a newspaper article. The
only point which is desired to be stressed
here is that Equity litigation is exclusively
a pastime for the more or less affluent, under
existing, conditions. This is an anomaly in a
State which claims to be democratic. It
is surely a hopeless condition of affairs when
justice can only be bought at a price which
about 98 per cent, of the populace are
unable to pay.
A Voice from South America.
“Bad Australian” to his countrymen:—
What's the matter with Australia that we don’t
hear anything about her military aviation corps, or
a world-beater on his own private ’plane?
This over-rated country (Argentina) and its people
are a one-horse proposition compared to Australia,
but they own a nice little bunch of warliawks, and
at the present moment the whole population is
shrieking “Viva” over the highest flier of them all,
engineer George Newberry* who has just raked in
the world’s record for altitude on his Morane-Saulnier
monoplane, Le Rhone motor, 100 kilos.
Newberry is the pioneer of Argentine aviation,
and has done some good work in this country, ter-
minating in the world’s record. He is also a most
excellent all-round athlete.
And it happened in this way:—
A few months ago a Chilian top-notcher named
Figueroa made an attempt to fly across the Andes,
from Santa Rosa do Los Andes, on the Chilian side,
to this hamlet (Mendoza), a distance of 240 kilo-
metres, following the course of the Transandjne Rail-
way.
The altitude of the Pass is 13,000 ft., so it was a
fairly tough proposition. Figueroa camped at the
summit for about a week, studying the winds, or,
rather, whirlwinds, as the air currents are very
much broken up by the surrounding peaks, some of
which are 4000 ft. or 5000 ft. higher than the pass.
He found that there was a fairly-calm time for
an hour or two in the early morning, and, after a
few trials over the city of Santa Rosa de Los Andes
he lit out for the summit. But, before he had
passed the 30-kilometer mark, he struck several
“holes” in the air, the last being a regular abyss.
He dropped like a shot pigeon, but his luck stuck to
him and he wasn’t irreparably damaged. 11 is
friends were not satisfied. Now he is awaiting the
arrival of the most up-to-date European machine that
dollars can buy. This was ordered by cable, and in
the meantime the Chilian Government (which is solid
on aviation) offers a whole cartload of legal tender
to the first Chilian aviator, military or civilian, who
gets across to Mendoza on his machine. Newberry
was in Europe when this occurred, so he had some-
thing special built under his own supervision, with
the idea of invading Chile before Figueroa got his
second wind. It was in making a trial with his new
’plane over the aviation grounds at El Palomar,
Buenos Aires, that he got away with the world’s
record. He did not propose to break anv record
when he started, but things panned out so well that
he kept going until he felt the absolute need of the
oxygen which he had left below, otherwise it is
quite possible he would have been climbing yet.
In any case he reached an altitude of 6200 metres,
and his friends now reckon that the Transandine
flight from Mendoza is a sure thing for Argentina.
But this scribe “dunno.” He has a very lntinpu
knowledge of the uncertain nature of the wmus
and air currents around the summit of the Ana
in these latitudes. The man who flies across t
Andes will have to do it at an altitude of 16,0001 -
or 17,000 ft., otherwise he is liable to strike a sn g
rounding a corner of one of the great peaks on n a
line of flight. .
Newberry has to Btudy the local conditions y
boiling his billy on the summit for a week or t •
and by that time Figueroa may have his ne
machine ready. In that case it is about even betti g
as to who gets there first. But perhaps the odorir -
ous old-man condor who sits in the seat of the exa
may be the only one in it at the finish. The con
is a bird the size of a ferry-boat, and it dwells P
among the Higher Criticism and eats horses.
“B.” writes about an English marriage
which is attracting quite a pile of attention .
Re George Cornwallis-West, second hsuband and
divorce of Lady Randolph Churchill, and the ne
(1914) husband of Mrs. Pat Campbell, actress. When
Lady Randolph, widow, decided to claim anu
accept the hand of the debonair George
W„ all London Society knew from a glance
at the Society almanacs that Lady Randolpn
was taking on a proposition some months the
junior of her illustrious son, the present First Lora Ol
the Admiralty. Now the cruel cable tells us that
after 13 years of legalised attachment George had
been so careless in handing out the things known as
conjugal rights that the lady was granted a divorce.
And, to top it all, comes the pronouncement that on
the very day the decree nisi became absolute George
quietly took unto himself as wife Mrs. Pat Campbell-
Mrs. Campbell is a glorious actress; though, like
most of us, she is now busy sparring, against that
insidious complaint known as “years.” She is- or
was, in her prime—-very like the equivalent of an
English Bernhardt —all soul and no calves.
You perhaps remember the Divine Sarah’* recep-
tion in Sydney, years ago, when, exposing a pair
of tan-colored spindle-shanks, she tripped off the
boat in the rain, making her way to the dais where
the late Dan O’Connor, then Postmaster-General, was
waiting officially to greet her as. “the greatest and
most illustrious bony-fidy riprisintitive of La Belly
Frongshay that has ivcr set foot on these terresthrial
shores.”
The late Pat Campbell was a dashing, dare-devil
officer, the kind of man who didn’t care a damn.
Handsome, fascinating and a real good sort, he was
out for death or glory. Well, the little nickel-plated
Boer bullet got him in the right place. His remains
were interred in the 0.R.C., near Kimberley. He
lived like a philosopher, and went to hi* death like
a sportsman.
Having got my military discharge in Pretoria, I
returned to London, and one murky, pitiless morn-
ing toward the end of 1902 I was told off by W. J.
Evans, editor of the Evening News, to see Mrs.
George Cornwallis-West at her town house just off
Piccadilly. My instructions were to inquire why a
certain monthly publication, of which she was
founder, proprietress and editress, had ceased to
exist. The publication was being issued to “sub-
scribers only” at £2 2s. per copy. It was rather
a cheeky job, but the editor knew that a quarter-
column from the again newly-wedded lady would be
worth reading. I was ushered into the presence by a
butler about 10.30 a.m. Mrs. Cornwallis-West, then
anything between 49 and 55, swept in, wearing a
dressing-gown. Looking all her years, she said, with
the Jerome lisp, that she really could not, under-
stand what her private affairs had to do with the
public. “Thstill,” y she continued, “if it ith nethethy
to thay thso, the failure of my production muthst be
attributed to want of proper appreciation.”
Just about this time George Cornwallis-West —
slim, fair and perfectly groomed and tailored —came
down to breakfast. He struck me as being a very
nice young man, who, in taking his matrimonial
plunge, missed the ’bus just by one generation. This
second dive is a bit better; though she can give
him—well, I should bet evens on 12 years ; or, take
five to four, 16. And no longer is the First Lera sf
the Admiralty supposed to call him “Father” 1

Eightieth Annual Report of the Directors of the Bank of Australasia,
With the Accounts for the Half-year to 13th October, 1913.
Presented to the Proprietors at the Annual General Meeting, held on Thursday, 26th March, 1911.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the proprietors the Balance-sheet as at 13th October last, with the Profit
Account for the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and
Colonial rates and taxes, and for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the Half-year amounted
to £220,976 15s. sd.
To this sum must be added £29,192 3s. 9d. brought forward from the previous Half-year, making
a total of £250,168 19s. 2d., out of which the Directors have declared a dividend for the Half-year
at the rate of 11 per cent, per annum, or £2 16s. per share, and a bonus at the rate of 3 per cent, per
annum, or 12s. per share, together £3 Bs. per share, free of income-tax.
The dividend and bonus, on the present capital of £2,000,000 (the new shares having been fully
paid up), will absorb £170,000, and the Directors have further appropriated £30,000 to the Reserve
Fund, and £20,000 in reduction of the cost of bank premises. There will then remain £30,168 19s. 2d.
to be carried forward to the next Account.
The dividend and bonus will be payable, in London and in the Colonies, on the 27th March.
The premium, £600,000, received on the new shares has been added to the Reserve Fund, which now
stands at £2,690,000. ■ T i- c a
The Directors retiring by rotation are:—The Hon. Sir C. W. Fremantle, K.C.8., Leslie Sanderson,
Esq., and Charles E. Barnett. Esq., who, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
The proprietors will be asked to approve the election as Directors of Mr. Frederick 1 . C. Livingstone-
Learmonth D 5.0., and the Hon. Michael Hugh Hicks-Beach, M.P., in place of the late Mr. W. R.
Arbuthnot and the late Rt. Hon. Alfred Lyttelton, respectively.
The auditors, Messrs. Wclton. Jones and Co., offer themselves for re-election.
C. W. FREMANTLE, Chairman.
4 Threadnecdle Street, London, 16th March, 1914.

THE CONNOISSEUR’S FAVORITE.
POLITICAL
POINTS
“The Listener at the Keyhole” supplies a
confidential report:—
The apparent causes of Premier Holman’s trouble
with the N.S.W. Labor Caucus were his swelled
head and the vagaries of the last batch of Labor
nominees sent to the Council by the Labor Govern-
ment. Neither Caucus nor P.jj.L. had any hand in
appointing those gentry, and when they saw them
consistently voting against Labor measures they con-
cluded that the Ministry was a bad selector of Legis-
lative Councillors, and determined it should not be
allowed to make a similar ass of itself again. But
for his swelled head Holman would probably have
agreed, but for a long while he has chafed under
the restraint of caucus and Political Labor League
Conference. William Arthur has a profound con-
viction that he and his personality alone were re-
sponsible for the downfall of Wade. His view is that
on his return from England he found the party in a
most disgraceful state, and quite unfit to visit the
constituencies. In fact, it was hardly fit to visit an
ash-heap. So he washed its face, cleaned its shoes,
put on a clean pinnjq and, combing its hair nicely,
took it round to see the constituencies looking quite
respectable. The constituencies fell on its neck and
loaded it with gifts of power and sweets of office.
And then William Arthur marched it home again, and
hoped it would always remember how much it owed
him, and pointed out that it should be very grate-
ful and obedient. And the party was quite prepared to
be these things till William Arthur, in his arrogance,
put both feet through the Labor programme and
finally proposed to repeat the unfortunate blunder
by appointing more men like barrister Brown and
Allen T ivlor to the Council. Then it kicked. It
kicked so high that its boots wrecked the ceiling.
Federal Minister Glynn proposes to finish
the railway from Port Augusta to Darwin,
thus making a Federal line across the con-
tinent with a Federal port at each end; to
connect it with Queensland on one side and
Westralia on the other ; and to do some harbor
improvements in the Territory. Railway-
authorities reckon that, with the easy gradi-
ents and the fact that there will be no land
resumptions to pay for. the whole big job
can be done for £12,000,000. And that is
just about as much as the present N.S.W.
loan expenditure for 16 months. So it doesn't
look so very amazing a “problem,” after all.
The N.S.W. Attorney-General, who isn’t a
very celebrated lawyer at best, has issued a
further ukase that “laborers, artificers and
workmen” need not put the twopenny stamp
on a receipt for £2 worth of wages. This is
an heroic effort to exempt the class from
which the Government draws most of its
support. But the new law says baldly that
every receipt for £2 or more must be stamped,
so, despite Hall’s pronouncement, there is
room for a lot of litigation. Even if the
opinion holds good it isn’t much to the Gov-
ernment's credit. When a wharf-laborer who
has earned £9 doesn’t pay the twopenny-
tax, while a clerk who has earned 455. must
do so, the tiling is a very lopsided sort of
justice, and a very capsized or tipped-up
species of equity.
THE OLD OPPORTUNIST.
He admitted that Australia’s Arbitration tribunals
were busy with troubles affecting industry in the pre-
vailing good times. It was impossible to conjecture
what might happen in bad times, but the leadership
of both sides was good.— George Reid, in England.
THE HIGH COMMISSIONER: “ You are both
fine fellows.” (Aside.) “There’s no teding
which’ll be in power when the time comes
to renew- my appointment.”
A curious streak of hypocrisy was that
upholding of the hands (also both feet) in
horror by the English Tory press over the
reported “conspiracy” to move troops to
Belfast and disarm the inhabitants. A cer-
tain crowd in a certain corner of Ulster
declares publicly that it in illegal possession
of guns, and that, in a certain contingency,
it will illegally shoot with them. Why the
proposed confiscation of the unlawful gun
should be called “conspiracy” and other wild
names passes understanding, but the eastern
scrap of Ulster has a vocabulary and a logic
of its own.
THE PIPES OF PAN.
(Up-to-date version.)
That stormy combination, the Industrial
Workers, of the World, has threatened to
paralyse the United States with “the great-
est strike in history” if war really breaks out
with Mexico (it seems to have broken out),
and “to prevent strife at all costs.” The
annoying thing is that, if it succeeds in its
peaceful intentions, a Mexican army may loot
New York. Even peace can be carried too
far.

REFUGEE SETTLER (from Southern State):
“ Damn! You here, too!!”
The Sydney Worker explains the inward-
ness of the row between Premier Holman
and Speaker Meagher in the N.S.W. Labor
caucus in this wise: (1) Holman thinks he
is leader of the Labor party; (2) the party
has no leaders ; (3) the party has got beyond
the stage at which leaders are required. The
theory is beautiful, but it is to be hoped that
when some foreign enemy comes this way
with a desire to wreck the White Australia
policy and to hang the Trades Hall as high
as Hainan, the defence of the country isn’t
run on these lines.
“The Old Soldiet” :
This is the second time in history that the
United States has been in trouble about a salute.
Also, there was a civil war raging on the other
occasion, as on this. The only difference was that
the United States was the warring party and not the
other side. During the hostile armed correspondence
between North and South the West India mail
steamer Trent was stopped in the Atlantic, and two
passengers—who were really Confederate agents—■
were extracted from the recesses of the hold. There
was a tremendous diplomatic row, and it was thought
at first that Washington wanted to provoke a war
with Britain. Furious postcards were exchanged.
A British cruiser was ordered to take a look at the
east coast of the U.S. and see if it was being put in
a state of defence. The first place to be visited was
the fort on Cape Henry, at the entrance of Chesa-
peake Bay. An officer was sent on shore to intimate
that the ship was going to salute Old Glory. The Yankee
gunner laughed a dreary laugh like the noise of a
lost soul with false teeth, and said: “Well, you can fire
your salute if you like, but I haven’t a gun mounted
in the fort to answer it,” and he showed that he
hadn’t. Whereat the cruiser went back contented.
As regards the present disturbance it is quite feasible
to assume that Huerta didn’t salute the U.S. flag,
and so save the worry and expense of having Mexico’s
ports blockaded and its property ruined, for the
simple reason that invasion by the hated Yank suited
him. His position was none too strong. His over-
throw at the hands of Carranza was on the cards.
President Wilson’s action may yet force Carranza to
make common cause with him. At all events, that
hefty ruffian can hardly join the Yankees against
him. When the U.S. is finished with —if it ever is—
Carranza will be a much simpler problem than he
is now, so far as the bespectacled Aztec who is in
nominal charge of events is concerned. And during
all the intervening period the capable and military
Huerta will necessarily be top-dog. He is the national
hero and the national defender. Whoever else is
prejudiced by the war it looks like a chuck-in for
Huerta.
“Solomon Malaprop”: There
was much truth in that Bulletin
leader about “How Brown Lost
His Inheritance,” because the
English army was unprepared in
1066. To this day the descendants
of the French invaders own most of
the land and do most of the ruling,
and it is still a poor mean thing
to be a real Englishman named
Brown or Smith in England. Even
Tennyson, who thought he was
English, showed the popular senti-
ment by accident when he wrote
that “kind hearts are more than
coronets,” and added that it was
better to have “simple faith” than
to have escaped the _ disgrace of
being of pure English descent.
Tennyson wanted to give “simple
faith” a leg-up, and that was the
best remark he could think of. If
the English army had been better
prepared in 1066 Tennyson would
never have had a chance to write these
awful lines.
Somebody has drawn attention to the
curious fact that the recent N.S.W. Stamp
Act provides for a stamp duty on every
scrip certificate or scrip
Entitling any person to become the proprietor of
any share or part of a share in any company, etc.
Now, no scrip certificate “entitles any person
to become,” etc. It simply states for his own
satisfaction that he already owns the shares.
In the same way a birth certificate doesn’t
entitle a person to be born. It records the
fact that he has been born.
A scrap about the troubles of
Samuel, our uncle :
Already, under the reduced tariff, the
howl of unemployment is heard in the
United States. Mobs are being led by-
agitators of the Independent Workers'
League. A crowd entered a fashionable
New York church during Sunday worship,
and there was wild disorder and a great
inrush of the police. Several hundred
arrests were made, and the holy edifice
didn’t look any better after the episode.
In California 1500 unemployed started
to walk to Washington in order that they
rnight tell President Wilson that their
jobs had given out. The passage of this
so-called “army” was paid across from
San Francisco to Alameda by parties un-
known. Alameda gave the army two
meals, and shunted it into Contra Costa
county. This combined with Solano
county in paying the railroad fares of all
hands to Sacramento. Everybody' feared
trouble, and passed the pilgrims on. A
river separates Sacramento from Yolo
county. The mob was ordered to cross the
bridge and get along. “General” Kelley,
the leader, refused. He was arrested. A
big force of soldiers, police and specials
shoved the army over the bridge. Army
camped in Yolo, by last advices. Con-
sideruble demonstrations also in Boston
and Chicago. Alleged 60,000 workless in Massa-
chusetts, and 45,000 in Chicago. One hundred and
ninety men and one woman arrested at Boston in one
day for revolutionary threats.
Here and there can be found a man who
is doubtful whether Richard Denis Meagher’s
hostility towards the N.S.W. Legislative
Council is altogether inspired by his loyalty
to Labor ideals. When the dust of the Dean
case settled, Meagher found himself no longer
a solicitor of N.S.W. He had gone out, not
for cash motives, but wrecked by his super-
lative vanity. Just when time was begin-
ning to efface the footmarks of the old recol-
lection, Meagher found his name once more
advertised in connection with the Willis Land
Scandals, and when he applied for re-instate-
ment the Court seems to have been influenced
by the part he was alleged to have
played in the disturbance over the
soil. Later on he applied again, and al-
though the Court turned down the
request it held out a sort of vague promise
that next time he pounded on the door he
would not pound in vain, if he pounded hard
enough. It was as good. as its vague pro-
mise, and Meagher was practically rein-
stated, but the Law Society appealed, and the
High Court hurled him out. A Bill was then
introduced into the N.S.W. Assembly to
reinstate him as far as the Ma State was
concerned. The Bill passed the Assembly,
but was butchered in the Council, to make
a Hebrew holiday, for the ghost of Julian
Salamons was still potent in the land, and
Salamons was a long-distance hater. Now
there are these possibilities: (1) Meagher
as Premier, vice Holman deposed; (2) the
Meagher Government wiping out the N.S.W.
Upper House; (3) an Act completely rein-
stating Meagher; (4) an Act amalgamating
the professions of solicitor and barrister;
(5) Jabez Wright, the irreconcilable, hunting
for Meagher’s billet as Premier; (6)
Meagher moving on to the Supreme Court
Bench; (7) Jabez Wright at the head of the
new Ministry.

PETO’S REMEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yet another Censure debate is in progress in the
Federal House of Representatives, while a score of
important National questions await settlement.
- —Daily Taper.
Ar! dicken to oil of this forkin’!
The ’arf o’ w’ich ain’t understud.
(Said Peto, the pug, with a leer and a shrug)
I cud make better men outer mud !
This ’ere Parlerment game is the limit;
Not one of ’em’s fit fer ’is grip—
Pertendin’ they’re fightin’ be ’owlin’ an’ skitin’—
It gives me the blitherin’ pip!
I ain’t no perlitical stoodint;
. I leaves them affairs to the ’eads;
But a whisper I ’cars, an’ the game, it appears,
Is as crook as the bikes or the peds.
Ther’s a bloke at the barber’s, ’e tells me
Them members is orl uv ’em crooks.
1 gits the straight griffen they’re runnin’ a stiff-un,
An ’orl in the ’ands uv the books.
They sends ’em up there, so I’m ’earin’,
To toil fer the good er the crowd ;
Fer to run on the square orl sergarney an’ fair,
As the rools uv the game ’as allowed.
They starts from the jump to run schlenter;
They’ve fluted an’ chiacked an’ chinned ;
An’ never does nuffin’ but gassin’ an’ guffin’,
Ixeep’ when they’re sparrin’ fer wind,
.list listen to me fer a second:
I’ve ’it on a bonzerin’ lurk.
If these ’ere two clicks loafs around an’ does nix,
Jist only pertendin’ to work ;
W’y, give ’em the blitherin’ bullet,
An’ let the ’ole crowd uv ’em drop,
Wif ther ’owlin’ an’ skitin’—an’, if yeh want fightin’,
You give me an’ Micko the cop.
Now, Mieko’s a bird wif ’is maulers;
’E’s ’ot —an’ I’m tollin’ the troof.
We’re a match, me an’ ’im, an’ we’ll put up a scrim
As ’ull pack a ’ouse full to the roof.
Turn Parlerment inter a sta’jum,
Shut down on their silly ole yap—
W’ich is no sorter use—an’ jist let us cut loose,
We’ll put up a beautiful scrap!
I’m forkin’ erconomy, mind yer,
Me dart is the savin’ uv gilt.
I’m told these ’ere chaps ’ang3 around an’ jist yaps
W’ile millyuns uv revenoo’s spilt.
They’re costin’ the country a forchin —■
Gits paid fer the things they don’t do.
But Micko an’ me we will give the show free
If yeh chuck in a bit when we’re through.
I’m arstin’ yeh. Where is the differ ?
Wot game is them Member blokes at ?
They mix it an’ rouse on the floor uv the ’Ouse —
Well ? Micko an’ me kin do that!
The gate ’ull work out at a profit
Wif orl uv our exes allowed ;
Yeh’ll be savin’ yer dough ; it’ll be a fair go,
An’ a damsite more fun fer the crowd!
Ole Micko’s no good at debatin’,
But strooth ! yeh should see ’is left ’ook !
’E’ll be Oppersition ; I’ll take the persition
At presen’ ’eld down be Joe Cook.
You gimme the tip an’ I’ll word ’im.
This sorter thing orter appeal:
You’ll git as much lor; an’ a damsite less for;
An’ the country ’ull save on the (leal.
VICTORIA.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The inquest on the death of the well-known
jockey W. H. Smith, on the Rosehill (N.S.W.) race-
course while riding Anrofodina in the Rosehill Cup
on March 21, was a most unsatisfactory business. The
Coroner found that Smith was accidentally killed—or,
as he put it. “died from injuries accidentally re-
ceived through falling at Rosehill, in the Rosehill
Cup, while riding the racehorse Anrofodina.” It
seems to this paper that a much more virulent finding-
should have been recorded. The inquest dug out the
following interesting facts:—
(1) Quite a number of the riders in the field of
25 were boys of >5 years.
(2) It was a rough race, in which a great deal of
bumping took place.
(3) Some of the riders on the outside squeezed
those on the rails in a reckless fashion.
(4) Gracelle, the first horse to go down, was blind
on the off-side, and was ridden by a jockey aged 15
years, described by another jockey as a “little boy.”
(5) The accident seems to have been due to the
fact that this semi-blind horse was bumped on to
the rails by the pressure of some of the horses on the
outside ; and as a result of this squeezing- and bump-
ing, the half-sighted beast fell, and brought down a
number of others. ■
It seems that either (he Law or the A.J.C. should
go after the scalps of those riders on the outside who
applied the pressure.
“Place Getter”: Last A.J.C. meeting
advanced Cider and Radnor into the ranks of Aus-
tralian horses that have won five figures .in - stakes.
The complete list includes only 20 names, and is
headed by Carbine, with £29,470. In the days
when giants like Chester, Grand Flaneur and others
were on the turf, prizes were small, and none of
them reached .£ 10,000. Now that stakes are boom-
ing upwards, the list of 10,000 pounders will soon
lose interest. A Melbourne Cup winner with an
item of £BOOO to start with will in most cases
soon mop up the difference. Chester, with 18 vic-
tories, barely won that amount.
Jockeys are out of luck in Adelaide just
now. The Victorian, Mason, was killed in a hurdle
race on Easter Monday, through his mount, Wanstcad,
rolling on him after a fall. On, the following Satur-
day Jacques had a fatal skull-fracture over hurdles
at Morphettville. He was only 19, and an expert on
the flat, but —like his mount, Luger—something of a
novice at the jumping game.
* * * *
Hurdle-racer Clontaft is rather a useful
sort of brute. On top of winning both events over
the sticks at the A.J.C. meeting, at Sydney, Clon-
taft picked up a £2OO hurdle handicap at Caulfield,
on Saturday. However, the prad’s success doesn’t by
a long way make up for the other four-footed breth-
ren which have let the foolish betting public into the
soup.
The well-known bookmaker, F. D. Kenyon,
died suddenly in Sydney on Monday last, lie was, at
Tattersall’s Club, of which he was a member, and
had attended to his settling during the morning- and
early afternoon. Towards evening he rose from the
table to summon a waiter, reeled and fell. They
carried him into, the open air, but he gave no sign
of life, and when a medico arrived he pronounced him
dead. In addition to being a bookmaker the deceased
was an ardent courser, and not onlv gazed upon the
iniquitous sport but raced dogs in it. He was better
known in Victoria than N.S.W.-, having spent the
greater portion of his spoiling life in the southern
State.
“Montalex” : “0.1 v.” (B. 23/4/’l4) is
much in error about old-time pistols, certain kinds of
which shot extremely well. With a century-old Man-
ton “dueller” I have made as good practice as with
an up-to-date Henry single-barrelled breech-loader,
within duelling distance, that is to sav, up to about
30yds. At longer range, of course, the smooth-bored
pistol was not in it with the rifled. Also —having
been myself “some” revolver-shot, and being familiar
with all the sterling makes—l am entitled to say
that the revolver is not properly an “arm of preci-
sion.” With the Henry pistol above-mentioned I
could make absolutely certain of my “placing,” but
not so with even the best brands of revolver; while,
as for the cheap sort, they shoot very much as God
pleases. But the real arm of precision has arrived—
tb’o late, unfortunately, for my failing eyesight. The
automatic pistol is quite worthy of the status I have
refused to the revolver. It has about three times the
range of any revolver of equal weight, and has, con-
sequently—distances also being equal—three times
the penetrative energy. It is, moreover (chiefly be-
cause the recoil is-taken-up in-the reloading action).
as accurate as the best single-barrelled target-pistol,
and never did I more regret my vanishing vision than
when I found what I still could do with a certain
automatic make, which, of course, I cannot here
advertise by specifying.
J. M. Chapman, the Yankee promoter who
threatened to revive Australian professional cycling,
has thrown in the towel, and the job will remain
with the sleepy Leagues of AVheelmen. The last
heard of the N.S.W. organisation, which Chapman
threatened to revive first, was a request from a num-
ber of wheelers to call a meeting to endeavor to
locate the missing organisation.
The Dunlop Relay Test closes to-day,
April 30, and the various clubs are working over-
time in a last effort. So far, Maoriland has the best
of the deal, but last Saturday the Granville (Syd-
ney) club made a desperate effort and dislodged
South Canterbury (M.L.) from the second place. But
Maoriland, as represented by the Marshland Club, is
still on top. At time of writing, the position is as
follows, and is not likely to be allowed to remain
so.—

by A. Kennedy, who covered the 10-mile section in

The Motor Cycle Club of N.S.W. has
thrown over its usual week-end competition this
Saturday so as to give members a chance to see
Guillaux flying at Victoria Park racecourse. Mean-
while a special committee will wrestle with the
problem of how to start, inaugurate and arrange for
an Australian “Grand Prix” Motor Cycle Race. A
huge entry has been received for this mis-named con-
test, and the interest is keen. The course and con-
ditions will be evolved by the time this appears in
the groggy hand of the public, and the announce-
ment will appear in the Lord’s good time.
On Friday morning last week 12 motor-
cars left Sydney on a Reliability Test conducted by
the Automobile Club of Australia. The weathpr was
perfect and the roads were said to be good—-that is
to say, they weren’t so bad as the main roads of
Cashmere, where the mountain path sometimes ends
abruptly and the traveller climbs up a knotted rope
and finds a new section of the track at the top. The
Club nearly fell down dead with astonishment when
only seven of the dozen competitors finished in
conformity with the conditions. On Friday Schwartz
and R. A. Pye failed to reach Goulburn as stipulated,
and Saturday’s run of 152 miles from Wollongong
to Sydney by a circuitous route biffed out three
more such seasoned drivers as Sherwood (Dunlop
Co.), R. G. Todman and Arthur Kidman. The suc-
cessful ones were Paul Meyer (Benz), Carter (Cross-
ley), Boyd Edlcins (Vauxhall), Bradley (Empire)
arid A. V. Turner (Benz). All these fared through
without, losing points, for reliability, and the victory
would have to be decided on petrol consumption
and hill climbing. In the final dust-up R. A.
Carter’s Crossley scored 200 points for reliability,
100 for petrol consumption, 148 for hill climbing-
total 448. Paul Meyer’s Benz also got 200 for re-
liability, 70 for petrol consumption and 170 for hill
climbing—total 440. A. V. Turner’s Benz, 200 for
reliability, 98 for petrol and 136 for hill climbing
—total 429. It was a fine contest.

Guillaux, the French aviator now in
N.S.W., seems to have had something like a trium-
phal progress at Newcastle on Saturday, when in the
presence of about 6000 people he soared 7000 ft. in
the air and thrilled the people with feats that were
hideous in their daring. He looped the loop, he dived
the “corkscrew dive,” he described innumerable figure
eights in the air, and left the fig’ures outlined in
white smoke against the cloudless blue; he dived
straight down towards the earth and then flew up
again into the sky ; he flew upside-down, and down-
side-up, and he flew on his side : —in fact, he flew
every possible way but backwards. There is no trace
of him having done that, although he looped the
loop backwards, or, in other words, he flew over his
own head. He used a Bleriot monoplane, driven by
a 50-h.p. Gnome engine, and he proved to have the
contraption under perfect control. That he is daring
unto recklessness was proved by the stupid jokes he
played on the crowd, diving straight at some massed
cadets and skimming over their heads with only a
few feet to spare—which is fine and magnificent as
long as it comes off- —but might have been murder if
something had gone wrong with the works.
N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union is a very lively
organisation, and begins the season with a member-
ship . of, 1064, spread over 39 affiliated clubs. As
the finances of the Union are also on a sound basis,
the coming season has a healthy look about it.
.Jim Paddon, who yesterday left for Eng-
land to meet Barry on the Thames for the sculling
championship of the world, got a shock last Satur-
day. In a mile race for a prize presented by Peter
Kemp he conceded his pacemaker, Sydney of that ilk,
three seconds in a mile. And when the numbers went
up it was found the pacemaker had beaten the cham-
pion by half a length. Which means either the cham-
pion is not the only pebble on the beach or is not
quite the champion we thought him. But, of course,
there is a mighty difference between one mile and
four and a quarter. There is a good Australian
flavor about the champion’s expedition. His pacer,
Sydney Kemp, is son of that Peter Kemp who himself
was champion in 1888 and again in 1890. Paddon
took with him two Australian-built boats, the price
of one of which was coughed up by the Svdney Referee.
The craft was built by George Towns, another ex-
champion. The second boat was presented by the
aforementioned sometime champion Peter Kemp, who
built it with his own rugged hands. The contest will
rage between Putney and Mortlake, a distance of
four and a quarter miles, and will be for the cham-
pionship of the world, the championship of England,
and, by special permission of the trustees, for the
Sportsman Challenge Cup.
* * * *
Tasmanian sculler Cecil McVilly was in
evidence again last week—this time in Melbourne,
on Saturday afternoon—when he blew in first in the
Open Sculling Handicap on the Yarra, winning bv
two feet from the next man. It was described by
those who saw it as a magnificent finish, and was a
great performance, as McVilly conceded some hard
doers as much as 65sec. in a race of 11 miles.

South Australia had a tilt at the Ma
State bowlsdom at Sydney last week. But the Ma
State arose in her warpaint, and repressed the pre-
sumptuous efforts of the invaders by 129 to ’8 and
118 to 84. Adelaide Club team took revenge by
castigating Waverley (holders) by 25 to 21 in the
struggle for the Stevenson Cup—an inter-State. club
competition goblet. It was a small compens; lion,
but better than nothing.
N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Association is
holding its head in its hands over the financial pj o-
lem last year’s doings left on hand. The A.A.A.
dropped £2l0 —-£150 of which was pooled toward
giving the Yankees a splendid time. They got it.
and the A.A.A. has now got the bill, and doesn’t know
what to do with it. Another smack like that, and
the game is dead. But surely there is enough in-
terest taken in amateur athletics in N S.W. to help
the association out of the hole it has fallen into while
trying to foster a clean and health-giving sport.
The winter is upon us, and this week will
see the heavyweight forms of sport unroll themselves
in the shape of four brands of hoofball! (League.
Union, Soccer and Australian the greatest
of these is League, with Soccer risiiig rapidly.) In
addition, there will be lacrosse, .baseball and hockey
all stretching out a hand for the public sprat. The
public sprat is a much-hunted fish.
The Rugby division is bigger this year
than ever before, and the struggle between the Union
and League divisions for popular support will be so
considerable that sorheone will be badly left. The
League seems to carry too many guns for its decrepit
parent, the Union. This is how the opposing forces
stand:—- (

The League will have the touring Knglishmen and a
Queensland XIII to aid its end of the fight, while the
Union will try the lure of invading teams- from
Maoriland and the Northern State. The practice foot-
ball made a bad beginning with a crop of accidents,
some of which are alleged to have been due to a
superfluity of “boot.” However, the referees weeded
out a couple of ruffians, and the drastic punishment
should have a salutary effect.
* * * *
Vic. League hoofball started its season on
Saturday just about where it left off seven months
ago, with much the same players, ditto crowds and
the same old 10 clubs. Chiefest collision was at
Victoria Park, where the Magpies were at home to
Carlton, and a pleasing and edifying spectacle occurred,
resulting, after a strenuous and exciting last quarter,
in a draw —’Wood, 8 goals 8 behinds; Carlton, 6
goals 20 behinds; 56 points each. Game was fast
and open, with exceptionally little out-of-bounds
plays, and ’bailers had lost the stiffness of their long
rest; also the “gate” was in the best of form. The
Old Blues relied chiefly on weight, high marking and
long kicking; the Magpies on speed and short pass-
ing, and though they led by a small margin through-
out, in the middle of the last quarter the Blues pulled
up the nine points they were behind and added a
goal as well. The locals passed the ball down again
and eluded the defence, getting a good mark in front
and the needed goal just as the bell rang. A most
intersting game. Down at Geelong an equally excit-
ing episode, between the Seasiders and Essendon,
ended similarly; though here the ’Dons led through-
out, and were overwhelmed by a vigorous attack when
they thought they had everything secure, Geelong
piling on 24 points to ’Don’s seven in the last term
—8 goals 13 behinds all. Of the others, ’Roy,
premiers, beat Melbourne by 93 points to 37 ; South
pounded the Students by 98 to 44 ; and St. Kilda
jumped on Richmond 64 times to 42, in a manner
of speaking. As to the Students, though South is
admittedly a hard proposition, they are hereby re-
quested to train properly and bog in, lest they re-
peat this last year’s performance of failing to win
one solitary game. That sort of record gives a bar-
racker the pip.
* * * *
The Vic. Ass. games, the third of the
round, resulted in Port sustaining its first defeat,
at the feet of ’Scray, by the considerable margin of
89 points to 50. Brunswick gained its first victory
by collecting 90 points to Brighton’s 52 ; North coi-
lared four more points than Essendon, after a tall
struggle—63 to 59; P’ran polished off the Haw-
hawthornites in a workmanlike manner by 84 to 56,
and ’Town galloped over ’Cote by 87 to '24. ’Town
and ’Scray are already the only undefeated teams
of the 10, and they don’t collide till May 16, when
something sudden is liable to happen to one of them.
* * * *
A strenuous effort is being made in Fog-
land and Europe to alter the service law in lawn
tennis. The proposed new rule stipulates that the
server must, before serving, stand with both feet at
rest on the ground behind the base line, but he may
raise one foot from the ground before the service is
delivered provided such foot is replaced on the
ground behind the base line before either foot is
placed on the court. The Whole sting of the altera-
tion is in the tail, and there is more than a sus-
picion that the change is being aimed straight at
Norman Brookes. Uncle Samland is fighting the
suggested change, and is declaring that it has been
designed to compel Brookes and McLoughlin to aban-
don the service that they have been developing and
perfecting for years. If it does come into force it
will do away with the lifted foot and the quick stride
forward, and will prevent these hurricane servers
from getting quickly to the net to kill a weak return.
Wilding, by the way, is in favor of the suggested
change. But then he is essentially a base-line player.
* * * .
There is a new locally-made tyre on the
market that threatens to' make blow-outs ancient his-
tory and puncture as scarce as eloquent clergymen.
It is much the same to look at as the ordinary tyre,
but between the canvas and the rubber is a solid
layer of chrome leather, soft yet tough as old boa-
constrictors. Hence the name, the Chromatic tyre.
The co. is so far only in the crawling stage, and the
output limited, but when the thing gets going pro-
perly this paper has visions of puncture outfit cos.
going bungi. The price is much the same as for
ordinary tyres.
While France is expending its natural
excitement on “le boxe” Germany has gone mad on
dog racing. The first big meeting was held at
Leipzig last month and drew enormous crowds. A
rather queer event was a race for bulldogs
(known to the Teutons as “boxers”), seven of
whom ran snorting.round the track amid great quan-
tities of excitement. Another interesting event was
a hurdle race confined to Airedale terriers; but pre-
sent scribe can’t understand why there wasn’t a bend-
ing race for Dachs-hounds. Anyhow, the meeting
was a great success, although there was some trouble
because a number of the owners persisted in hovering
about at the finishing-post with mutton-chops on
strings to induce some particular tyke to put his best
paw forward.
On Monday night the Stadium manage-
ment entertained quite a contingent of visiting
Yankee boxers, including Jimmy Clabby, Joe Wel-
lings, J. Shiff, Steve Ketchell (no relative to his
illustrious namesake). Shugrue and Moore. Jeff
Smith and Jimmy Clabby have been matched for
May 23. Solicitor W. H. Drew was in the chair,
and took advantage of the opportunity to introduce
the pressmen present to the new company, The
Stadia, Limited. Mr. Drew informed his guests
that the company had been registered that very day.
and that the directorate consisted of Messrs. J. wren
(Chairman), Reg. Baker, Thatcher, Morrison and
W. H. Drew, the speaker. Mr. Reg. Baker had been
appointed managing-director for a term of years, and
the classic Stadium would progress as it had done
aforetime without any perceptible change. During
the absence of Reg. Baker in furrin parts Mr. Harry
Reesing has acted as general manager, and on Mr.
Baa.er , ~ return it is understood that he will con-
tinue to be the chief lieutenant of the debonair
Reginald. By the way, it is time the Stadia, Ltd.,
had a conference of its referees to standarise things
somewhat and to decide whether occipital wallops
and a few' other vagaries that have been shown of
late should not be utterly wiped out. The occipital
wallop is a very burning question, as if it is allowed
to continue someone will get injured, and in the
consequent riot the boxing game will be uppercut
and put down for nine seconds if not outed.
At Melbourne Stadium on Saturday night
Milbum Saylor again demonstrated that Englishman
Nat Williams is no match for him. For the first
three rounds Williams would appear, from all ac-
counts, to have outboxed his hard-hitting opponent
and to have scored generally till towards the end of
that term. Then Saylor threw a whole cathedral at
him and landed on the body. It knocked the speed
out. of Williams, who continued to crumple up as
time went on, till, in the sixth round, Saylor put him
down three times. When Saylor saw Williams rise
once more and reel towards him he begged referee
Marre to stop the scrap. Bui the gong saved the
knock-out. In the seventh Williams again went
down twice, and as he staggered up Marre, seeing' his
case was hopeless, took the responsibility of stopping
the fight and awarded Saylor the thick end of the
purse.
The beginning of the end of Williams was that left
swing to the body tow’ards the end of the third
round. It knocked all the speed out of him —and
speed is one of the essentials for success in a battle
with Saylor. The others are a weighty punch and a
horseshoe in the glove, to bring luck. The man w'ho
fights Saylor should remember that like conquers like.
Saylor is a wild sort of hitter, and half his blows
land anywhere but where they are intended for. The
spot they were intended for would have received the
blow from the padded knuckles ; but where they do
usually fall receives the wallop from the heel of the
palm or the wrist, and some of his most destructive
uppercuts have been delivered with the forearm.
Quite unintentionally, admittedly; but- >xine the less
effective for all that. Therefore the gentleman who
next meets Saylor should be prepared to In id out the
same sort of accident. Part of his preparati u should
consist of practising how to avoid the ap -earance
of evil while collecting the benefits thereof. But it
must be also remembered that, quite apart from these
little vagaries, Saylor is a clever fighter and a > mag-
nificent tactician. Much of his success is due to
wonderful suppleness of muscle that enables him to
hit from anywhere and everywhere, and to hit hard
all the time. He has a perfect genius for swinging
his body so as to get the power for a tremendous
wallop from any quarter whatsoever. Another element
in his favor is the long reach that gives him such
an advantage in countering; and, very wisely, he
counters on every possible occasion. It was a series
of ferocious counters that hammered Tom McCormick
to bits. But the man who can use a straight left
with speed and power and has the Jeff-Smith-like
agility to dodge the powerful swings should find no
difficulty in destroying Saylor utterly. The writer
freely admits he has not seen that party around
lately.
* * * *
Fritz Holland, the Yankee welter-weight
who clawed Tom McCormick recently in Melbourne,
made his Sydney d6but on Saturday night against
James Fitton. Fitton is an uncommon name, and
the only other party to boast that monniker the
writer wots of was a certain “Mistress Fitton,” maid
of honor to bad Queen Bess, to whom Bill Shakspeare
addressed many, if not all, of his honeyed sonnets.
Whether the boxer who appeared in the classic
Stadium on Saturday night is any relation only the
Lord knows. Fitton is by way of being a boxer.
He has a good left, and can send over rights with
steam behind them. Also, his footwork is good, and
his ducking, when he does duck, excellent. The
trouble on Saturday night was that he didn’t duck
often enough. He has sandy hair and an amiable ex-
pression, wdiereas what a boxer wants is a face like
a Gorgon. -.That is where Holland scores. He has
a very grim, almost sinister, expression. His bald
dome gives the idea of a young man grown suddenlv
old, and he would appear to have shed his teeth early
in life; with the result that most of the time he
beamed on all and sundry, with a demoniac sort of
grin ; that made the crowd angry. A very little
makes a crowd angry at times. ' He is - a -mighty
slogger before the Lord —a sort of welterweight
replica of Saylor, but, on Saturday night’s showing,
without the distinctive skill that redeems much of
Saylor’s wild work. He is a voracious assimilator
of punishment, and though to him it is better to
give than to receive, he doesn’t mind how much he
receives so long as he can get in and give what he
yearns to bestow upon his pugnacious friend, as the
lawyers say. That sort of crude slogger is an easy
guy for'the man with a straight arm that has steam
behind it, and on Saturday night on two occasions
Fitton had him rocking from heavy rights. A heavy
left or two causes Holland to bore in wildly and
leave himself wide oj)en for a knock-out, provided
the opposite party has a quick and thunderous deliv-
ery. Fitton has the quick delivery, but not the
thunder behind it. However, to come to the actual
scrap: Holland weighed lOst. 61b. to Fitton’s lOst.
251 b. ; but the latter was obviously out of condition.
This was not his fault, however; he had not had
sufficient time since stepping off the boat in which
to get properly hardened up. He used a graceful
left, and occasionally sent over powerful rights
which made Holland think hard. In the fourth Hol-
land went in to slog with the wild fervor of a
religious fanatic, but Fitton met him with straight
lefts, which knocked his head back again and again.
Fitton missed several good opportunities to put over
the right. He seemed to hesitate about following
up, apparently for fear of the dangerous hooks that
Holland flung around so wildly. Holland was for
most of the time a mere chopping block, and looked
horribly crude; but the three or four wallops he
did land took a lot of the grit out of Fitton. In
the fifth Holland tried a few' straight lefts on his
own account, and though Fitton eluded most of them
what he collided with did him no good. His condi-
tion began to show signs of disrepair. In the sixth
Holland bored right in and roughed, and the crowd
howled to Fitton to smite him hard. Fitton pulled
himself together and passed over a couple of rights
that shook Holland very badly. But that toughened
streak shook his head, grinned hideously, bored in
some more, and worried Fitton badlv. The seventh
was a battle grim and great. Holland bored in
more than ever, and Fitton nearly knocked his head
off. but could not drop him. Holland showered left
hooks like rain. Fitton’.s condition faded very
rapidly, so fast indeed that it could be seen shrink-
ing. In the eighth, though showing wonderful skill,
he was so exhausted that he could no longer hit
hard enough to stop the grinning aggressor. In the
ninth he was down three times, and was ranidlv
being belted right out when the Inspector of Police
arose, and at the same moment the towel of surrender
arose, and a report arose that the game was fin-
ished. Although ‘ beaten, Fitton had delighted the
crowd with his showing, and it cheered him vocifer-
ously after having in the most flagrant and unfair
fashion hooted the .fighting Holland. Popular ani-
mosity is an inexplicable thing. It is all mouth
and feet, and has no brains.

Secrets of Beauty
SELECTED RECIPES FROM HERE
AND THERE—THINGS EVERY
WOMAN WANTS TO KNOW.
Getting Rid of
Female Moustaches.
“Practical Suggestions.”
To women who are annoyed by disfiguring downy
hair growths a method of permanently eradicating
the same will come as a piece of good news. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be used.
Almost any chemist should be able to supply an
ounce of this drug. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to remove the disfiguring growth
instantly, leaving no trace, but also to actually kill
the hair roots without irritating the skin. * . * , *
Objectionable body odors resulting from perspiration
and other causes may be instantly banished by
simply applying a little powdered (white) pergol to
tile affected surface occasionally. * * * Smart
women are rapidly adopting the use of the natural
allacite of orange blossoms when the complexion is
inclined to be oily. It makes a capital greaselcss
cream, holds the powder perfectly, and does not
encourage hair growths.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
“Home Chemistry.”
Good stallax not only makes the best possible
shampoo, but has the peculiar property of leaving
the hair with a pronounced natural “wave,” an
effect presumably desired by many ladies. A tea-
spoonful of the stallax granules, dissolved in a cup
of hot water is ample for a luxurious head wash,
and leaves the hair with a peculiar lustre and flutfi-
ness obtainable from nothing else as far as known.
It is perfectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been so little
used for this purpose it comes only in sealed original
packages, enough to make up twenty-five or thirty
individual shampoos. * * * For an actual hair
grower nothing equals pure boranium. It is quite
harmless, and sets the hair roots tingling with new
life. * * * The use of rouge is almost always
obvious, but powdered colliandum gives a perfectly
natural color and defies detection.
Oxygen Removes a
Bad Complexion.
“Practical Suggestions.”
Oxygen is now used to clear the complexion. Its
peculiar property of destroying waste matter and not
injuring healthy tissue is well known. Bad com-
plexions are merely the accumulation of half-dead
waste matter on the skin surface. This accumulation
shows in the form of sallowness, moth patches and a
generally lifeless appearance. Smart women now
clear off these imperfections by getting some merco-
lised wax from the chemist’s, and applying it for a
few nights like cold cream. This wax contains
oxygen, which attacks and removes the disfiguring
waste matter. It is pleasant to use and perfectly
harmless. The fresh, healthy skin which has been
covered up is soon revealed in all its beauty, and the
face so treated looks much younger and prettier as a
result. * * * TO bring a natural red color
to the "lips rub • them with a soft stick- of pro-
la'ctum. * * * For tired, hot or perspiring feet
use a teaspoonful of powdered onalite in a foot-
bath.
Home Beauty Aids.
“Household Hints.”
A persistently shiny nose or a dull, lifeless com-
plexion drives many a woman to cosmetics and con-
sequent despair. And all the time a simple remedy
lies at hand in the home. If you have no eleminite
in the house you need only get about an ounce from
your chemist and : add just sufficient water to dis-
solve it. A little of this simple lotion is Nature’s
own beautifier. It is very good for the skin, and
instantly gives the complexion a soft, velvety, youth-
ful bloom that any woman might envy. It lasts all
day or evening, renders powdering entirely unneces-
sary, and absolutely defies detection. * * * To
make the eyelashes grow long, dark and curling,
apply a little mennaline with the finger tips occa-
sionally. It is absolutely harmless, and beautifies
the eyebrows as well. * * * Pilenta soap is the
most satisfactory for all complexions. It even works
well in cold or hard water.

Business, Robbery, etc.
* * * *
That enterprising business man and imperialist,
“bleeds” Fitchett, of Life, lias .succeeded in
thoroughly annoying the medical profession in con-
nection with his new paraffin spec. In a recent issue
of the Fitchett periodical Sir Arbuthnot Lane was
quoted extensively in support of the theory that
paraffin makes a useful intestinal lubricant. Accord-
ing to the Australian Medical Journal (28/3/’l4) :
“In a recent number of the British Medical Jour-
nal there appeared an article by Prof. J. G. Adami,
of Montreal, expressing dissent from Sir Arbuthnot
Lane’s conclusions, and doubt as to the efficacy of his
procedure. This article is actually quoted in the
current number of Life as supporting Sir A. Lane.
It is the most astounding example of journalistic
but perhaps it would be better not to classify it.”
Fitchett’s machinations in the matter of face-foods
and paraffin cures only seem to indicate, amongst
other things, that a man may be a sturdy—even
frantic—Empire-builder, and possess withal but a
hazy conception of commercial ethics.
Up-country in N. S. Wales there is. a firm looking
for a genius. Here’s the description from the local
news-sheet:—-
WANTED.
A QUICK, smart, experienced carter, must be well
educated (college not absolutely necessary) ; good
with horses; non-smoker preferred; must be good-
looking, with a soft voice, but not loquacious;
splendid opportunity for good canvasser with plenty
of vim.
C. E. BUTCHER & CO., South Gundagai.
No reward is offered for his capture, but no doubt
it will be forthcoming. Mere gratitude would be
too Scotch a recompense.
Melbourne Tvpothetse combine printers are chewing
the rag just now. The price exacted by the paper
merchants under Typo, agreement was 3d. per lb.
from members for ordinary news printing paper,
which the anti-combine men could buy only 7 at a
higher rate even if the wholesale sharks would deal
with them at all. But the anti-combiners found that
they could get their paper from Adelaide at 2Jd. per
lb. So they are getting it, and some of the Typo-
thetse also are getting it, and the Melbourne mer-
chants feel like fat men who have cut their own
throats in an attempt to bleed the trade. The Typo-
combine appears to be damned at its source, so to
speak. 'The conspiracy was too local to last. Also
the few indispensable conspirators were too greedy.
* * * *
“Ricardo”: At Williamstown (Melb.) I am as-
tounded with the quantity of wheat stacked up.
The one ill-placed port is choked up. About 20
cargoes for the largest vessels. Yet when I went
yesterday it was only lazily trickling into two. The
galvanised iron sheds, built for storage, are crowded
out, and there is the wheat in the open air. There
have lately been abundant newspaper paragraphs
about the magnificence of the Victorian railways in
carting the wheat to Williamstown. But there it
sticks! Couldn’t some have been hauled away
from Geelong or Portland.
The narrow margin on which profit can balance.
An (American, of course) “mail-order house” admits
modestly that it uses every month a ton and a half
of pins (33601 b.). Of course they can be used over
and over again, so girl-labor is employed to remove
the pins from old papers—so long as new pins cost
more than a shilling a pound. The moment they get
below that, it is cheaper to “scrap” the old pins
with the paper—-and scrap the girls also.
If Willie Watt can spare time from play-
ing with his State Fright dolls he might give
some attention to an absurdity in Vic.’s
Licensing Act. That piece of wisdom states
that a publican may serve liquor pf any
description' to a bona-fide traveller on Sun-
day ; yet the same Act says that if the pub-
lican dares to open his bar door on Sunday
lie may be fined £5. So in the case of those
landlords who don’t carry beer in their
pockets or a bottle behind one ear, the
thirsty bona-fide has to get his liquor at the
horse trough.
A LAY OF LOANS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Being an account of the dreadful vision of an
habitual poet after a visit to the Loan Exhibition at
Federal Government House.
We sought the place where dwells apart
The Gov. in marble halls,
Far from the city’s pulsing heart.
Above were hung three balls.
The doorman, having made demands
For cash, spoke greetings with his hands.
Within the Throne Room, row on row,
Exhibits were displayed ;
They made indeed a goodly show,
Those loans which won’t be paid !
All shapes and sizes were on view,
And ev’ry brand of 10U.
A fat curator spoke to me
While lie massaged his nose:
“Now here’s a niccth young loan,’’ said he,
“But note how fasth id grows,
For this fine, large vone, over here,
Ith older only by a year.”
The man (his name was Palestein)
Took us around the show ;
Of knowledge he was quite a mine
About the cash we owe.
Each object, of which he with zest
Discoursed, was full of intherest!
Then turning to my son 1 said:
“My boy, you’ll one day own —
Just think of it! —when I am dead
And gone, each blessed loan
That’s here ! And the collection will
By then, no doubt, be larger still!”
But lo ! that most ungrateful son
Glared hard at me, his dad.
“So help me bob, you take the bun!”
Said he. “You ain’t too bad!
You got these blessed loans to blew,
And gave this bloke my I 0 U!
“The whole collection do I spurn!”
He cried with angry frown;
“And, if 1 get a chance, I’ll burn
The blamed museum down!”
He smashed a glass-case with his boot,
A priceless loan cracked ’neath his foot!”
The fat curator wore a look
As though compelled to dine
On pork ! Then an explosion shook
The place, and Palestein
In scattered fragments lay around,
Like Hebrew symbols, on the ground!
VICTORIA.

Hold the World’s Records
Mr. A. J. Dalton of the Collingwood Rifle Club beat "
all previous records by scoring 67 consecutive bullseyes
in actual competition. He used the famous No.
B.S.A. Martini Rifle and B.S.A. Aperture Sight.
Mr. J. Pepe put up another world’s record by scoring
299 out of a possible 300 at 25, 50 and ICO yds. He used
the No. 10 B.S.A. Martini with B.S.A. aperture sights.
Mr. H. Motton won the 1912 championship of New South
Wales, shooting with the No. 6 B.S.A. Martini.
Mr. J. Mues won the 1912 championship of Victoria
shooting with the No. 10 B.S.A. Martini.

or '32 bore and are unapproachable for range or
sporting work. Fully described in latest B.S.A.

The long-expected at last. A Japanese girl, doing
balancing feats on a stage at Marseilles (France),
" fell and was 'killed. Also, “her ladder fell into the
orchestra, and seriously damaged a musician.” One
of these evenings an Australian stalls-patron will be
Seriously Damaged; and then there will be a row.
Genee told a London Referee interviewer that she
is likely to leave, the stage, so as to get some “real
home life” with 'heir, husband. At present, she has
to practise “every day, for many hours. A dancer’s
woik is never done.”- .She added the. truly feminine
reason, for *retiring soon, that, as she was before, the;
public at the age of 12,-people think of her. as much
older than she is. As for being a has-been \tdio has
made her pile, “as regards my age, health and
income, there is lio reason Wjby i should not keep on
dancing for many years, sav another 10 or 15, and
then I should not be at all old." Her dream for
later life is to found a School of Dancing.
“C. Ockney”: “Who’s the Lady?” it species of
chemise comfedy which- is to pay this country a visit
in * a mouth, or two, got n great advt. in' Dublin
recently. 1 They .dislike the tabasco business in Ire-
. land, and the result was that when the really, torrid
portions of “Who’s the Lady?” were submitted, the
crowd began to hoot. One of the objectors Was
arrested. He told the beak that' lie - had hissed the •
play, and would do it again on the earliest opportu-
nity. The Bench, after hearing evidence, remarked:
“I consider the defendant has done: a public; service,
and I-dismiss the ease.’.’ The only person who came •
badly out of the episode .was the British Censor.
“Snell”: I note a new development among .theatre-
goers. Formerly if people disliked a show they
stayed away, and left an aching emptiness in the
theatre to signify disapproval’in the usual manner.
Nowanigbtij people go to shows whether tljiev like
them or not. - They go from 'sheer force of habit, or
because of.' the melancholy lack of something to do
after dinner. Nobody, has a good word for two
entertai nlmhits that are doing quite well in my
metropolis just now, and I have to admit that while
the scarcity of appreciative epithets is richly deserved
the large attendance, js’ utterly uncalled for. ; 1 anti-
cipate a time when theatre-going will have become
mechanical, and when nobody will take much notice
if now and then the management neglects to put on
any play at all. >
“Stargazer”:-Would you expect the fruit jtrade to
be susceptible to dramatic influence ? I was buying some
fruit in a Melbourne city shop one evening recently,
greengages among "other things', and the salesman
said. “Great demand for greengages lately. Would
you mind telling 'me if you have any particular
reason for buying greengages?” I answered that I
bought -greengages because I rather liked that hind
,of gage. The shopman became . explanatory. ,“I
asked,” he said; “because I’m investigating. I
lgfticed that every second customer of ours was
wanting! gfeeiigages. :jl. inquired, at jdjther places,;apd
“discovered that the demanft was general. Then I
looked into it, and found it was all due to a drama
tbey’ve been playing at the Royal, ‘The Doctor’s
Dilemma.’ In that play a doctor recommends
greengages; and later a chap is cured of something
by greengages. It’s created quite a rush on green-
gages. I wish an author would ring in a few words
about water melons.”
- “Harrison O. ”: Technically, “Madame N” is one of
the worst plays Melbourne has been invited to take
seriously for some time. It is strung out in a pro-
logue amf the .simple device of keeping
various characters hard at'work* recounting ‘its 'central
incident"," which, by the way, happens before the rise
of the curtain. In the prologue we learn of how
Jacqueline Fioriot ran away from her ridiculous hus-
band. In the first act Jacqueline fa) tells the yarn
to the newly-acquired lover, (h) solemnly unwinds -it
as a warning to a serving-vveneh who is threatened
with an attack of conjugal infelicity, (c) the lover
passes tile dreary old scandal on to a brace of black-
mailers. In this act. too, the lady kills her lover
in full view of the limelight man. The second act
happens along and the blackmailers project the
oft-told chronicle at the head of the sorrowing
husband. Then comes act three, laid in an Assize
Court. The frayed narrative is repeated some more.
Also, the murder which the audience knows all about
is described in detail. This last is a particularly
glarihg fault, since there is no reason why the act
should not begin just before the finish of the public
prosecutor’s speech, seeing that the address of counsel
for the defence is the only part that matters tuppenCe.
On the other, hand, there being only tuppence worth
of interest in the act, perhaps it would be better; if
it didn’t start at all.
“G.D.8.”; I was in Canton, in September last,
just when they were cleaning up the revolt there.
I was on foot in the street that runs alongside the
railway station, one day, when I heard a shpt.
I blew along and found there was a row on at a
theatre. An obliging Italian explained that a dozen or
so rebels wanted to see the show for nothing, and
that this was too much for the proprietor, who set
his doorkeepers on to them. There was an argu-
ment during which a Mauser pistol went off, and one
of the doorkeepers went off too. The res f retired
and barred up the doors. The debate was still con-
tinuing, when a half-company of troops arrived
from nowhere in particular. I heard one orde> - given,
and the Dago and I got up the street as fast as We
could leg it. We ran slap bang into a crowd : of
rebels coming in the opposite direction. They didn’t
waste much time, but got to work on the killing
right away.- The shop people knew what was coming
alj. right,' for you could hear the doors hanging all
along the street. I suddenly missed my friend from
the sunny south and found myself alone. I squeezed
myjjelf into a doorway and., tried to see the -bullets ’
as they went’by." A Chinese' officer came along to
me (he was brave, I’ll say that much for him) and
Said- in excellent Eiiglish: “Do not, .be afraid. We
do not seek to molest foreigners. Keep close to the
wall.” He was shot 'before he got back to his men.
So .1 made myself as small as I could and waited.
The scrap lasted perhaps ten minutes—it seemed like
two hours —and when the rebs. had cleared off I
counted 40 dead marines. I found my. Italian a few
minutes after; lie had got into a shop. “Ah,” he
said, “I know these peoples. I was with the artil-
lery in Peking, when the Boxer war was on. They
fight like the devils, isn’t it?”
THE DETECTIVE ADVERTISEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is no longer good business for a leading actress
to have her diamonds stolen. The advt. that was
once so desirable is now out of date. When Miss
Daisy Jerome arrived in Melbourne —if we are to
believe the expert theatrical paragraphers—there was
a private detective waiting to escort-her (at a respeot-
ful distance), because “the enormous amount of jewel-
lery she carries may lead to a good deal of unsouglit-
for, and perhaps dangerous, attention.”
I saw .an actress, t’other day.
AVho for a change was simply walking.
Bchind'her, some few yards away,
A dark-eyed mystery was stalking,
A sly, suggestive cuss who glared
’Neath slouching hat, as black as thunder,
And every where ! the couple fared,
The people stopped and stared in wonder.
He seemed so fierce, so jealous-eyed,
So sedulous in his adherence,
Perchance lie thought of homicide.
This quite excused my interference.
“Behold,” I cried, “the villain dread,
Who follows you with dire intention.”
She smiled a lunelit smile, and said:
“Oil, there’s no danger, sir, to mention.”
“He does his duty, nothing more,
He is my own plain-clothes detective.
He guards my gems and gauds galore;
’Twould seem the ad. is quite effective.
You might have placed .the slouching wight,
I-lis regulation feet betray him.
My diamonds? Oh, they are all right.
I had to pawn the lot to pay him!”
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

HOW DYSPEPTICS CAN EAT WHAT
THEY WANT WITHOUT PAIN.
In the vast majority of cases, states a
leading specialist, indigestion, dyspepsia, and
other so-called stomach troubles are in no
way due to the fault of the stomach itself,
but almost entirely to fermentation of the
food contents and the resultant formation of
acid and gas which irritate and inflame the
delicate lining of the stomach, and unnatu-
rally distend the stomach walls, causing dis-
placement of the vital organs and dangerous
pressure on the heart and lungs. He esti-
mates that 96 per cent, of all stomach pains,
either acute or chronic, are directly or in-
directly due to acid fermentation; all of
which he has proved can be avoided by
neutralising the acid and stopping the fer-
mentation by means of a simple antacid
known among and obtainable from all
chemists under the name of bisurated mag-
nesia : half a teaspoonful in a little water
immediately after eating, effectively prevent-
ing the slightest -indication of fermentation
and discomfort even in the severest cases.
Inquiry 'among chemists confirmed the re-
markable value of this product, but readers
are cautioned when purchasing to see that
the name is spelled b-i-s-u-r-a-t-e-d, as there
are other chemical products bearing names
similar to bisurated magnesia, but which are
lacking in its peculiarly valuable properties.*
