IN BANANA LAND
Mick Hackett, of the Cooper, who passed
out at Windorah recently, was a fine ex-
ample of the Northern degenerate. He had
been shearer, selector and squatter; had
fathered a family of 10 (of whom the eldest
is a youth of 00) ; and finally dropped oui
of the race when Learing his 100th year.
If it hadn’t been for the thrice accursed
climate he might have got into the three
figures.
“Boro-Belle” : Bowen, with its natural har-
bor, is at last coming in for its own. The
first season’s output from the big Inkerman
sugar mill, at Ayr, estimated at 10,000 tons,
is to be sent by rail to Bowen for shipment
south. In addition, the Proserpine sugar-
some 7000 tons —generally lightered to Mac-
kay, will also be railed to Bowen for shipping
south. A huge sugar shed, capable of accom-
modating a week’s output from both fac-
tories, has been erected at Bowen. The
decision to ship the Inkerman sugar through
Bowen is largely due to the Government
having recently reduced the harbor dues there
from 2s. to Is. a ton, whereas the Townsville
dues are 2s. a ton. Incidentally, Bowen is
beginning to wake up. The town council
recently inspected the council’s works, and
the prospective improvements tabled include
a complete water service for the town, elec-
tric lighting, a band rotunda and motor ser-
vices to King’s and Queen’s beaches. A
promising adjacent coal seam further
adds to the prospects of the town that was
pushed aside years ago through the over-
powering influence of Townsville.
“Tooba” : Railways Minster Paget turned
the. first sod of the Drayton deviation the
other day, and Toowoomba rejoiced hugely
thereat. The line has quite an historical
interest. It was first agitated for 50 years
ago, and was the main factor in ejecting the
Philp party in 1903. John Leahy, then
Minister for Railways, turned the Drayton
deviation down, and Jim Tolmie, at the
behest of his enraged constituents, deserted
Philp, and, with several other malcontents,
crossed the floor and sat close up to Labor.
Philp, although not in a minority, resigned
a few days later—a step he afterwards
bitterly regretted. This affair was notable
as being the one solitary lapse into Rad-
icalism of..Tolmie’s political career. To do
him justice, he couldn’t helo it.
“Rangan” : Small country councils are very
seldom the theoretically all-for-the-good-of-
the-district bodies that they are supposed to
be, but the North Rockhampton Council is
an outsize in the line. For about six months
the only motion that had the least sign of
unanimity, and' that went" through without
any nasty remarks about - the . mental capa-
city of the members in general, was a want
HYDROPHOBIA.
SHORT-SIGHTED ORATOR : “Ah, my friends, I am glad you do not fear the rain. The
rain cannot hurt us till the fiend takes it and turns it into beer.”
SMALL SAY : “Stow it, Mister. They've all run to the pub fer shelter."
of confidence motion in the body itself, and
it accordingly asked the Home Secretary to
give it the sack. The Home Secretary
obliged, and the new council turned out to
be as bad as the other. The trouble, and it
is a common trouble, seemed to be that there
was only one mayoral chair and everybody
wanted it. However, when the new chair-
man took to court and had fined ten bob one
of the members for putting his feet on the
table or something, things quietened a little.
They broke out afresh a few weeks later,
when that member had his revenge by sitting
outside the door and poking faces at those
inside, who asked him to come in and make
a quorum. Now the ratepayers are en-
couraged to attend the goings-on by such
press notices as :
Ratepayers North Rockhampton. Roll up at the
Bear Garden, Tuesday night, Council Chambers, 8
p.m. Free.
The parable of the prophet “Errant” —-
N$W the people who dwell in the South said unto
their King: “Lord, there is a land in the North
that the worshippers of Buddha and Shinto envy.
Send thither a worthy man to get things ready for
our sons,, that they may people the empty spaces.”
And the King saw that it was good, and he said:
“Whom will we send?”
And one came saying. “Lord, I’ll go and prepare
a place for thy people’s sons.”
Then the King asked of him: “Canst thou govern a
country ?”
And he replied: “Verily, for I am a horse doctor.”
Then the King embraced him, saying: “You’re the
one; gather together men of the tribe of Offieialites
and their wives and take them with you.”
So he went and did even as the. King had told him.
When they came to the land in the North they
said one to another: “We’re on a good wicket.”
And the crock-mender, who was called Excellency,
said: “Gather men and build houses that we may
rest.”
And he gathered together the sons and daughters
of Binghi and of his wife Mary to be his men-servants
and his maid-servants; and the sons of Wun Lung
to prepare his venison.
And one came to him and said: “Master, give
me wdrk together with my wife in your household.”
But he mocked him, saying: “No hope,” arid he
sent him navVyiug on the road. And the Offieialites
did even as their master.
Then another came who said: “Is it lawful to em-
ploy the worshippers of Buddha ?” And they answered
him, “Yea.” “But,” he said, “doth not the com-
mandments say: ‘The white man shall inhabit- the
land’; also, ‘Blessed are the unionists’”? And they
stoned him, crying- “Socialist! Agitator!”
So he fled the land and came again to the South
and stirred the people up, saying: “The prad-fixer
and the Officialites whom we sent North do nothing.
Also they prefer Chow-mauled tucker.”
When the people heard this they were wrath,
gathering together in, the market places, crying.
“Sack the gee-gee mender! Give the vet the shoot!
Toss out the Officialites!”
But the King slumbered and heard them not.
When the Officialites and Shinto worshippers
learned this they embraced each other, saying:
“Banzai,” and they made merry.
And the Shinto men sent a messenger to their own
land, saying: “The time is nearly ripe.”
The Boys Along th’ Crick.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If l could only get back half the money I put
into this hole they could take the whole lot to-
morrow. It’ll break the heart of any man —-ask any
of the boys along th’ crick. —Remarks of pear-country
selector on the Wambo (Q.).
All along the wealthy Wambo, ’mid the bull-
oaks and belars
There’s a row of pine-built humpies rotting
roofless to the stars —
Just a chain of lost endeavor through the
land of pear and tick:
They were once the proud possessions of th’
Boys along th’ Crick.
There was Bill, fropi Hunter River, with his
little miner’s hoard ;
There was Salvo Tom, from Newtown, gladly
praising of the Lord ;
There was big Mick Burn, from Ascot, with
a wad of bank notes thick,
And a mob of knowing townies with the Boys
along th’ Crick.
There was joy on the selections when the
crowd first landed there;
When they gathered round of evenings they
would joke about the pear,
And each pigeon-chested owner would ex-
plain the easy trick
Of exterminating “prickly” to the Boys along
th’ Crick.
Now the terms were very easy—just a fifth
to clear each year.
“You can cut it soft and easy—you can do it
on your ear,”
So the Crown Lands Ranger told them; “set
to work and do it quick,
And you’ve got a fine selection all for ‘nix’
along th’ Crick !”
How they chopped the curse and skited for a
year or two or more,
Though their souls were full of prickles and
their hands were red and sore!
But they’d scratch their heads and wonder
when another cow fell sick,
And the cheques and sprees got scarcer with
the Boys along th’ Crick.
Oh, they chopped and squirted poison, and
they burnt and grubbed the weeds,
While the ’roos and cows and emus trotted
round and sowed the seeds;
Then they cursed the Pear Commission. “Let
’em come an’ try and lick
The united blanky efforts of us Boys along
th’ Crick !”
Then the cheerful Crown Lands Ranger
would call round and make remarks
Re that “fifth” they’d lightly scoffed at, and
they- listened to his barks,
Until Salvo Tom raised Sheol with Big Bill
and Tom and Mick,
And a mighty scared Crown Ranger galloped
madly down th’ Crick.
So by ones and twos they vanished, and the
pear grew over all
Till each lonely, rotting shanty had its prickly
strangling wall.
Now “a hundred fine selections” wait to work
the same old trick
On the next poor lot of chummies like the
Boys along th’ Crick.
QUEENSLAND.
JACKS.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane. April 2a. 1014.
My Dear Thargomindait,—
Brisbane is minus its most abundant
speech-producer, the Governor having, for
the greater part of the week, been sojourn-
ing iu the country up Toowoomba way.
While there he escorted Lady Mat
to the Hospital Ball. They were received
by the only unmarried mayoress I am
acquainted with, Miss Eugenie Boland. She
is not the really truly mayoress, but the
acting municipal hostess, and she is a bril-
liant success.
On Tuesday the St. George Society enter-
tained the Governor, and on Saturday he
accepted an invitation to be present at a
garden party given by Dr. and Mrs. Ilamlyn
Harris at Toowong. Dr. Harris is our
eminent local insect-examiner, and spends
most of his time in the Museum. Many an
insect would still be alive, and married, and
the father of a large family save for his in-
terference. .
Marriage is in the air here. Madge Wool-
cock, a daughter of lawyer W'oolcock, has
left the halls of her fathers (it’s time that
phrase had another airing) to manage the
establishment of Mr. Dinham-Peren. A
fruit farm and a hyphen were his most cher-
ished possessions until he made the last
addition to his worldly goods. The bride
has lots of friends, and St. Barnabas’ bulged
with inside pressure.
Elsie Maud, a daughter of ex-Premier
Philp, also joined the ranks of the “Mrs.”
clan, and became Henderson at the begin-
ning of the week. She is the second Philp
girl to marry a Henderson.
On Monday night a Bal Masque is to be
held in the South Brisbane Technical Col-
lege, but the Brisbane “season” is dull
compared with what it used to be. No one
seems to want to organise balls now, and
the rise in catering and all other expenses
has settled most of the sporting dances that
used to help to make life tolerable. The
tango had a flutter here, but died young. I
don’t think the Grundy woman approved of
it. !
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIAN JANE.
“Y.” : A fine growth Pugh’s Queensland
Almanack has made since it first appeared
as a trembling little sheet over half a cen-
tury: ago. It was then called the iUQRETON
Bay. Almanack, but grew rapidly to be a
hook, and is now a volume of all sorts of in-
formative matter. Pusrh brought the idea
with him from England. lie had been a
newspaper man there, and was acquainted
with the provincial sheets which collected
local facts. There was no money in-such a
venture when he unpacked it on Queensland,
so the young immigrant worked at any jour-
nalism offering, and out of the proceeds fed
his nursling. He sent news of Queens-
land happenings down to Parkes’s Empire
(SydneyL and contributed to the pages oi
Melbourne journals. lie was rather an un-
stable politician, but he succeeded in getting
into the Assembly for a part of Brisbane.
He joined party papers readily, and trans-
ferred himself to the opposite party papers
with equal readiness. The really strenuous
part of his mind went to the Almanack,
and the more time he gave it the louder it
squalled for increased attention. _At last
he dropped both journalism and politics ami
applied for something in the Government ser-
vice, where the salary would help to feed
the youngster. The job of police magistrate
was at that time about the easiest going, and
Theonhilis Pugh sat on the bench a few
hours a dav at Bundaberg or Rockhampton,
and rushed away—as soon as he had fined the
drunks and committed the horse-burglars for
trial—to his little library, where he elimin-
ated and added much to his bantling. The
work, now a limited company affair, repre-
sents Pugh’s life industry, for his other toil,
even the hurried trial and hanging-without-
the-option of the horse-incendiary, was a per-
functory sort of business.

The scarcity of bulldog breed among the
most gilded of the Great American people
these times can he perceived from the births,
engagements and marriages advts in any
issue of New York Herald. In the issue
of March 8 there are only nine births re-
corded, and the parents are Blum, Feldman.
Meyler, Selkowitz, Zander, etc. Even Mrs.
Lick, whose husband might be of English
descent, is described as “nee Hermina
Parian.” The long list of engagements
ranges from Bachrach-Singer to Werrnes-
Zsupnik, and the rest are Jewish, with
hardly an exception. The few marriages
begin with Amel-Hirsch and end with M eil-
Hess. But the Anglo-Saxon race scores a
few points in the Death notices, and comes
out quite strong among the In Memoriams.
The Jew names are in Sassiety and th--
British names in the cemetery.

HAVE YOU GOT
CATARRH ?
if you have a dropping: of phlegm into the throat,
If your nose is stopped up,
if crusts form in your nose,
If you are easily susceptible to colds,
If you suffer from frontal headaches,
If you have noises in the head,
If your hearing is affected,
If you have to clear your throat & expectorate often,
If a change in the weather affects you,
If you are a victim to shortness of breath,
If you sneeze frequently,
If youir sense of smell is affected,
If you have a heavy, tired feeling on awaking:,
If you suffer from a husky throat,
If you are affected with any of the above symptoms,
the chances are that you are suffering from
Catarrh. It is an exceedingly dangerous com-
plaint, and is so insidious that the sufferer hardly
realises how serious is his or her condition until
matters have reached an almost chronic stage.
Catarrh often begins with a cold in the head, hut it never stops there.
The simple cold becomes a protracted one, while the discharge from the
nOB6 grows more profuse and uncomfortable.
The inflammation extends to the throat and bronchial tubes, causing
hoarseness, a tickling sensation, and an aggra\ating cough. The matter
that is co itinually dropping hack into the throat finds its way into the
stomach, resulting in a distressing form of dyspepsia, nausea, and of
appetite and strength.
The catarrhal poisons are absorbed into the blood, and all the membranes
of the body become infected, and what was supposed to be a purely local
disease has become constitutional, deep-seated, and chronic, and may
eventually result in Deafness, Bronchitis, Asthma, Neuritis, Catarrh of
the Stomach, or that dread scourge of the human race, Consumption.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MSS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Old Goulburnite: The story that the
break of railway gauge arose through the desire of
Victorians and South Australians to go one better
than N. S. Wales is ignorance, or premeditated lying.
The three States started with an agreement to adopt
the 4ft. SJin. gauge. N. S. Wales handed over its
railway job to Private Enterprise, and Private Enter-
prise presently hired an Irish engineer who clamored
for the Irish 5ft. 3in. gauge. N. S. AVales intimated
to tlie other States that it was going to be a liar or
a fraud or something, and was going to break the
agreement and make the gauge 5ft. 3in. The other
two States reluctantly fell into line. Later on N. S.
Wales (or Private Enterprise in N. S. Wales) went
back to the 4ft. 84in. measurement, and.that was the
beginning of the break-of-gauge trouble. Seeing that
Private Enterprise presently went broke and left its
unfinished lines on the State’s doorstep, it was a pity
that the shoddy creature was allowed to make so
much annoyance. ... Boorara: “H. Singemoinc’s” week
with Jimmy Tyson occurred on shipboard between
Westralia and Melbourne. He doesn’t know whether or
no James ever visited England. It is possible that
he bought his clothes at a large Sydney emporium;
all lie is certain about is that they were bad and old
and insanitary-looking whenever they (the clothes)
crossed his (“Singemoine’s”) path... .Clarence B.:
Jacomb’s vogue is over. As a subject of local interest
he lias done his dash....The Satyr: Hardly any use
sending in gags that appeared in The Bulletin in the
days when the world was young.... Norfolk Island:
Not worth print.... S.: If you type your copy so
closely, so help us . But let us be calm. . . .

Opinion.” The deuce you did! It’s just as well you
mentioned it, Irma, otherwise we might have printed

verses of London Opinion or any other paper are
regarded here as private property, just as meat and
drapery and fancy goods are. You wouldn’t lift a
sheep off a butcher’s hooks or pirate a roll of cloth.
Why, then, does your conscience leave off when it
comes to lifting literary merchandise? A certain
amount of friendly borrowing (with acknowledg-
ment) is permitted or tolerated ; but if we once
started buving each other’s back numbers at so much
per inch from gentle third parties like yourself, some-
thing would have to burst. It would be very gay
for the third party while it lasted, but something
would have to burst eventually. Most probably it
would be the third party—-or, perhans, third pirate
would be a better term.... Homo: We’ll do without

“H.O.”: Are prominent Americans given
half a dozen low-comedy names at birth, or
do they adopt the dreadful appellations when
on in years? The solution of this mystery
has an important bearing on the increase,
or otherwise, of imbecility in the U.S. All
Yankee poems are by persons with names
such as Richard Edward Kendrick Le Tripe-
hound, W. Henry Hoffman Van-Bosh, and so
on. A Really High-Class American maga-
zine is full of inspiring contributions like
the following:—
THE TRAGIC CHILD.
By Mary Martha Wheeler-Wagstaff.
She was a babe,
Come fresh from God,
But, oh ! her eyes were sad !
Just that and nothing more. Its justifica-
tion is the fact that it is by Mary Martha
etc. And then there are the Christian names
of the incredibly haughty and aristocratic
members in the N’York Four Hundred. I
read, a while back, a chapter of what was
claimed by the magazine that published it to
be “the greatest novel of social life in
America ever written.” The heroine’s name
was Persis. Another female character was
called Gail. Brise, Flaught, Pantar and
Ilisposack were some of the girls’ names in
another publication of the same sort. Senna,
Coma,tose and Mobiloil were missing. An
oversight doubtless. The psychological ex-
planation of America’s demented nomen-
clature is, I fancy, the Great Republic’s un-
satisfied yearning for persons of titular dis-
tinction. The average Yankee —be he a
Wall-street magnate or a mere magazine-
devouring dry-goods store clerk —is vastly
inspired by persons with handles to their
names. He is more inspired than the Eng-
lishman,. who constantly sees hundreds of
such people, and far more so than the
Frenchman, who sees thousands weekly, if
not daily. As. his Constitution doesn’t allow
for such social luxuries as Lady Anna
Gould, the Earl of Chicago, Marquess Rocke-
feller and the like, we get Bloore van Wil-
loughby-Floot, Bricke Hyde-Swan-Schuyter
and similar monstrosities. They fulfil more
or less, what, after all, is the main object of
a title; that is to say, they suggest wealth
and importance. Few obscure and indigent
persons would dare answer to such jaw-
dislocating labels.

Legal Notices.
PURSUANT to an Order of the
Chancery Division of the High
Court of Justice made in the matter of the
estate of Robert Townsend Hippisley de-
ceased Hippisley v. Hippisley 1912 H. 1515
and dated the 12th October 1912 the follow-
ing inquiry is directed, viz.: An inquiry
whether CHARLES ERNEST HIPPISLEY
named in the will of Robert Townsend Hip-
pisley is living or dead and if dead when he
died and whether he left any and what will
and whether he was ever married and if so
when and to whom and whether there were
any children of the said marriage and who
are his legal personal representatives.
Notice is hereby given that the said
Charles Ernest Hippisley and all persons
claiming through or by descent from him
are personally or by their solicitors on or
before the 4th day of July 1914 to come in
and prove their claims at the Chambers of
Mr. Justice Joyce and Mr. Justice Eve,
Room 692 Royal Courts of Justice, Strand,
London. England, or in default thereof they
will be peremptorily excluded from the bene-
fit of the said order. Wednesday the 15th
day of July 1914 at half-past two in the
afternoon at the said Chambers is appointed
for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims. Dated the 15th day of December
1913. —A KEEN, Master of the Supreme
Court.
ROBBINS, OLIVEY & LAKE,
218 Strand, London, W.C.,
For ABBOT. POPE & ABBOT,
Bristol,
Solicitors for the Plaintiffs.
Note.—The above-named Charles Ernest
Hippisley who was a son of the testator
Robert Townsend Hippisley a solicitor of
Bristol England is now if alive about 51
years of age. He went to Australia in Feb-
ruary 1889 and was there employed by
Messrs. Robert Reid & Co., and afterwards
till May 1897 by Messrs. Sands & McDougall
(Limited). He resided at Rochester Lodge,
Collins-place, Melbourne. He left Mel-
bourne in May, 1897. by the Orient Steam-
ship Orizaba for London, where he stayed
one or two months, and is believed to have
resided at 123 Fulham-road, London. In
October. 1897, he left London, and until
April, 1898, was employed by Messrs. J.
Garlick & Co., of Alderley-street, Cape
Town, South Africa, and resided at 43 St.
•Tohn-street. Cape Town. He returned to
Melbourne in 1898, and expressed his inten-
tion of going to Queensland or Sydney. He
is alleged to have been seen in London by an
old schoolfellow in 1908.
Should the above advertisement, which
has reference to benefits accruing to him
under his father’s will, meet the eye of the
said Charles Ernest Hippisley, or in the
event of his death that of his widow or
children, lie is (or they are) earnestly re-
quested to communicate with the least pos-
sible delay with his brother Alfred Edward
Hippisley. of 8 Herbert-crescent. London,
S.W.. or with the said Messrs. Abbot, Pope
& Abbot, solicitors. Bristol.

The Inky Way.
Adelaide Mail, a 24-page Sunday paper, gave over
to “Society Women” the producing of its issue of
11/4/’l4. ! Ethelwin Robin acted as editor; Muriel
Farr. wrote on the Cost of Living; Mrs.
Box ‘ was on the art of home-making and did
the gardening column; Mrs. R. Anderson held
forth on University life, and supervised the
religious column; Mrs. Crawford Vaughan (who
is Evelyn Goode, the novelist) ; Mrs. E. W.
Hanker and Adela Pankhurst appeared on politics;
Mrs. O’Leary was the sporting authority; and there
were various other features, including an article on
Frenchwomen by one of themselves, and a native
legend by Mrs. P. R. Stow—better known by her
former name of Mrs. Langloh Parker. There are
various opinions about the success of the experiment.
Some of them are different, and some are more
different than others.
* * * *
The London Times brought to light last week some
unpublished verses by Keats, whereof this is one:—
You say you love, but when your lips,
Coral tinted, teach no blisses
More than coral in the sea,
They never pout for kisses,
Oh, love me truly.
Keats was said to have died of a caustic criticism in
a review. If he had sent that stanza to The
Bulletin on spec, he would have died of “Answers
to Correspondents.”
* * * *
“Lud”: What a fall was there, my countrymen!
The London Times down to a penny. Down to tup-
pence only last May. Must be somewhat of hard
Times. Of course the Times ought to have been a
penny in the generation before last. Borthwick,
with the Morning Post, showed the road, while the
nobs and snobs declared that he rushed to ruin.
Aristocracy is essentially the most penurious class.
Queen Mary’s mother used to save bits of string and
scraps of paper. Borthwick acted boldly on the
principle that blue blood thinks more of a copper
than a coster does. The Times was a threepenny
thunderer for 52 years. “We’ll never come down.
We are not as other men are, even this publican. I
am holier than thou.” People grabbed the sacred
Times at a halfpenny an hour, and one copy saw
many thumbs. You had to read it, or you were lost.
It is 129 years old. The way it used to whack into
other papers, and they into it and each other,. in
the ’3o’s was astonishing. The Times called the
Chronicle “A squirt of filthy water.” The
Chronicle termed the Post “A slop-pail of corrup-
tion.” The Standard called the Globe “Our blubber-
headed contemporary.” The Herald said the
Courier was “a spavined old hack.” The Times,
in an article headed “The Liberal Liars,” wrote of
the Chronicle : “The community must be shocked to
know that there are such beings as these scribblers
out of the treadmill. Every exposure of the raga-
muffins is proof that there have crept into the Press
a number of scoundrels who would be a disgrace to
the vilest coteries of Europe.”
A PARNASININE RHYMI
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Pegusass, my Pegusass,
That standest meekly by,
And chewest thistle heads for grass
With red and rolling eye,
1 needs must mount on thee and ride
Aeross the dusty plain,
For Verse ittust slide from thy duii hide
fete \Ve dome home agaiH.
Ih the jiggetty-jog Of the weil-Worn ti'ack
\Ve bump and bump along,
For you are a Very respectable habit
For ambling a roadster song. _ ,
i3lp\V high, blow lo\V all winds that g'd 1
In fair of stobipy weather
My feegusass and t must pass
i'he roid of rhyme tbgethbf.
ijphiil \Ve \Valk the bid heroic beat,
ten see-saW syllables gnd then a pause.
Th'e general effect. is father neat, (
And, if you needs must look to find the cause,
the measufe fits the shape of British jaws.
Spenser and Shakspeafe used it in their time,
iliitbn pursued in blank and Pope in weary rhyme.
the Alexandrine tops the hill,
And jogging down we go,
The gait that Wordsworth used to fill
Ten thousand lines or so.
Or if my neddy’s shoe is loose
We make a gayer jingle,
And double rhymings introduce
To alternate with single.
Along the fiat meadows the metre is kind,
In a low, easy canter we glide like the wind.
(Please notice the ending. We seldom have sinned
By calling the swift moving atmosphere wind.
We prefer the long syllable still to the short,
And the modern form causes my neddy to snort,
Although if it suited we mightn’t be loth
To do as John Davidson did and use both.)
Pegusass, Pegusass,
Let such small matters pass.
Who looks for clean rhymes
In these swift-riding times?
By the pebbles you scatter
It’s really no matter
To beast or to man
If the lines rhyme or scan,
As long as you never
Quite fail to look clever,
With dark sayings, peculiar, exultant, obscure,
Of whose meaning'your readers can never be sure.
God prosper long our noble king,
For whatsoe’er befall,
The ancient ballad has a swing,
And so God save us all;
But the newer ballad also has a long and fatal flow,
And to get a rhyme or ending you’ve a weary way
to go;
Yes, beyond the Darling Biver there are horses,
swags and shears,
And the Lord forgive my donkey with the sunset on
his ears.
All waits for the right voices! \
Good enough! Go crop unequal grass on Whitman’s
lawn.
Henley comes out of hospital and feels too tired to
look for rhymes.
Don’t bother after rhythms. ’Tis enough.
Just say things.
The tired people are mostly eating dinner.
Woah, you brute!
•%. canT jog to this acrobatic amble.
Gee up, and keep your hooves out of my eyes.
My Pegusass, my Pegusass,
Jog on to Hippocrene ■>
(I mispronounce the word, alas, ’
Like Keats, to rhyme with green).
The fountain may not be so clear
As when in Greece’s elder year
The Muses saw it bubble.
Jog on, my steed, there lives in beer
Some inspiration wanted.Jiere,
Our licensed Hippocrene is near,
Which saves a lot of trouble.
N.S.W.
D. M. W.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE sung a song; an’ I sat silent there
Wif bofe ’an’s grippin’ tight on to
me chair;
Me ’eart, that yesterd’y I thort wus broke
Wif ’umpin’ sicli a ’eavy load o’ care,
Come swellin’ in me throat like I would
choke.
1 feels ’ot blushes climbin’ to me ’air.
’Twas like that feelin’ when the spring wind
breaves
Sad music in the sof’ly rustlin’ leaves ;
An’ when a bloke sits down an’ starts to
chew
Crook thorts, wifout quite knowin’ why ’e
grieves
Fer things ’e’s done ’e didn't ort to do,
Fair winded wif the ’eavy sighs ’e ’eaves.
She sung a song; an’ all at once I seen
The kind o’ crool an’ ’eartless brute I been.
In ev’ry word I read it like a book —
The slanter game I’d played wif my Doreen.
I ’card it m ’er song; an’ in ’er look
I sor wot made me feel fair rotten mean.
Poor 'urt Doreen! My tender bit o’ dull'!
Ar, men don’t understand : they’re fur too
rough ;
Their ways is 'ard an’ coarse wif lovin’
tarts;
They never slings ’em symperthy enough;
But treats ’em ’arsh, an’ tramples on their
’carts,
Bocos their own crool ’earts is leather-tough.
She sung a song; an’ all them bitter things
That chewin’ over barneys ever brings
(live place to thorts of sorrer an’ remorse;
Tike when some ’eart-broke punter goes an’
slings
Is larst lone deener on a stiffened ’orse,
An learns to know that vain regret wot
stings.

lo drown them memories o’ fancied wrong.

Tut, when I met ’er eye—O strutli! ’Twas
strong,

C. J. DENNIS.
Stowaways.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT’S the easiest thing in the world to
stow away, these days,” said the
purser, as we sat In the ship’s bar,
gi\ ing occasional orders to the bar-rooui
steward. There were the chief steward, the
second engineer, the assistant purser, and a
little clergyman, one of the passengers, who
was sipping lemonade and winking devilishly
when a point was made in the conversation.
•‘lt/s dead easy,” repeated the purser.
“Earner, in fact, than would be the job of a
Millions Club in Hell. I know if ever 1 want
iu go anywhere as something else than an
officer of the ship, it’s me for the Stowaway
Stakes. I only call it ‘stowaway,’ mind,
because the word doesn’t signify what it did
in the old days. But my deadhead sea going
won t be done in the hold, or in the steerage,
like some of those poor devils. I’m going-
saloon.
It’s not so difficult, you know. Just
pick your ship, finding out one which is to
carry a good number of saloon passengers. Co
on board, lock yourself in the bathroom, and
have a bath. Not long after she starts, the
purser will come along and call out, ‘Ticket
in there, please!’ ‘Right,’ you answer, ‘l’ll
give it to you when I corn? out. It’s in my
cabin.’ The purser will be pretty sure to
then pass on, having too many other tickets
to collect to wait for your emergence from
the bath. He may come back, but —ill, of
course, find that you’re gone. You will see
to that. You just walk down as cool as you
like when meal-time comes, and sit among
the mob, sailing into your eight-course joy-
ousness with untrembling conscience and
appetite. Having no legitimate berth, you
would have to bring a few rugs with you
and sleep up on deck, or in the smoke- or
bar-rooms, which is permissible.
“I don’t say you could work this scheme
from Sydney to London, you know, but you
could do it to Maoriland, or Melbourne, or
Brisbane, or even as far north up the coast
as Townsville—perhaos even farther.
Neither the purser nor the chief steward will
notice you during the voyage, because their
time will be fully occupied in attending to
the other passengers—especially the frilled
ones. A man doesn’t want to give in his
name for games of deck cricket you know;
and however musical he mav be. it would be
unwise for him to have his monniker put
down on the programme for a music-room
concert, because either the steward or purser
would notice it. probably, as not being in
(he berthing-list. The free traveller can
well afford to forego little extras of the
kind. His business is to get from one
port to another for nothing, and not to
parade the fact that he has a soul for music.
If he is content to leave well alone, then he
is fairly safe. In the second-class it’s
easier still. You don’t meet any of the
officers there. Officers on steamships con-
sider themselves a cut above the second-
class.
‘‘l say, the days of hiding down amongst
the cargo are mostly over. That game is too
uncomfortable for your modern, luxury-lov-
ing ‘stowaway.’ Anyhow, what can hanpen
to him, even if he is discovered? He can be
only lined or gaoled, at the very worst. It’s
a confounded nuisance for the purser, and.
maybe, a couple of the other officers to have
to go and waste perhaps half a day giving
evidence against a stowaway! at the court:
so that, to save trouble, the impostor, when
discovered, is often given a job in the
galley or stokehold, to enable him to work
out his salvation to the port he has consigned
himself to. And so it often happens that when,
the trip is over, the ship owes the original
stowaway two or three pounds in wages.'
It is usual for stowaways, when working-
in such circumstances, to complain bitterly
about the food they receive. Stealing a
passage, or attempting it. even though it be
a valuable one, seems to be an offence of no
importance beside that of stealing a watch
and chain.
“Some stowaways try to beat you with
the not-enough-money or the dying mother
racket. I had an experience witli one of
the former the other day. We had just left,
and I was "oing round as usual getting the
tickets when this fellow came up to me.
“ ‘Purser?’ he says. I nodded.
“ ‘You look a pretty good sort of a bloke,’
he remarks.
“‘Do I?’ says I.
“ ‘l’ve on’y got ten bob,’ pleads he, ‘and 1
wanter go ter Brisbin.’
“ ‘Then you must dig up the extra money
somewli! re.’ I told him, ‘or you’re due for
the magistrate,’
“ ‘Tell you what,’ he proposes. ‘Give me
a few hours, and I’ll take me ten bob down
the steeridge and start a game o’ poker. I’m
something of a prince at ther game, and I
might ’ave luck,’ He winked.
“I told him to please himself about that,
but that he would have to hurry up. He
caAne back in an hour and a half, with a
big grin and £l5 in notes.
“ ‘l’ve won it.’ he confided. ‘I can buy
half a dozen steeridge passidges now if I
want ’em.’
“The liar! I know that sort of cove!
He’d had the money all the time ! It takes
a smart one to beat me nowadays, I tell
you. But some of them are very cunning.
Sometimes, when they want to stretch out
their nassages (having’ intended to do so all
along) they tell you they’ve been over-
carried. That’s a great wheeze of the
Hindus —the men who win all the land
ballots round the N.S.W. North Coast dis-
tricts. They plead no-savee, and of course
it’s hard to prove that they are lying.
“The dying-mother game, as I have told
you, is a favorite ruse. But you get genuine
cases now and again. There was a lad of
about 20 who told me his mother was dying
of consumption in Adelaide, and had had a
wire sent to him in Sydney, asking for him
to be on hand before the end. if possible.
The yciine fellow was £1 short of the single

ARE YOUR KIDNEYS WEAK?
Weak Kidneys Cause Backache, Gravel, Urinary
Disorders, Dropsy, Lumbago and Rheumatism.
Every moment of your life poisonous waste keeps
getting into the blood from your food and from the
wear and tear of the body. But the kidneys filter it
out and keep the blood pure.
But when the kidneys are weak they cannot do
their work thoroughly. Then some of the poisonous
waste gets left in the blood, and you begin to feel
dull, heavy and drowsy; your back is bad, your head
aches; dropsical swellings appear in the ankles or
under the eyes, and the heart becomes affected. You
may get rheumatic twinges, lumbago attacks or dizzy
spells. The bladder acts too often, or there is pain
in seeking relief. There may be sediment, gravel or
cloudiness of the water.
When the kidneys are ill they need a kidney medi-
cine—ordinary medicines won’t do —they cannot
cleanse and relieve the kidneys. Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills act quickly and directly upon the
kidneys and bladder—help the kidneys to fdter the
mischievous kidney poisons out of the blood and
dispose of them through the urinary system. Doan’s
Pills give the best results of any kidney medicine
known.

SYDNEY TESTIMONY.
FROM ST. LEONARDS.
Mrs. F. V. Richards, “Earlswood,” Rawson Street, St.
Leonards, Sydney, says: “For about 12 months I was a
martyr to kidney complaint. At times I used to cry out
with the terrible pains in my back. I could not do my house-
work. Many a time I have had to get a neighbor to come
and rub my back with liniment. I was treated by a doctor
for a considerable time, but he did me no good. I heard
that Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills were curing similar
cases, and decided to try them. They gave me relief at
once, and a course of the pills perfectly cured me. It is
four years since I was cured, and I have never had any
return of the complaint. I can do my housework now
without constant torture. I cannot speak too highly of
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.”
Three years later, Mrs. Richards says: “It is now seven
years since Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills cured me of
backache and kidney trouble, and I have had no return of
these ailments during all that time.”

steerage fare, so I told him, having sure
information that he was bona fide, to see if
he. could borrow the deficit. He tried to do

“Women, as usual, get better treatment
than they perhaps merit. A collection is
invariably taken up to make good the pas-
sage-money of a she-stowaway. The ticket
subscribed for is mostly saloon, and if the
collection (which is always made very
quietly and secretly, so as not to hurt the
lady stowaway’s feelings) doesn’t bring the
required amount, the saloqn passengers, as a
rule, get up a concert, and make up the
fare with the box-takings.
“Crooks wanted by the police travel pretty
extensively around the Australian coast,
considering their small proportion to the
general community. When they’re badly
wanted their usual scheme is to go in a
well-ventilated case as cargo. And now and
again they actually make voyages as corpses
—yes, corpses. I remember one little mat-
ter of the kind, years ago. Now, it’s a
wrong idea to suppose that corpses are
always put into the ship’s refrigerator to
keep. Sometimes they are put down in the
hold with the general cargo, but ordinarily
are carried on deck when the distance is
short enough for them not to become objec-
tionable. When they’re carried on deck it
is in a plain, common-looking box not a bit
like a coffin, and quite devoid of plated fit-
tings. Carried at shipper’s risk? Certainly.
Well, in this instance the wanted person,
who was going as a stiff, was taken right to
the port to \yhich he had been consigned.
The hatchman, during a lull in the discharge
of (he general cargo, caused by a break-
down of the winch, thought it would be a
goof! chance to get (he supposedly ‘departed’
pne s box off the ship. His temper was more
than usually sour, owing to the accident to
(he_ \vinch. Unconscious of the nature of
their seats, a couple of stout old ladies were
squatting on the case. Acidly (he hatchman
addressed them: ‘Would you mind getting
up off that corpse, please?'
“The portly females uttered terrible
shrieks, and fled. The row they made gave
the -corpse such a shock that he bumped
his head audibly against the side of the
box. The ha(chman’s suspicions were
aroused, the case opened, and the crook con-
founded and arrested. You know, it’s very
hard to give a suitable excuse for not being
dead.
“It reminds me of the prettiest stowaway
I ever had dealings with. It was in 1898,
when I was on a line running to Maoriland.
She got on at Sydney. She was a lovely
girl of 19. fair hair and blue eyes, and she
confessed in tears, when I was coming round
for the tickets off Rose Bay, that she had
no money, and absolutely had to get to
Maoriland. I could do nothing, but I ex-
plained her case to a cprtain big bookmaker
aboard, whom I knew, and who was on his
way to Maoriland for a holiday. He bought
her a first-saloon passage right off. And yet
they want to bring in the totalisator! She
thanked both of us, and couldn’t be grateful
enough. What a bright little giri, too. She
said that friends would meet her on the
wharf at Auckland. Op the way over, with
her joyous conversation and her charming
little ways, she was the light of the saloon
table. She used to sit next to me, you know.
Then she quietly threw herself overboard
one night. Cancer of the breast was her
trouble. I was really fond of that girl.”
The purser was staring straight ahead
with eyes which did not see the “Time-table
of Steamers” on the wall opposite, and we
silently went to our bunks.
C. R. L. HALLORAN.
John Isaac Ephraim Bull.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AMONG the chiefest of those stupidly
self-satisfied organisations which
exist to prove that the Deity is an
Englishman, and that any battle which
would crushingly defeat England must be
Armageddon and presage the rapid ending
of the world, is that Exeter Hall body
which calls itself variously “British Israel”
or “The Society for the Establishment of
the Identity of the British and American
with the Lost Ten Tribes of Is-
rael,” or "The British Israel Identification
Association.” I attended a few of their
services at Exeter Hall and found them a
collection of Dreary and Blatant Advocates
of the Unnecessary, and Provers of the
Impossible.
Their literature is tremendous. They
publish a weekly paper,. the Bannek of
Israel, and books by the hundred : “British
Israel” ; “British Israel Tracts” ; “The Wit-
ness of the Nation” ; “The Birthright Bles-
sings” ; “The New Old Story” ; “The Eng-
lish Israelite”; and a hundred others. As
if to prove that the promoters of the new
Yiddishers are Jews themselves, at any
rate, they charge like the Light Brigade
for tjieir wads of madness and run them
into 10 and 12 editions.
1 am ready to believe that the British
Israelite is the worst singer in the world.
I heard all these self-styled Jews howling
at the top of their alleged voices: —
Lift up thine eyes, O Ephraim, then, and sing!
Break forth, 0 barren one! Cry aloud, rejoice!
Gather thy sons from far to meet thy King I
And praise him with one heart, one mind, o,ne
voice.
After this tremendous poem had been bel-
lowed, the leading Israelite uttered these
statements as “facts” with that sublime in-
difference to proof which is characteristic of
all cranks, and especially of the religious
crank of any sort: —
“Subsequent to their —ah —captivity the
—ah—ten tribes were pwophesied to wander
to The ‘lsles of the Sea’—to the—ah—
‘lsles afar off’ —ah —‘To the North and to
the West.’ Like a mighty wivver they have
passed through many changes, but they-—■
ah —began as Israelites and we see their
descendants now as the —ah —British
Nation.”
And then, in a triumphant challenging of
all refutation —
“The Wivver Whine at its source is—ah —-
a vewy differwent looking wattah compared
to when it we&ches its mouth, but twuly it
is the Wjvver Whipe for ajll thgt.
“It was pwophesiqd of Iswael that she
should possess the Gatos of her enemies,
We have nov. sixty-six such gates open—
ah—continually day and night, such as Gib-
waltar, Malta, Pewim, Aden, Socotwa, and
—and—so on.
“Bwitain now uses almost the exact
weights and measures used by the Hebwews.
“Iswael was commanded to lend to many
nations, but to bowwow from none. This is
pwecisely what Gweat Bwitain does, having
lent five hundwed an’ eighty millions—er —
I forget the exact amount.
“Bwitish Iswael is, as a wule, fwaternal
to the abowigines of her colonies, but never-
theless finds them dying out, as foretold by
Jewemiah.
“The Iswaelites were to be blind to their
owigin. So is Gweat Bwitain.
“The Pwomised Land is still unsold. God
has been keeping it for his people for over
3SOO years, and 192 million acres, from the
Wivver of Egypt to the Wivver Euphwates
are Our Inhewitance.
“It is pwobable that the stone in the
cowonation chair at Westminster Abbey is
the God guaided stone of Lethel Jacob S
fetone, or the 1 1 liar of Witness. •
“The name ‘Saxon’ is dewived fwom ‘Saac-
Sunnia,’ sons of Saac, or Isaac.
“The twibe of Dan was the pioneer twibe
in Bwitain, and the twibal name may still be
twaced in such wivvers and places as Dan-
übe, Danan, Dantzig, Danenborg, Denmark,
The Don and the Dannie Alps.
“By the pwophesy Iswael is to be a gweat
nation and a company of nations. The
gweat nation is the Bwitish and Amewican
and the company of (nations is Canada, Aus-
tralia, South Africa and—er—others.
“Then we have a descendant of David
weigning over us, and our national hewaldrv
is the Lion and the Unicorn 99
All this and much more. ‘ Proving every-
thing from nothing going nowhere, boxing
the compass with Jonah s whale; discovering
the seed of David and Abraham in the com-
fortable figureheads of the House of Ilan-
over. The Gates of her Enemies become
Gibwaltar and Pewim and the rest of it,
even the foolishly archaic British method
of weighing and measuring is adduced as a
« f Fl ! iae . orlgl “;« r a t least of Divine
dani, P r °t ec ti° n - When the prophecy is
against them they twist it so that England,
with her tremendous national debt, is made
to appear as owing nothing, or at least as
n *°? iri ? t0 L ~t be Gentiles (which
may be true, too, but in a way the opposite
to that intended).
As to the aborigines dying out, the Aus-
tralian black apparently was not wiped out
with bullets and strychnined flour for any
other purpose but to prove the infallibility
of the “pwophesies of Jewemiah.”
It would be a quite harmless madness if
it didn’t use some good money and if it
didn’t have a political bearing. In repre-
sents the Nonconformist Conscience in its
silliest moments, and its political bearing is
on that impracticable Imperial Federation
which is the refuge of the visionary politician
in his old age. But principally it is to be
objected to by a hard-headed generation,
which wants facts and nought else, as the
work of kindly idiots who would deny the
Equator if it were necessary to prove that
Dan established Denmark, or that Denmark
is so named because of Daniel’s den of
lions, which animals, no doubt, had some
connection with the Lion of Judah; hence
the old Kingdom of Lyonesse. the remains
of which are now the Scilly Isles, and Old
Jewry in London, and trial by jury, and the
Isle of Jura, and the British occupation of
Egypt because Moses was there when the
light went out. (That humble herb called
moss was invented by Moses and named
after him, in consequence of which some
people are called Moss to this day, the name
having grown upon them.) And from all
this we deduce that the family of Simpson
was descended from Simeon, and that the
game of nap had its origin in Naphtali, and
other circumstances too numerous and un-
necessary to call for special mention.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Little Man in Bed.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Great preparations are being made for the visit of
King George to Paris. He will sleep in the bed of
Napoleon the First. — News item.
WHAT are you doing in that bed,
little man?
Fit your head to its pillow, if you
can !
For there has lain the giant brain that
planned tumultuous things ;
That turned the kings to valets and the
valets into kings ;
An Ego vast, and overcast with cloudy
dreams of fate ;
Two hands that wrestled Europe in a grip
of dreadful hate:
He changed the maps of continents as over
them he ran :
What are you doing in his bed, little man?
What are you doing in that bed, little man?
And can you sleep? Well, I suppose you
can!
There’ll come no ghost with haughty boast:
“I made you cringe and crave;
The Austrian was my servant, the Prussian
was my slave ;
I fought and lied to stand astride the mon-
archs crawling probe ;
I kicked aside their dynasties, and made
their pride my own ;
For I was more than fifty kings, if for a
little span:—
What are you doing in my bed, little man?”
What are you doing in that bed, little man?
He who would fill it, let him, if he can!
The Man who strode along the road built
of his own grand schemes
Will laugh to see you gaping in the bed
that knew his dreams ;
The visions bold to seize and hold, to con-
quer and to smite,
Oh, they will not disturb you on the sleepy
banquet night!
Sleep, kinglet, sleep; Napoleon left your
sires as they began;
But France! that France should give you
his bed, little man!
Was there no bed but that bed, little man?
N.S.W.

That to run a Steam Plant Economically
you must have Dry Steam. The only
efficient method to adopt to ensure your
steam being free from water is to lead
the water with the steam to a proper
collector, and discharge it through a
Steam Trap. But you must have a Trap
that is Reliable and does not Leak.

High Pressure STEAM TRAP
